You should reason from first principles about the balance of pressure-curvature drive versus field-line bending, how axisymmetry constrains the tokamak mode structure, and how three-dimensional geometry alters the global coupling of Fourier harmonics and the effect of localized zero-shear pockets in stellarators. Select all correct statements.

A. In axisymmetric tokamaks, high-$n$ ideal ballooning instabilities are regulated by finite magnetic shear $s$ along field lines, and the ensuing pressure-driven threshold exhibits a Troyon-like scaling, effectively tying the allowable $\beta$ to $I_p$ through the competition between pressure-curvature drive and field-line bending; at fixed $q$ profile and shaping, this produces an approximately device-independent normalized threshold, consistent with axisymmetric field-line geometry.

B. In stellarators, global pressure-driven limits are not controlled by $I_p$ but by three-dimensional curvature and magnetic shear distributions; local regions with $s\approx 0$ reduce field-line bending stabilization, enable extended mode structures along complex field lines, and can precipitate global ballooning or interchange-like instabilities that set $\beta$ limits depending on the distribution of bad curvature and three-dimensional coupling, not on a tokamak-like $I_p$ normalization.

C. Because three-dimensional curvature in stellarators introduces alternating favorable curvature, it generally stabilizes ballooning more effectively than axisymmetry, implying an intrinsic increase of the $\beta$ limit over tokamaks that is insensitive to detailed geometry or local shear-free pockets.

D. Local shear-free regions in a stellarator cannot materially affect the pressure-driven limit if the flux-surface-averaged shear is finite, since ballooning stability depends exclusively on global averages; therefore, localized $s\approx 0$ segments do not lower the global threshold.

E. The tokamak Troyon-like limit is independent of the safety factor $q$ and plasma shaping because high-$n$ pressure-driven modes behave as global interchange modes without sensitivity to magnetic shear; therefore, the threshold depends only on $B$ and $R_0$ rather than on $q$ or $s$.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{AB}$$", "solution": "The starting point is ideal Magnetohydrodynamics (MHD), whose force balance is $\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$. Linear stability is assessed by the ideal MHD energy principle, which states that an equilibrium is stable to a perturbation $\\mathbf{\\xi}$ if the second variation $\\delta W$ is positive for all allowable $\\mathbf{\\xi}$. In the high-$n$ ballooning-mode ordering, the mode is localized along a field line and the stability can be studied via a field-line differential equation balancing destabilizing pressure-curvature terms against stabilizing field-line bending terms, with coefficients depending on local magnetic shear and curvature. Although we avoid writing specific shortcut formulas, it is useful to recall that the destabilizing term is proportional to the pressure-gradient projected onto bad curvature, while the stabilizing term scales with the square of the perpendicular wavenumber enhanced by magnetic shear $s$, and the field line parallel connection length. \n\nFor an axisymmetric tokamak, the magnetic field-line geometry is simpler: the bad curvature is concentrated on the outboard side due to toroidal geometry, and the safety factor $q(\\psi)$ and magnetic shear $s(\\psi)$ are well-defined functions that vary smoothly. In high-$n$ ballooning analysis, the stabilizing field-line bending increases with magnetic shear $s$ and with the perpendicular scale of the perturbation. The pressure-curvature drive scales with the local pressure gradient and the unfavorable curvature. The competition between these determines a threshold often summarized empirically by a normalized $\\beta$ that ties $\\beta$ to the net plasma current $I_p$ via the axisymmetric bending stiffness and shear; this is the essence of the Troyon-like scaling. The dependence on $q(\\psi)$ and shaping enters because both curvature and shear coefficients depend on equilibrium geometry; device-independent normalization emerges only when $q$ and shaping are comparable across devices. Thus, in tokamaks, axisymmetry and finite $s$ everywhere yield a high-$n$ ballooning threshold that approximately produces a constant normalized limit at fixed $q$ and shaping, effectively linking allowable $\\beta$ to $I_p$.\n\nFor a stellarator, the magnetic field is three-dimensional, with curvature $\\kappa(\\theta,\\phi)$ alternating in sign along a field line and magnetic shear varying along the line. There is no large net toroidal plasma current $I_p$, so the field-line bending and shear are set by externally generated magnetic geometry. Crucially, the presence of local shear-free pockets, where $s\\approx 0$ along an extended segment, removes a key stabilizing mechanism of ballooning modes: field-line bending enhanced by shear. In such segments, the parallel connection length can be larger and the mode can balloon or interchange more readily, with the destabilizing pressure-curvature drive unopposed by local shear. Three-dimensionality couples multiple Fourier harmonics along the field line and across neighboring lines, producing global modes whose threshold depends on the spatial distribution of bad curvature and the extent of these shear-free regions. Consequently, the global pressure-driven limit in stellarators depends on the geometry and local shear structure, not on an $I_p$-based normalization. Therefore, local $s\\approx 0$ regions can have an outsized impact on the global threshold by enabling maximal drive where stabilization is weakest.\n\nWith these principle-based arguments laid out, we now evaluate the options.\n\nOption A: This states that in axisymmetric tokamaks, high-$n$ ideal ballooning is regulated by finite magnetic shear $s$, producing a pressure-driven threshold exhibiting Troyon-like scaling that effectively ties allowable $\\beta$ to $I_p$ through the competition between pressure-curvature drive and bending. It also states that at fixed $q$ profile and shaping, the normalized threshold is approximately device-independent, consistent with axisymmetry. This is consistent with the energy principle and ballooning physics: axisymmetry constrains the field-line geometry, and $s$ plays a central stabilizing role; the empirical Troyon-like scaling captures the interplay between current-driven shear and pressure drive. The caveat that device independence requires comparable $q$ and shaping is correctly mentioned. Verdict — Correct.\n\nOption B: This states that in stellarators, global pressure-driven limits are not controlled by $I_p$ but by three-dimensional curvature and shear distributions; local regions with $s\\approx 0$ reduce stabilization, enable extended mode structures along complex field lines, and can precipitate ballooning or interchange-like instabilities; thresholds then depend on bad curvature distribution and three-dimensional coupling, not $I_p$ normalization. This follows from the lack of net current, the geometric origin of shear and curvature, and the critical role of shear-free pockets diminishing field-line bending stabilization. It properly emphasizes global three-dimensional coupling and the dependence on geometry rather than $I_p$. Verdict — Correct.\n\nOption C: This asserts that three-dimensional curvature generally stabilizes ballooning more effectively than axisymmetry and thus stellarators intrinsically have higher $\\beta$ limits than tokamaks independent of geometry and local shear-free pockets. This is an overgeneralization and incorrect. While three-dimensional geometry can introduce favorable curvature and other stabilizing features, it also introduces bad curvature and complex coupling that can be destabilizing, especially in shear-free regions; stellarator $\\beta$ limits are highly sensitive to geometry and local shear structure. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nOption D: This claims that local shear-free regions in stellarators cannot affect the pressure-driven limit if the global average shear is finite, because stability depends exclusively on global averages. Ballooning stability, however, is dictated by local field-line properties; instability can localize in regions where $s\\approx 0$, and such regions can dominate the global threshold. Therefore, global averages do not guarantee stability when local pockets exist. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nOption E: This states that the tokamak Troyon-like limit is independent of the safety factor $q$ and shaping, depending only on $B$ and $R_0$ because high-$n$ modes are interchange-like without sensitivity to shear. This misrepresents ballooning physics; $q$ and $s$ directly affect field-line bending and the stability coefficients, and shaping alters curvature and shear. The threshold is not solely a function of $B$ and $R_0$, and high-$n$ ballooning is sensitive to shear. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nTherefore, the correct options are A and B."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{AB}$$", "solution": "The problem statement is first validated to ensure it is scientifically sound, well-posed, and objective.\n\n**Step 1: Extract Givens**\n- **Systems:** An axisymmetric tokamak plasma and a three-dimensional stellarator plasma, both near pressure-driven stability boundaries.\n- **Governing Physics:** Ideal Magnetohydrodynamics (MHD).\n- **Force Balance:** $\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$.\n- **Stability Criterion:** The second variation of potential energy, $\\delta W$, must be non-negative for linear stability ($\\delta W \\ge 0$). Instability occurs for any displacement $\\mathbf{\\xi}$ that makes $\\delta W<0$.\n- **Analysis Method:** Ballooning-mode ordering.\n- **Destabilization Mechanism:** Pressure-curvature drive, associated with the integral of \"bad curvature\" along a magnetic field line.\n- **Stabilization Mechanism:** Field-line bending, associated with magnetic shear.\n- **Tokamak Characteristics:** Axisymmetric, monotonic safety factor $q(\\psi)$, finite local magnetic shear $s(\\psi)$ everywhere, net plasma current $I_p$.\n- **Stellarator Characteristics:** Three-dimensional ($3$D) geometry, no large net toroidal plasma current, localized regions where local shear $s \\approx 0$ over extended angles, and field-line curvature $\\kappa(\\theta,\\phi)$ that alternates between favorable and unfavorable values.\n- **Notation:** $\\beta$ is the ratio of plasma pressure to magnetic pressure, $I_p$ is the tokamak plasma current, $B$ is the characteristic magnetic field, $a$ is the minor radius, and $R_0$ is the major radius.\n- **Question:** Identify the correct statements comparing the pressure-driven limits in tokamaks (Troyon-like) and stellarators, based on the interplay of pressure-curvature drive, field-line bending, axisymmetry, and $3$D geometry (including local shear-free regions).\n\n**Step 2: Validate Using Extracted Givens**\n- **Scientifically Grounded:** The problem is based on the foundational principles of ideal MHD stability theory in magnetic confinement fusion, specifically the concepts of ballooning modes, magnetic shear, and magnetic curvature, which are central to understanding pressure limits in tokamaks and stellarators. It accurately represents the key distinctions between these two confinement schemes.\n- **Well-Posed:** The problem is well-defined. It asks for a qualitative comparison based on established physical principles and provides sufficient information to reason through the differences in stability mechanisms.\n- **Objective:** The language is technical, precise, and free of subjective claims. The described characteristics of tokamaks and stellarators are standard idealizations used in plasma theory.\n\n**Step 3: Verdict and Action**\nThe problem statement is scientifically sound, well-posed, and objective. It contains no contradictions or ambiguities. Therefore, the problem is **valid**. We may proceed to the solution.\n\n**Derivation from First Principles**\n\nThe stability of a plasma against pressure-driven modes is determined by the sign of the change in potential energy, $\\delta W$, under a small perturbation $\\mathbf{\\xi}$. For high toroidal mode number ($n \\gg 1$) perturbations that are localized on a magnetic flux surface and along a field line, $\\delta W$ can be analyzed using the ballooning mode formalism. This leads to a second-order ordinary differential equation for the mode amplitude along a field line coordinate, let's call it $\\chi$. The generic form of the equation encapsulates the competition between stabilizing and destabilizing effects:\n$$\n\\frac{d}{d\\chi} \\left( C_{bend}(\\chi) \\frac{d\\Phi}{d\\chi} \\right) - C_{drive}(\\chi) \\Phi = 0\n$$\nwhere $\\Phi$ is the eigenfunction related to the plasma displacement.\n\nThe two key terms are:\n1.  **Field-Line Bending (Stabilizing):** The term $C_{bend}(\\chi)$ represents the energy required to bend magnetic field lines. In ballooning theory, this term is directly related to the local magnetic shear, $s$. A finite shear $s$ causes the radial wavevector of the mode to grow along the field line, which increases the required bending energy and thus localizes the mode and stabilizes it. Schematically, the stabilizing contribution to $\\delta W$ is $\\delta W_{bend} \\sim \\int s^2 |\\nabla_\\parallel \\xi|^2 dV$, where $\\nabla_\\parallel$ is the gradient along $\\mathbf{B}$. In regions where $s \\approx 0$, this stabilizing effect is lost.\n2.  **Pressure-Curvature Drive (Destabilizing):** The term $C_{drive}(\\chi)$ is proportional to the product of the pressure gradient, $\\nabla p$, and the component of the magnetic curvature vector normal to the flux surface, $\\mathbf{\\kappa}_n$. The sign of the contribution depends on the relative direction of $\\nabla p$ and $\\mathbf{\\kappa}_n$. \"Bad\" curvature exists where $\\nabla p \\cdot \\mathbf{\\kappa}_n > 0$, driving instability. \"Good\" curvature exists where $\\nabla p \\cdot \\mathbf{\\kappa}_n < 0$, which is stabilizing. Instability occurs when the integrated effect of the bad curvature drive overcomes the field-line bending stabilization.\n\n**Application to Tokamaks:**\nIn an axisymmetric tokamak, the geometry is relatively simple. The outboard side of the torus ($R > R_0$) has bad curvature, and the inboard side ($R < R_0$) has good curvature. The problem states that the magnetic shear $s$ is finite everywhere. A ballooning mode is thus driven on the outboard side but stabilized by both the good curvature on the inboard side and the finite shear $s$ everywhere along the field line. The stability limit is reached when the pressure gradient (proportional to $\\beta$) is large enough to overcome this stabilization.\nThe magnetic shear in a tokamak is intrinsically linked to the profile of the safety factor $q$, which is in turn determined by the plasma current $I_p$. A larger current $I_p$ (at fixed $a, B$) corresponds to a lower $q$ and generally allows for stronger magnetic shear, enhancing stability. This direct link between the stabilizing mechanism and the plasma current leads to the Troyon limit, an empirical and theoretical scaling where the maximum attainable $\\beta$ is proportional to the plasma current, normalized by the device size and magnetic field: $\\beta_{max} \\propto I_p / (aB)$. The normalized beta, $\\beta_N = \\beta / (I_p/aB)$, is a key figure of merit that is found to be largely independent of the specific device, a consequence of the underlying universal MHD physics in an axisymmetric geometry.\n\n**Application to Stellarators:**\nIn a non-axisymmetric, $3$D stellarator, the situation is more complex.\n- The magnetic configuration is created by external coils, not a large net plasma current $I_p$. Therefore, any pressure limit cannot be normalized by $I_p$.\n- The curvature $\\mathbf{\\kappa}$ varies in a complex manner in all three dimensions. A key design goal for modern stellarators is to shape the magnetic field to minimize regions of bad curvature and maximize regions of good curvature, leading to what is called \"quasi-symmetry\" or \"omnigenity\".\n- The problem states that there can be localized regions where the magnetic shear $s \\approx 0$. If such a shear-free region happens to coincide with a region of net bad curvature, the stabilizing field-line bending term vanishes. This creates a \"weak link\" for stability. A mode can grow in this region with very little to stop it, potentially becoming very extended along the field line and resembling an interchange mode. The stability of such a mode depends critically on the local balance of curvature. The overall $\\beta$ limit is then determined by the success of the $3$D design in avoiding such detrimental combinations of low shear and bad curvature anywhere on any flux surface. The limit is highly sensitive to the details of the $3$D geometry.\n\n**Option-by-Option Analysis**\n\n**A. In axisymmetric tokamaks, high-$n$ ideal ballooning instabilities are regulated by finite magnetic shear $s$ along field lines, and the ensuing pressure-driven threshold exhibits a Troyon-like scaling, effectively tying the allowable $\\beta$ to $I_p$ through the competition between pressure-curvature drive and field-line bending; at fixed $q$ profile and shaping, this produces an approximately device-independent normalized threshold, consistent with axisymmetric field-line geometry.**\nThis statement is a complete and accurate description of the physics. The regulation of ballooning modes by shear ($s$), the competition between drive and bending, the resulting link between $\\beta$ and $I_p$ (the Troyon-like scaling), and the device-independent normalized threshold ($\\beta_N$) are all cornerstone concepts of tokamak stability.\n**Verdict: Correct**\n\n**B. In stellarators, global pressure-driven limits are not controlled by $I_p$ but by three-dimensional curvature and magnetic shear distributions; local regions with $s\\approx 0$ reduce field-line bending stabilization, enable extended mode structures along complex field lines, and can precipitate global ballooning or interchange-like instabilities that set $\\beta$ limits depending on the distribution of bad curvature and three-dimensional coupling, not on a tokamak-like $I_p$ normalization.**\nThis statement correctly contrasts the stellarator with the tokamak. The $\\beta$ limit in a near-currentless stellarator depends on the externally imposed $3$D magnetic geometry. It correctly identifies the critical danger of local shear-free ($s \\approx 0$) regions, which eliminate the main stabilizing mechanism and can lead to extended, interchange-like modes. The limit is therefore set by the detailed geometric distributions of curvature and shear, with no equivalent to the $I_p$-based Troyon scaling.\n**Verdict: Correct**\n\n**C. Because three-dimensional curvature in stellarators introduces alternating favorable curvature, it generally stabilizes ballooning more effectively than axisymmetry, implying an intrinsic increase of the $\\beta$ limit over tokamaks that is insensitive to detailed geometry or local shear-free pockets.**\nThis statement makes an incorrect generalization. While using $3$D shaping to create favorable curvature is a key principle of stellarator design, the outcome is extremely sensitive to the details of that geometry. The claim of insensitivity to \"detailed geometry or local shear-free pockets\" is fundamentally wrong. As explained for option B, local shear-free pockets can be profoundly destabilizing and can severely limit $\\beta$. The high performance of a stellarator is a result of meticulous design optimization, not an \"intrinsic\" property that ignores crucial geometric details.\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n**D. Local shear-free regions in a stellarator cannot materially affect the pressure-driven limit if the flux-surface-averaged shear is finite, since ballooning stability depends exclusively on global averages; therefore, localized $s\\approx 0$ segments do not lower the global threshold.**\nThis statement misunderstands the nature of ballooning modes. Ballooning modes are localized phenomena, sensitive to the *local* conditions along a magnetic field line. Stability is determined by the \"weakest link\"—the most unstable field line on a flux surface. A local region of $s \\approx 0$ represents such a weak link, dramatically reducing local stability regardless of the average shear value on the flux surface. The mode will simply localize in this region of weak stabilization. Therefore, localized $s \\approx 0$ segments absolutely can, and do, materially affect and lower the global $\\beta$ threshold.\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n**E. The tokamak Troyon-like limit is independent of the safety factor $q$ and plasma shaping because high-$n$ pressure-driven modes behave as global interchange modes without sensitivity to magnetic shear; therefore, the threshold depends only on $B$ and $R_0$ rather than on $q$ or $s$.**\nThis statement is incorrect on multiple counts. The tokamak ballooning limit is strongly dependent on the safety factor $q$ (limit on pressure gradient scales as $p' \\propto 1/q^2$), the magnetic shear $s$, and plasma shaping (e.g., elongation, triangularity). High-$n$ pressure-driven modes in a standard tokamak are ballooning modes, not interchange modes, precisely because finite shear provides stabilization that interchange modes lack by definition. The Troyon limit implicitly contains all these dependencies through its normalization with $I_p$, which itself depends on $q$, $a$, $R_0$, and $B$. The threshold is not a simple function of only $B$ and $R_0$.\n**Verdict: Incorrect**"}]


title: [multiple_choice] Justify thalamic territorial borders based on PCA P1–P2 segment branching and basilar tip anatomy, including the variability of the polar artery origin.

Question: An advanced student of systematic anatomy is asked to justify the anterior, paramedian (medial), inferolateral (thalamogeniculate), and posterior (pulvinar/posterior choroidal) territorial borders of the thalamus using only the following foundational bases: (i) the definition of arterial segments by their fixed landmarks, (ii) the principle that end-arterial perforators define non-overlapping primary territories with limited anastomosis, and (iii) the observation that flow contributions and branch densities follow proximal-to-distal branching hierarchies and caliber at bifurcations. The student is to reason from the branching of the Posterior Cerebral Artery (PCA) at segments $\mathrm{P1}$–$\mathrm{P2}$ and the geometry of the basilar bifurcation (basilar tip), while accounting for variability in the origin of the polar (tuberothalamic) artery.

Which option most accurately articulates a justification of these territorial borders from those bases?

A. The paramedian thalamus is supplied by thalamoperforating arteries arising proximally from $\mathrm{P1}$ before the junction with the Posterior Communicating Artery (PCom); thus, the medial territory is delimited by the reach of end-arterial perforators sourced at the basilar tip and $\mathrm{P1}$. Variation in basilar tip geometry and relative $\mathrm{P1}$ caliber modulates perforator number and reach, so the medial border with lateral territories reflects the transition from $\mathrm{P1}$ perforators to distal circumflex branches at the $\mathrm{PCom}$ junction. The inferolateral thalamus is supplied by thalamogeniculate (inferolateral) branches originating from $\mathrm{P2}$ circumflex segments, establishing a lateral boundary distinct from the medial paramedian field. The posterior thalamus and pulvinar are supplied predominantly by posterior choroidal arteries that arise from $\mathrm{P2}$, delimiting the posterior border. The anterior (tuberothalamic) territory is usually supplied by a polar artery from the $\mathrm{PCom}$; when the polar artery is absent or, variably, arises from $\mathrm{P1}$ or $\mathrm{P2}$, the anterior border shifts as paramedian or inferolateral sources encroach. In the Artery of Percheron (AOP) variant, a single trunk from one $\mathrm{P1}$ subserves bilateral paramedian fields, shifting the midline boundary according to the unilateral source.

B. The inferolateral thalamus is chiefly supplied by perforators from $\mathrm{P1}$, whereas the paramedian region depends on circumflex branches from $\mathrm{P2}$; the posterior choroidal arteries mostly arise from $\mathrm{P1}$. The polar (tuberothalamic) artery is an invariant branch of $\mathrm{P1}$, and basilar tip geometry does not influence territorial borders because perforators anastomose widely within the thalamus.

C. The polar (tuberothalamic) artery consistently arises from the Anterior Choroidal Artery (AChA) and fixes the anterior border independently of posterior circulation variants; the paramedian territory is defined by $\mathrm{P2}$ branches that cross the midline freely, while the inferolateral and posterior territories arise predominantly from $\mathrm{P1}$. Basilar tip configuration is negligible for these borders.

D. In the fetal-type PCA configuration, the dominance of the $\mathrm{PCom}$ abolishes thalamic paramedian perforators from $\mathrm{P1}$, so the anterior and paramedian thalamus are supplied by the Middle Cerebral Artery (MCA), whereas the inferolateral and posterior territories are supplied by $\mathrm{P2}$ branches. Borders therefore track cortical watershed zones rather than $\mathrm{P1}$–$\mathrm{P2}$ branching.

E. Territorial borders in the thalamus are determined primarily by white matter tract architecture such as the anterior thalamic radiation, so arterial segment anatomy at $\mathrm{P1}$–$\mathrm{P2}$ is incidental. The polar artery origin is uniform and does not affect the location of the anterior border, and basilar bifurcation geometry does not alter perforator distributions.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "Begin from segment definitions, end-artery principles, and branching hierarchy. By convention, the Posterior Cerebral Artery (PCA) segment $\\mathrm{P1}$ extends from the basilar artery bifurcation (basilar tip) to the junction with the Posterior Communicating Artery (PCom), and $\\mathrm{P2}$ extends distal to that junction along the ambient cistern. End-arterial perforators define territories with limited anastomoses, so the origin site strongly predicts the specific field they supply. Proximally originating perforators tend to feed medial, deep structures (short path length to paramedian diencephalon), while more distal circumflex and choroidal branches tend to sweep laterally and posteriorly.\n\nDerive the medial (paramedian) territory. Thalamoperforating arteries arise from $\\mathrm{P1}$ near the basilar tip before the $\\mathrm{PCom}$ junction, coursing dorsomedially to the paramedian thalamus and rostral midbrain. Because these are end arteries with limited overlap, their reach defines the medial paramedian territory. The basilar tip geometry (height and symmetry) and relative $\\mathrm{P1}$ calibers influence the number and distribution of thalamoperforators from each side. In variants such as the Artery of Percheron (AOP), a single trunk from one $\\mathrm{P1}$ supplies bilateral paramedian territories, shifting the midline boundary in accordance with the unilateral source. If $\\mathrm{P1}$ is hypoplastic, perforator density may be reduced or compensated contralaterally, affecting the medial border.\n\nDerive the inferolateral territory. The thalamogeniculate (inferolateral) arteries originate from $\\mathrm{P2}$ circumflex segments, sweeping laterally to supply the ventrolateral thalamus and geniculate bodies. The lateral boundary between paramedian and inferolateral fields follows the transition from $\\mathrm{P1}$-derived perforators to $\\mathrm{P2}$-derived circumflex branches at and distal to the $\\mathrm{PCom}$ junction. Limited collateralization between these end-arterial systems preserves a meaningful border.\n\nDerive the posterior (pulvinar/posterior choroidal) territory. Posterior choroidal arteries arise from $\\mathrm{P2}$ and supply the pulvinar and posterior-medial thalamus. Their origin distal to the $\\mathrm{PCom}$ positions their supply zone posteriorly and dorsally, delimiting the posterior border with the inferolateral and paramedian territories.\n\nAccount for the anterior (tuberothalamic) field and the polar artery variability. The tuberothalamic (polar) artery most commonly arises from the $\\mathrm{PCom}$ and supplies the anterior thalamus (including anterior nuclei). However, its origin is variable: it may be absent, or it may arise from $\\mathrm{P1}$ or, less commonly, from $\\mathrm{P2}$. When absent, the anterior territory is variably supplied by adjacent systems, most often encroachment by $\\mathrm{P1}$ thalamoperforators (paramedian) or by $\\mathrm{P2}$ thalamogeniculate branches; this shifts the anterior border. Because the polar artery’s origin is not uniform, territorial borders cannot be assumed fixed independent of this variability.\n\nSynthesize basilar tip influence. The basilar bifurcation determines the proximal geometry for $\\mathrm{P1}$ and the launch point for thalamoperforators. Asymmetry in $\\mathrm{P1}$ caliber or a high/low basilar tip can alter the density, angle, and cross-midline propensity of perforators, including permitting the AOP variant. These anatomical facts justify why the medial borders and even midline delineations can vary according to basilar tip morphology.\n\nNow evaluate options:\n\nA. This option aligns with all derived principles. It correctly places paramedian supply in thalamoperforators from $\\mathrm{P1}$ proximal to the $\\mathrm{PCom}$, ties medial borders to the $\\mathrm{P1}$–$\\mathrm{P2}$ transition, and recognizes basilar tip and $\\mathrm{P1}$ caliber as modulators of perforator density and reach. It correctly attributes inferolateral supply to $\\mathrm{P2}$ thalamogeniculate branches, posterior supply to posterior choroidal arteries from $\\mathrm{P2}$, and recognizes the usual $\\mathrm{PCom}$ origin and variability of the polar artery, including absence and alternative origins from $\\mathrm{P1}$ or $\\mathrm{P2}$ that shift borders. The Artery of Percheron (AOP) is properly described as a single trunk from one $\\mathrm{P1}$ supplying bilateral paramedian territories. Verdict — Correct.\n\nB. This reverses the principal supply pattern, incorrectly assigning inferolateral supply to $\\mathrm{P1}$ and posterior choroidal origin to $\\mathrm{P1}$. It asserts an invariant $\\mathrm{P1}$ origin of the polar artery and overstates anastomosis within the thalamus, contradicting the end-arterial principle. It denies basilar tip influence, which is not consistent with known variability. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nC. This places the polar artery as consistently arising from the Anterior Choroidal Artery (AChA), which is not the usual source; the typical source is the $\\mathrm{PCom}$ with variability that can include $\\mathrm{P1}$ or $\\mathrm{P2}$ or absence. It also incorrectly assigns paramedian supply to $\\mathrm{P2}$ and posterior/inferolateral to $\\mathrm{P1}$. The claim that cross-midline freedom determines borders ignores the end-arterial nature of these perforators. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nD. This overgeneralizes the fetal-type PCA variant. While a dominant $\\mathrm{PCom}$ can alter $\\mathrm{P1}$ caliber and perforator contributions, it does not abolish $\\mathrm{P1}$ thalamoperforators categorically, nor does it reassign the anterior and paramedian thalamus to Middle Cerebral Artery (MCA) territories. Cortical watershed logic does not govern deep thalamic end-arterial borders. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nE. This shifts the determinant of borders to white matter tract architecture, which does not define vascular territories that are governed by arterial origin and end-artery distribution. It also assumes a uniform polar artery origin and denies basilar tip influence, both contrary to established variability. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nTherefore, option A most accurately justifies the thalamic territorial borders from $\\mathrm{P1}$–$\\mathrm{P2}$ branching, basilar tip anatomy, and polar artery variability."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "The problem statement is subjected to validation before proceeding to a solution.\n\n### Step 1: Extract Givens\n\nThe problem provides the following bases and context for justifying the vascular territories of the thalamus:\n1.  **Topic of Justification**: The anterior, paramedian (medial), inferolateral (thalamogeniculate), and posterior (pulvinar/posterior choroidal) territorial borders of the thalamus.\n2.  **Foundational Bases**:\n    *   (i) The definition of arterial segments by their fixed landmarks.\n    *   (ii) The principle that end-arterial perforators define non-overlapping primary territories with limited anastomosis.\n    *   (iii) The observation that flow contributions and branch densities follow proximal-to-distal branching hierarchies and caliber at bifurcations.\n3.  **Anatomical Context**: Reasoning must proceed from the branching of the Posterior Cerebral Artery (PCA) at segments $P1$–$P2$ and the geometry of the basilar bifurcation (basilar tip).\n4.  **Specific Constraint**: The reasoning must account for variability in the origin of the polar (tuberothalamic) artery.\n\n### Step 2: Validate Using Extracted Givens\n\nThe problem is assessed for validity against the established criteria.\n\n*   **Scientifically Grounded**: The problem is firmly rooted in established principles of neurovascular anatomy. The anatomical structures mentioned—the thalamus, Posterior Cerebral Artery ($PCA$), its segments ($P1$, $P2$), the basilar artery, Posterior Communicating Artery ($PCom$), and specific perforating arteries (thalamoperforating, thalamogeniculate, polar, posterior choroidal)—are all standard components of human systematic anatomy. The principles provided—segmental anatomy, end-arterial supply with limited collaterals, and hemodynamic influences on branching—are fundamental concepts in the study of cerebral circulation. The problem is scientifically sound.\n*   **Well-Posed**: The question requests a justification of known anatomical borders using a specified set of principles. It is structured as a multiple-choice question asking for the \"most accurate\" articulation, which is a standard format for assessing complex reasoning in medical sciences. The provided information is sufficient to construct a logical argument and evaluate the given options.\n*   **Objective**: The language is technical, precise, and devoid of subjective claims. The task is to apply established principles to an anatomical system.\n\nBased on this assessment, the problem statement does not violate any of the criteria for invalidity. It is scientifically sound, well-posed, objective, and formalizable within the domain of systematic anatomy.\n\n### Step 3: Verdict and Action\n\nThe problem statement is **valid**. A solution will be derived by applying the given principles, followed by an evaluation of each option.\n\n### Derivation of Solution from First Principles\n\nBased on the provided axiomatic bases, we can construct the anatomical justification as follows:\n\n1.  **Arterial Segmentation (Principle i)**: The $PCA$ is divided into segments by landmarks. The $P1$ segment extends from the basilar artery bifurcation to the junction with the $PCom$. The $P2$ segment extends distally from the $PCom$ junction, coursing around the brainstem. This segmental division is crucial as different groups of perforating arteries arise from these distinct segments.\n\n2.  **End-Arterial Territories (Principle ii)**: The thalamus is supplied by deep perforating arteries that function as end-arteries. This means they supply discrete, non-overlapping volumes of tissue with minimal collateral connections (anastomosis) between adjacent territories. Consequently, the border between two territories is sharply defined by the interface between the capillary beds of two different perforating artery groups. The territory's existence and borders are a direct consequence of the origin and distribution of its parent perforating artery.\n\n3.  **Branching Hierarchies and Hemodynamics (Principle iii)**: The location of an artery's origin along the main trunk (proximal vs. distal) and the geometry/caliber of the parent vessel influence the number, size, and reach of the branches. Arteries arising more proximally (like from the $P1$ segment) are expected to supply the more medial and anterior structures relative to the course of the parent vessel. The geometry of the basilar bifurcation directly impacts the flow dynamics into the $P1$ segments and thus affects the perforators arising from this segment.\n\nApplying these principles to the thalamic territories:\n\n*   **Paramedian (Medial) Territory**: This territory encompasses the medial parts of the thalamus, often on both sides of the midline. Logically, these structures are closest to the origin of the $PCA$ at the basilar tip. Therefore, their blood supply must come from the most proximal perforators. These are the thalamoperforating arteries, which arise from the $P1$ segment. The extent of this territory is determined by the reach of these end-arteries. Variations in basilar tip anatomy and $P1$ caliber (Principle iii) will modulate the number and distribution of these perforators, thus influencing the size and precise borders of the paramedian territory.\n*   **Inferolateral Territory**: This territory is lateral to the paramedian region. As the $PCA$ courses distally, it passes the $PCom$ junction and becomes the $P2$ segment, which wraps laterally around the midbrain. The arteries supplying this more lateral region, the thalamogeniculate arteries, arise from this $P2$ segment. The border between the paramedian and inferolateral territories is thus the boundary between the supply zones of the $P1$-derived thalamoperforators and the $P2$-derived thalamogeniculate arteries. This follows the proximal-to-distal branching hierarchy (Principle iii).\n*   **Posterior Territory**: The most posterior part of the thalamus, the pulvinar, is supplied by arteries arising even more distally along the $P2$ segment—the medial and lateral posterior choroidal arteries. These define the posterior vascular border of the thalamus.\n*   **Anterior Territory**: This territory is supplied by the polar (or tuberothalamic) artery. The problem states its origin is variable. The most common origin is the $PCom$. If it arises from the $PCom$, it creates a distinct anterior territory. If it is absent or arises variably from $P1$ or $P2$, this territory will be subsumed by the perforators that would normally supply the adjacent paramedian or inferolateral territories, respectively. The anterior border is therefore plastic and depends on this specific anatomical variation.\n*   **Artery of Percheron ($AOP$) Variant**: The $AOP$ is a specific anatomical variant where a single trunk from one $P1$ segment gives rise to the thalamoperforating arteries for both paramedian thalami. This is a direct instantiation of Principle (iii) (variation in branching) and Principle (ii) (end-arterial supply), explaining bilateral infarction from a single vessel occlusion and the shift of the midline vascular boundary.\n\nThis reconstructed model provides a comprehensive justification for the thalamic vascular territories based strictly on the problem's premises.\n\n### Option-by-Option Analysis\n\nNow, each option is evaluated against this derived model.\n\n**A. The paramedian thalamus is supplied by thalamoperforating arteries arising proximally from $P1$ before the junction with the Posterior Communicating Artery (PCom); thus, the medial territory is delimited by the reach of end-arterial perforators sourced at the basilar tip and $P1$. Variation in basilar tip geometry and relative $P1$ caliber modulates perforator number and reach, so the medial border with lateral territories reflects the transition from $P1$ perforators to distal circumflex branches at the $PCom$ junction. The inferolateral thalamus is supplied by thalamogeniculate (inferolateral) branches originating from $P2$ circumflex segments, establishing a lateral boundary distinct from the medial paramedian field. The posterior thalamus and pulvinar are supplied predominantly by posterior choroidal arteries that arise from $P2$, delimiting the posterior border. The anterior (tuberothalamic) territory is usually supplied by a polar artery from the $PCom$; when the polar artery is absent or, variably, arises from $P1$ or $P2$, the anterior border shifts as paramedian or inferolateral sources encroach. In the Artery of Percheron (AOP) variant, a single trunk from one $P1$ subserves bilateral paramedian fields, shifting the midline boundary according to the unilateral source.**\n\nThis option aligns perfectly with the derived justification. It correctly identifies the arteries, their segmental origins ($P1$ vs. $P2$), and the territories they supply (paramedian, inferolateral, posterior). It correctly applies the principles of end-arterial supply (delimited reach), branching hierarchy (proximal/distal origins), and hemodynamic influence (basilar tip geometry). It correctly incorporates the specified variability of the polar artery and the significance of the $AOP$ variant.\n**Verdict: Correct**\n\n**B. The inferolateral thalamus is chiefly supplied by perforators from $P1$, whereas the paramedian region depends on circumflex branches from $P2$; the posterior choroidal arteries mostly arise from $P1$. The polar (tuberothalamic) artery is an invariant branch of $P1$, and basilar tip geometry does not influence territorial borders because perforators anastomose widely within the thalamus.**\n\nThis option contains several gross factual errors. The supply of the paramedian and inferolateral territories is reversed; paramedian is from $P1$, inferolateral from $P2$. Posterior choroidal arteries arise from $P2$, not $P1$. The polar artery's origin is explicitly stated to be variable in the problem, not invariant. The claim of wide anastomosis directly contradicts Principle (ii) of \"limited anastomosis.\"\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n**C. The polar (tuberothalamic) artery consistently arises from the Anterior Choroidal Artery (AChA) and fixes the anterior border independently of posterior circulation variants; the paramedian territory is defined by $P2$ branches that cross the midline freely, while the inferolateral and posterior territories arise predominantly from $P1$. Basilar tip configuration is negligible for these borders.**\n\nThis option makes multiple errors. The polar artery does not consistently arise from the $AChA$; its classic origin is the $PCom$ and it is variable. The paramedian territory is from $P1$, not $P2$. Inferolateral and posterior territories are from $P2$, not $P1$. The claim that branches cross the midline \"freely\" is an oversimplification that ignores the end-arterial principle, except in specific variants like the $AOP$. The claim that basilar tip configuration is negligible contradicts Principle (iii).\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n**D. In the fetal-type PCA configuration, the dominance of the $PCom$ abolishes thalamic paramedian perforators from $P1$, so the anterior and paramedian thalamus are supplied by the Middle Cerebral Artery (MCA), whereas the inferolateral and posterior territories are supplied by $P2$ branches. Borders therefore track cortical watershed zones rather than $P1$–$P2$ branching.**\n\nThis option introduces a profound anatomical error. The Middle Cerebral Artery ($MCA$) does not supply the thalamus. Its perforators (lenticulostriate arteries) supply the basal ganglia and internal capsule. Thalamic territories are defined by deep perforators from the posterior circulation, not by cortical watershed zones. While it correctly identifies that inferolateral/posterior territories come from $P2$, the central claims about $MCA$ supply are fundamentally false.\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n**E. Territorial borders in the thalamus are determined primarily by white matter tract architecture such as the anterior thalamic radiation, so arterial segment anatomy at $P1$–$P2$ is incidental. It claims that vascular anatomy is \"incidental\" to white matter architecture, which contradicts the task of reasoning *from* vascular principles. It falsely claims the polar artery origin is uniform, contradicting the problem's explicit constraint. It falsely claims basilar geometry is irrelevant, contradicting Principle (iii).\n**Verdict: Incorrect**"}]</wiki_content5.1>

<wiki_content5.2>title: [multiple_choice] Describe the difference between RWM and vertical displacement events (VDEs) and clarify under what equilibrium and control conditions an $n=0$ instability vs an $n=1$ RWM dominates.

Question: A circular-cross-section tokamak of major radius $R_0$ and minor radius $a$ is equipped with a thin resistive conducting wall at radius $b$ whose conductivity and thickness give a magnetic diffusion time $\tau_w$. The plasma is elongated with elongation $\kappa>1$, has pressure $p(r)$, current density $\mathbf{J}(r)$, and toroidal rotation frequency $\omega_\phi(r)$ that is sub-Alfvénic. The plasma is in static equilibrium satisfying $\mathbf{J}\times\mathbf{B}=\nabla p$ with perfectly nested flux surfaces, and is surrounded by a vacuum region up to the wall. The vertical position is controlled by an external feedback system with characteristic response time $\tau_c$ and limited bandwidth; non-axisymmetric error-field control coils exist but are not engaged in the scenario.

Consider the linear stability of two distinct classes of magnetohydrodynamic perturbations, characterized by toroidal mode number $n$: axisymmetric perturbations with $n=0$ and non-axisymmetric external kink-like perturbations with $n=1$. Let $\beta_N$ be the normalized beta, and suppose the device operates at $\beta_N$ between the ideal no-wall stability limit $\beta_{N,\mathrm{nw}}$ and the ideal perfectly conducting wall stability limit $\beta_{N,\mathrm{ww}}$, i.e.,
$$
\beta_{N,\mathrm{nw}}<\beta_N<\beta_{N,\mathrm{ww}}.
$$
Assume that the rotation is modest such that $\omega_\phi \tau_w$ is order unity, and that the elongation is high enough that the axisymmetric vertical restoring force from external fields is weak.

Starting from the ideal magnetohydrodynamics (MHD) equilibrium condition $\mathbf{J}\times\mathbf{B}=\nabla p$, the definition of toroidal mode number $n$ for perturbations $\propto e^{i n \phi}$, and the qualitative consequences of finite-conductivity walls (eddies that slow but do not prevent magnetic flux change over time $\tau_w$), reason about how the resistive wall modifies boundary conditions for $n=1$ kink-like perturbations and for $n=0$ vertical perturbations. Use the energy principle conceptually (that a mode is unstable if the total potential energy variation $\delta W$ is negative) and the role of plasma rotation (which can provide kinetic damping for non-axisymmetric modes) to distinguish the physical drivers and timescales of the two instabilities.

Which statement most accurately distinguishes resistive wall modes (RWMs) from vertical displacement events (VDEs) and correctly identifies the equilibrium and control conditions under which an $n=0$ instability versus an $n=1$ RWM will dominate the dynamics?

A. A resistive wall mode is a non-axisymmetric ($n=1$) external kink destabilized when $\beta_N$ exceeds the no-wall limit and plasma rotation is insufficient relative to the wall diffusion ($\omega_\phi \lesssim 1/\tau_w$), so its growth is limited by $\tau_w$. A vertical displacement event is an axisymmetric ($n=0$) motion driven by weak vertical restoring force at high $\kappa$, and it dominates when the vertical control bandwidth is inadequate ($\tau_c \gtrsim$ the inverse vertical growth rate), even if $\beta_N$ is below the with-wall kink limit. Thus $n=0$ dominates with high elongation and poor position control, while $n=1$ dominates near the kink threshold with low rotation, regardless of vertical feedback quality.

B. A vertical displacement event is a non-axisymmetric ($n=1$) pressure-driven kink that occurs only when $\beta_N>\beta_{N,\mathrm{ww}}$, whereas a resistive wall mode is axisymmetric ($n=0$) and is triggered by high elongation but can be completely prevented by a perfectly conducting wall; therefore, $n=1$ dominates at low elongation and strong feedback, and $n=0$ dominates at high rotation.

C. Both RWMs and VDEs are primarily stabilized by increasing plasma rotation; in particular, raising $\omega_\phi$ above $1/\tau_w$ destabilizes the RWM by decoupling the plasma from the wall, whereas the VDE is independent of feedback bandwidth and is controlled only by $\beta_N$. Therefore, $n=1$ dominates at high rotation and $n=0$ dominates at low $\beta_N$.

D. RWMs grow on the Alfvén time $\tau_A$ and are unaffected by wall resistivity, while VDEs grow only on resistive wall times $\tau_w$; accordingly, $n=1$ dominates when $\tau_w\gg \tau_A$ and $n=0$ dominates when $\beta_N$ exceeds the no-wall limit but rotation is fast.

E. $n=1$ RWMs and $n=0$ VDEs are identical in symmetry and both can be eliminated by fast vertical position feedback ($\tau_c\ll \tau_w$); the wall only affects $n=0$ modes. Consequently, whichever instability has the higher linear growth rate is always suppressed first by the same control coils, independent of $\beta_N$ or $\omega_\phi$.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "The equilibrium $\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$ implies force balance on flux surfaces and sets up a base state for linear stability analysis. The toroidal mode number $n$ labels the azimuthal dependence of a perturbation as $e^{i n \\phi}$, so $n=0$ is axisymmetric (no toroidal variation) while $n=1$ is the lowest-order non-axisymmetric kink-like perturbation.\n\nFor non-axisymmetric external kink-like perturbations ($n=1$), the ideal MHD energy principle indicates that as the pressure (or normalized beta $\\beta_N$) increases, the plasma approaches the ideal external kink limit. In the presence of a perfectly conducting wall, image currents in the wall provide stabilizing boundary conditions that can raise the stability threshold from the no-wall limit $\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{nw}}$ to the with-wall limit $\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{ww}}$. For a real wall with finite conductivity, the wall cannot maintain constant magnetic flux indefinitely; magnetic field perturbations diffuse through the wall over a characteristic time $\\tau_w$. This finite resistivity relaxes the effective boundary condition from “perfect conductor” towards “vacuum” on timescales comparable to $\\tau_w$, allowing a mode whose ideal stability requires a conducting wall (the resistive wall mode) to grow with a characteristic rate set by the wall diffusion. The plasma’s toroidal rotation $\\omega_\\phi$ couples the mode to a continuum of stable waves (e.g., the resistive or Alfvén continua) and can provide kinetic damping; when $\\omega_\\phi \\tau_w$ is sufficiently large, rotation can effectively prevent the mode from seeing the wall as resistive on the relevant timescale, while when $\\omega_\\phi \\tau_w\\lesssim 1$ the mode can grow on the resistive wall timescale. Therefore, an $n=1$ RWM is driven by pressure/current profiles near the kink limit with finite-conductivity wall and is sensitive to rotation and wall time.\n\nFor axisymmetric vertical motion ($n=0$), the perturbation does not involve toroidal variation but rather a rigid-body-like vertical displacement of the plasma column. Its stability is set by the net vertical restoring force, which depends on the external poloidal field configuration and plasma shape (especially elongation $\\kappa$). High elongation reduces the passive vertical restoring force (because the curvature of field lines and proximity to the wall alter the net potential well), so the $n=0$ vertical mode can become weakly damped or even unstable in the absence of active control. The resistive wall supports eddy currents that can provide some passive stabilization by opposing changes in poloidal flux, but this is limited by $\\tau_w$. In practice, vertical stability in elongated tokamaks is achieved by active position control with coils and sensors providing a restoring force via feedback; the ability to stabilize depends on the control loop bandwidth and response time $\\tau_c$ relative to the intrinsic vertical growth rate $\\gamma_v$ (which is set by the magnetic configuration and wall properties). If $\\tau_c$ is too large compared to $1/\\gamma_v$, the control cannot respond quickly enough and a vertical displacement event can occur, even when non-axisymmetric kink limits are not exceeded. Plasma rotation has limited direct stabilizing influence on the axisymmetric vertical mode compared to the role of external control.\n\nPutting these elements together:\n- $n=1$ RWM: non-axisymmetric, pressure/current-driven external kink, destabilized when $\\beta_N$ exceeds the no-wall limit ($\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{nw}}$) but is still below the with-wall ideal limit ($\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{ww}}$), provided plasma rotation is insufficient relative to wall diffusion (qualitatively, $\\omega_\\phi \\lesssim 1/\\tau_w$). Growth is on a timescale comparable to $\\tau_w$ and can be mitigated by sufficient rotation or non-axisymmetric feedback.\n- $n=0$ VDE: axisymmetric vertical shift, driven by weak passive vertical restoring force due to high elongation $\\kappa$ and insufficient active vertical control. Dominates when the vertical control system’s response time $\\tau_c$ is not fast enough compared to the intrinsic vertical instability growth time (i.e., $\\tau_c \\gtrsim 1/\\gamma_v$), often independent of the $n=1$ kink thresholds. Passive stabilization by the wall is limited by $\\tau_w$ but the primary tool is active control.\n\nNow evaluate each option:\n\nA. This option correctly identifies an RWM as non-axisymmetric ($n=1$) external kink, destabilized when $\\beta_N$ is above the no-wall limit with a finite-conductivity wall, and specifies the role of insufficient rotation via the qualitative condition $\\omega_\\phi \\lesssim 1/\\tau_w$. It correctly states that its growth is limited by $\\tau_w$. It correctly describes a VDE as axisymmetric ($n=0$), driven by weak vertical restoring force at high elongation, and emphasizes the dependence on vertical control bandwidth ($\\tau_c$ relative to the inverse growth rate). The concluding comparison—that $n=0$ dominates with high $\\kappa$ and poor position control, while $n=1$ dominates near the kink threshold with low rotation—aligns with the physical reasoning from ideal MHD boundary conditions, wall resistivity, and control physics. Verdict: Correct.\n\nB. This option incorrectly asserts that a VDE is non-axisymmetric ($n=1$) and pressure-driven at $\\beta_N>\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{ww}}$, which confuses the symmetry and driving mechanism; VDEs are axisymmetric and tied to vertical force balance rather than exceeding the with-wall kink limit. It also mislabels an RWM as axisymmetric ($n=0$) and claims it can be completely prevented by a perfectly conducting wall; RWMs are defined by the presence of a resistive wall and are non-axisymmetric external kink-like modes. The concluding dominance conditions are reversed and unsupported. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nC. In reality, increasing $\\omega_\\phi$ tends to stabilize RWMs via kinetic damping and coupling effects; claiming destabilization when $\\omega_\\phi$ is large is the opposite of established behavior. It also claims the VDE is independent of feedback bandwidth and only controlled by $\\beta_N$, which neglects the critical role of active vertical position control and the fact that vertical stability is weakly dependent on $\\beta_N$ compared to shape and control. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nD. This option asserts that RWMs grow on the Alfvén time $\\tau_A$ and are unaffected by wall resistivity, while VDEs grow only on $\\tau_w$. RWMs are characterized by growth on the resistive wall time, not on the Alfvén time; ideal external kinks (without wall resistivity considerations) can be fast, but RWMs specifically are slow, wall-resistivity-governed modes. VDE growth can be comparable to wall times but is driven by vertical force balance and control dynamics; stating that RWMs are unaffected by wall resistivity contradicts the definition of a resistive wall mode. Its properties (conductivity and thickness) define a magnetic diffusion time $\\tau_w$.\n*   **Plasma Properties:** The plasma has pressure $p(r)$, current density $\\mathbf{J}(r)$, and a sub-Alfvénic toroidal rotation frequency $\\omega_\\phi(r)$.\n*   **Equilibrium:** The plasma is in a static magnetohydrodynamic (MHD) equilibrium satisfying $\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$ with perfectly nested flux surfaces. A vacuum region exists between the plasma and the wall.\n*   **Control Systems:** A vertical position feedback system with response time $\\tau_c$ and limited bandwidth is present. Non-axisymmetric error-field control coils exist but are not engaged.\n*   **Perturbations:** Two classes of perturbations are considered, characterized by toroidal mode number $n$ for fields varying as $e^{i n \\phi}$: axisymmetric ($n=0$) and non-axisymmetric external kink-like ($n=1$).\n*   **Operating Regime:** The normalized beta, $\\beta_N$, is between the ideal no-wall stability limit, $\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{nw}}$, and the ideal perfectly conducting wall stability limit, $\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{ww}}$. This is expressed as the inequality $\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{nw}}<\\beta_N<\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{ww}}$.\n*   **Assumptions:** The plasma rotation is modest, such that $\\omega_\\phi \\tau_w$ is of order unity. The elongation is high enough that the intrinsic vertical restoring force from external fields is weak.\n\n**Step 2: Validate Using Extracted Givens**\n\nThe problem statement establishes a scenario within the standard theoretical framework of MHD stability in tokamaks. The concepts presented—such as $\\beta$ limits, the resistive wall model, plasma rotation, vertical instability in elongated plasmas, and feedback control—are all scientifically grounded and central to fusion plasma physics.\n\nA minor inconsistency exists in the description of the plasma geometry: it is described as both a \"circular-cross-section tokamak\" and having an \"elongation $\\kappa>1$\". By definition, a circular cross-section has an elongation of $\\kappa=1$.
