A unique conceptual answer can be derived.\n*   **Objective:** The language is technical and free of subjective claims.\n\nThe problem does not exhibit any of the listed flaws (e.g., scientific unsoundness, incompleteness, etc.), provided the minor geometric ambiguity is resolved as discussed.\n\n**Step 3: Verdict and Action**\n\nThe problem is deemed **valid**. We may proceed to the solution.\n\n### Derivation and Analysis\n\nThe problem requires distinguishing between two types of MHD instabilities in a tokamak: the Resistive Wall Mode (RWM) and the Vertical Displacement Event (VDE), and identifying the conditions under which one would dominate.\n\n**1. Fundamental Concepts**\n\n*   **MHD Equilibrium:** The starting point is the static equilibrium force balance $\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$. Any perturbation that disrupts this balance can lead to an instability.\n*   **Energy Principle:** An instability can grow if a plasma displacement $\\xi$ leads to a negative change in the system's potential energy, $\\delta W < 0$. $\\delta W$ includes contributions from the plasma volume, the plasma surface, and the surrounding vacuum region.\n*   **Role of a Conducting Wall:**\n    *   An **ideal wall** (infinite conductivity, $\\tau_w \\rightarrow \\infty$) acts as a perfect flux conserver. The boundary condition is that the normal component of the perturbed magnetic field, $\\delta B_n$, is zero at the wall. This provides strong stabilization for external modes.\n    *   A **resistive wall** (finite $\\tau_w$) allows magnetic flux to penetrate on the timescale $\\tau_w$. It acts like an ideal wall for fast perturbations (growth rate $\\gamma \\gg 1/\\tau_w$) and provides no stabilization for very slow perturbations ($\\gamma \\ll 1/\\tau_w$). For modes with growth rates on the order of the wall time ($\\gamma \\sim 1/\\tau_w$), the wall slows but does not prevent instability.\n\n**2. Analysis of the $n=1$ Resistive Wall Mode (RWM)**\n\n*   **Symmetry and Driver:** The RWM is a non-axisymmetric instability, typically with toroidal mode number $n=1$. It is fundamentally an external kink mode, driven by plasma current and pressure gradients, especially at high $\\beta_N$.\n*   **Operating Regime and Growth:** The problem specifies the operational space as $\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{nw}} < \\beta_N < \\beta_{N,\\mathrm{ww}}$.\n    *   $\\beta_N > \\beta_{N,\\mathrm{nw}}$ means there is sufficient free energy from the pressure gradient and current density to drive an external kink mode if there were no wall. This corresponds to $\\delta W < 0$ in the absence of a wall.\n    *   $\\beta_N < \\beta_{N,\\mathrm{ww}}$ means an ideal wall would stabilize this mode.\n    *   With a *resistive* wall, the mode is no longer fully stabilized. The wall's resistance allows the stabilizing eddy currents to decay, permitting the external field perturbation to grow. The growth is limited by the rate at which magnetic flux can penetrate the wall. Consequently, the ideal kink mode, which would grow on the fast Alfvén timescale ($\\tau_A$), is converted into a slowly growing RWM with a growth rate $\\gamma_{RWM} \\sim 1/\\tau_w$.\n*   **Stabilization by Rotation:** A non-axisymmetric mode structure ($n=1$) rotates with the plasma at frequency $\\omega_\\phi$. From the perspective of the stationary wall, the mode's magnetic field oscillates. If the oscillation is fast enough, specifically $\\omega_\\phi \\gg 1/\\tau_w$, the resistive wall does not have time to respond and acts like an ideal wall, thus stabilizing the mode. Instability occurs when the rotation is insufficient, i.e., when $\\omega_\\phi \\tau_w \\lesssim O(1)$, allowing the mode to \"lock\" to the wall and grow. The problem states $\\omega_\\phi \\tau_w \\sim O(1)$, placing the plasma near the threshold for rotational stabilization.\n\n**3. Analysis of the $n=0$ Vertical Displacement Event (VDE)**\n\n*   **Symmetry and Driver:** The VDE is an axisymmetric instability ($n=0$). It is not driven by $\\beta_N$ in the same way as a kink mode. Instead, it is a positional instability inherent to plasmas with non-circular, vertically elongated cross-sections ($\\kappa>1$). The external magnetic fields required to create the elongation also create a configuration where a small vertical displacement moves the plasma into a region with a weaker restoring vertical field (or a stronger destabilizing field), leading to an unstable positive feedback loop. This is an instability of the plasma's rigid vertical position.\n*   **Growth Rate and Stabilization:**\n    *   The raw instability grows on the very fast poloidal Alfvén timescale. However, any passive conducting structures, such as the resistive wall at radius $b$, provide passive stabilization. As the plasma moves, it induces eddy currents in the wall that create a restoring force. This slows the growth rate down to the wall's resistive time constant, i.e., $\\gamma_{VDE} \\sim 1/\\tau_w$.\n    *   Since the instability is now slow, it can be controlled by an active feedback system. This system measures the plasma's vertical position and uses a set of axisymmetric ($n=0$) control coils to apply a corrective magnetic field. For stabilization to be successful, the feedback system's characteristic response time $\\tau_c$ must be much shorter than the instability growth time: $\\tau_c \\ll \\tau_w$. If the feedback system is too slow, has insufficient gain, or limited bandwidth ($\\tau_c \\gtrsim \\tau_w$), it will fail to control the plasma's position, resulting in a VDE where the plasma drifts vertically until it collides with the wall.\n*   **Role of Rotation:** Since the VDE is an axisymmetric ($n=0$) motion, it is symmetric with respect to toroidal angle $\\phi$. Therefore, toroidal rotation $\\omega_\\phi$ has no direct stabilizing or destabilizing effect on it.\n\n**4. Conclusion on Dominant Instabilities**\n\n*   An **$n=1$ RWM will dominate** if the plasma operates in the window $\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{nw}} < \\beta_N < \\beta_{N,\\mathrm{ww}}$ AND plasma rotation is insufficient for stabilization ($\\omega_\\phi \\tau_w \\lesssim 1$). This is independent of the vertical position control system.\n*   An **$n=0$ VDE will dominate** if the plasma is sufficiently elongated ($\\kappa > 1$) AND the vertical position feedback is inadequate ($\\tau_c \\gtrsim \\tau_w$). This is largely independent of $\\beta_N$ and $\\omega_\\phi$.\n\n### Option-by-Option Analysis\n\n**A. A resistive wall mode is a non-axisymmetric ($n=1$) external kink destabilized when $\\beta_N$ exceeds the no-wall limit and plasma rotation is insufficient relative to the wall diffusion ($\\omega_\\phi \\lesssim 1/\\tau_w$), so its growth is limited by $\\tau_w$. A vertical displacement event is an axisymmetric ($n=0$) motion driven by weak vertical restoring force at high $\\kappa$, and it dominates when the vertical control bandwidth is inadequate ($\\tau_c \\gtrsim$ the inverse vertical growth rate), even if $\\beta_N$ is below the with-wall kink limit. Thus $n=0$ dominates with high elongation and poor position control, while $n=1$ dominates near the kink threshold with low rotation, regardless of vertical feedback quality.**\nThis statement accurately defines both the RWM and VDE. It correctly identifies the toroidal mode numbers ($n=1$ for RWM, $n=0$ for VDE), the physical drivers ($\\beta_N > \\beta_{N,\\mathrm{nw}}$ for RWM, high $\\kappa$ for VDE), the instability timescales ($\\sim \\tau_w$ for both), and the distinct stabilization mechanisms (rotation for RWM, feedback for VDE). The summary of dominance conditions is also correct.\n**Verdict: Correct.**\n\n**B. A vertical displacement event is a non-axisymmetric ($n=1$) pressure-driven kink that occurs only when $\\beta_N>\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{ww}}$, whereas a resistive wall mode is axisymmetric ($n=0$) and is triggered by high elongation but can be completely prevented by a perfectly conducting wall; therefore, $n=1$ dominates at low elongation and strong feedback, and $n=0$ dominates at high rotation.**\nThis statement incorrectly swaps the mode numbers of VDEs and RWMs. VDEs are $n=0$, RWMs are $n=1$. It also incorrectly states VDEs occur for $\\beta_N>\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{ww}}$, which describes an ideal instability, not a VDE. It falsely claims RWMs are triggered by elongation. The conclusion is based on these incorrect premises.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\n**C. Both RWMs and VDEs are primarily stabilized by increasing plasma rotation; in particular, raising $\\omega_\\phi$ above $1/\\tau_w$ destabilizes the RWM by decoupling the plasma from the wall, whereas the VDE is independent of feedback bandwidth and is controlled only by $\\beta_N$. Therefore, $n=1$ dominates at high rotation and $n=0$ dominates at low $\\beta_N$.**\nThis statement is fundamentally flawed. Only the non-axisymmetric RWM is stabilized by rotation; the axisymmetric VDE is not. It incorrectly claims rotation *destabilizes* the RWM. It also falsely states the VDE is independent of feedback, which is its primary active control mechanism.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\n**D. RWMs grow on the Alfvén time $\\tau_A$ and are unaffected by wall resistivity, while VDEs grow only on resistive wall times $\\tau_w$; accordingly, $n=1$ dominates when $\\tau_w\\gg \\tau_A$ and $n=0$ dominates when $\\beta_N$ exceeds the no-wall limit but rotation is fast.**\nThis statement incorrectly describes the RWM growth time. Its defining characteristic is that its growth is slow, on the resistive wall time $\\tau_w$, not the Alfvén time $\\tau_A$. An instability that is unaffected by wall resistivity and grows on $\\tau_A$ is an ideal mode, not an RWM. The conclusion is based on this false premise; fast rotation *stabilizes* the $n=1$ RWM.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\n**E. $n=1$ RWMs and $n=0$ VDEs are identical in symmetry and both can be eliminated by fast vertical position feedback ($\\tau_c\\ll \\tau_w$); the wall only affects $n=0$ modes. Consequently, whichever instability has the higher linear growth rate is always suppressed first by the same control coils, independent of $\\beta_N$ or $\\omega_\\phi$.**\nThis statement is incorrect on multiple counts. The modes have different symmetries ($n=1 \\ne n=0$). Vertical position feedback is an $n=0$ system and cannot control an $n=1$ mode. The resistive wall is critical to the physics of both modes. Different control coils are required for instabilities of different symmetries.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**"}]


title: [multiple_choice] Define the sausage instability in a cylindrical pinch as an $m=0$ varicose mode and describe the physical mechanism by which magnetic pressure imbalance amplifies radial bulges and necks.

Question: A long, straight, cylindrical $Z$-pinch of unbounded length consists of a plasma column of equilibrium radius $R_0$ carrying a uniform total axial current $I$ in the $+\hat{\mathbf{z}}$ direction. The surrounding medium is vacuum. The equilibrium magnetic field is purely azimuthal, $\mathbf{B}(r)=B_\theta(r)\,\hat{\boldsymbol{\theta}}$, generated by the plasma current, and the equilibrium force balance satisfies the ideal magnetohydrodynamics (MHD) condition $\nabla p=\mathbf{J}\times\mathbf{B}$ inside the column, together with continuity of total pressure across the plasma–vacuum boundary. Consider an ideal magnetohydrodynamic, axisymmetric boundary perturbation of the form $R(z,t)=R_0+\delta R\cos(k z)\,e^{\gamma t}$ with $\delta R\ll R_0$, so that the column develops alternating bulges and necks along $z$. Assume that on the instability timescale the total current $I$ remains uniform along $z$ and that, to leading order, the perturbation is incompressible in each axial slice so that the internal gas pressure does not instantaneously adjust to cancel boundary total-pressure differences. Starting from the ideal magnetohydrodynamics momentum equation $\rho\,d\mathbf{v}/dt=-\nabla p+\mathbf{J}\times\mathbf{B}$ and the vacuum magnetic field outside the column, reason from these principles to identify which statement correctly defines the sausage instability as an $m=0$ varicose mode and explains the physical mechanism by which magnetic pressure imbalance amplifies radial bulges and necks.

Choose the best option.

A. The sausage instability is the axisymmetric mode with azimuthal mode number $m=0$ in which the column radius modulates along $z$ into bulges and necks. In the ideal limit with predominantly azimuthal field, a local increase of radius at a bulge reduces the surface azimuthal field as $B_\theta(R)\simeq \mu_0 I/(2\pi R)$, so the magnetic pressure $B^2/(2\mu_0)$ at the bulge decreases, weakening inward confinement there while it strengthens at the neck. This magnetic pressure imbalance provides positive feedback that amplifies the varicosity.

B. The sausage instability is a helical bending of the column with $m=1$ in which magnetic pressure increases at bulges and therefore pushes plasma out of bulges into necks, smoothing perturbations and providing a restoring force that stabilizes the varicosity.

C. The sausage instability requires a strong axial field $B_z$ and is driven by magnetic tension along $B_z$ that pulls plasma from bulges into necks even when $B_\theta=0$; the azimuthal field is not essential to the mechanism.

D. In the sausage mode, rapid Ohmic diffusion redistributes the current so that $I$ closely follows the local radius $R(z)$ and keeps $B_\theta$ nearly constant along $z$, preventing any magnetic pressure imbalance; the growth is therefore driven solely by a decrease of gas pressure at the necks.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "The appropriate starting point is the ideal magnetohydrodynamics (MHD) momentum equation,\n$$\n\\rho\\,\\frac{d\\mathbf{v}}{dt}=-\\nabla p+\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B},\n$$\ntogether with equilibrium force balance $\\nabla p_0=\\mathbf{J}_0\\times\\mathbf{B}_0$ and continuity of total pressure across the boundary. For a $Z$-pinch with a purely azimuthal equilibrium field $\\mathbf{B}_0=B_\\theta(r)\\,\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\theta}}$ produced by an axial current density $\\mathbf{J}_0=J_z(r)\\,\\hat{\\mathbf{z}}$, the vacuum field outside the plasma is set by the total enclosed current $I$, namely $B_{\\theta,e}(R)=\\mu_0 I/(2\\pi R)$ at the plasma–vacuum interface of radius $R$. The vacuum magnetic pressure just outside the boundary is thus\n$$\np_{\\text{mag},e}(R)=\\frac{B_{\\theta,e}^2(R)}{2\\mu_0}=\\frac{1}{2\\mu_0}\\left(\\frac{\\mu_0 I}{2\\pi R}\\right)^2\\propto \\frac{1}{R^2}.\n$$\nBecause the perturbation is axisymmetric, it has azimuthal mode number $m=0$: the boundary displacement produces varicose, or sausage-like, alternating bulges and necks along $z$ without helical bending of the axis. Let the perturbed boundary be $R(z,t)=R_0+\\delta R\\cos(k z)\\,e^{\\gamma t}$ with $\\delta R\\ll R_0$. Assuming the total current $I$ remains uniform along $z$ on the instability timescale, the local change of the vacuum magnetic pressure due to a small change in $R$ is obtained by differentiating $p_{\\text{mag},e}(R)\\propto R^{-2}$:\n$$\n\\delta p_{\\text{mag},e}=\\frac{d}{dR}\\left(\\frac{B_{\\theta,e}^2}{2\\mu_0}\\right)\\Bigg|_{R_0}\\delta R\n=-2\\,\\frac{B_{\\theta,e}^2(R_0)}{2\\mu_0}\\,\\frac{\\delta R}{R_0}\n=-\\frac{B_{\\theta,e}^2(R_0)}{\\mu_0}\\,\\frac{\\delta R}{R_0}.\n$$\nHence, at a bulge where $\\delta R>0$, the external magnetic pressure decreases; at a neck where $\\delta R<0$, it increases. In the ideal, nearly incompressible limit, the internal gas pressure $p$ and internal magnetic pressure $B_\\theta^2/(2\\mu_0)$ do not instantaneously adjust to cancel this change. The total pressure just outside the boundary is reduced at a bulge and increased at a neck. Continuity of total pressure across the boundary then implies that, immediately after the perturbation, there is an outward net pressure imbalance at a bulge and an inward imbalance at a neck. The resulting forces amplify the bulge and squeeze the neck. This constitutes a positive feedback that grows the axisymmetric varicosity: the essence of the sausage instability.\n\nA complementary viewpoint is via the work done by magnetic forces: the azimuthal magnetic field lines are circles about the axis and thus do not incur significant bending energy in an $m=0$ varicose deformation; the dominant magnetic response is the change in magnetic pressure with $R$. This contrasts with the $m=1$ kink mode, where magnetic tension associated with bending field lines plays the leading role as a restoring force.\n\nWith this physics established, evaluate the options:\n\nA. This option correctly defines the sausage instability as the $m=0$ axisymmetric varicose mode. It identifies the key magnetic effect: with $I$ approximately uniform along $z$, $B_\\theta(R)\\simeq \\mu_0 I/(2\\pi R)$ decreases at bulges and increases at necks, so $B^2/(2\\mu_0)$ weakens inward confinement at bulges and strengthens it at necks. The resulting magnetic pressure imbalance amplifies the initial perturbation. Verdict — Correct.\n\nB. This describes an $m=1$ helical bending, which is the kink mode, not the sausage mode. Moreover, it asserts that magnetic pressure increases at bulges in a way that smooths varicosity, which is opposite to the actual behavior for a current-driven azimuthal field: at bulges, $B_\\theta$ decreases and magnetic pressure weakens. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nC. This asserts that the instability requires strong axial field $B_z$ and is driven by magnetic tension along $B_z$ even when $B_\\theta=0$. In reality, a pure axial field without azimuthal field does not produce a pinch; magnetic tension along $B_z$ is typically stabilizing for $m=0$, not the driver of varicose growth. The sausage instability in a $Z$-pinch is fundamentally associated with the azimuthal field and its magnetic pressure. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nD. This invokes rapid Ohmic diffusion to keep $B_\\theta$ nearly constant along $z$, eliminating magnetic pressure imbalance. However, ideal magnetohydrodynamic instability evolves on Alfvénic timescales, during which resistive diffusion is negligible in a sufficiently conducting plasma; $I$ does not redistribute quickly enough to flatten $B_\\theta(z)$. Furthermore, even if diffusion were present, it would generally reduce current gradients and tend to be stabilizing rather than provide the stated driving solely by gas pressure changes. Verdict — Incorrect.\n\nTherefore, only option A correctly defines and explains the sausage instability mechanism in the stated setting."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "The problem statement poses a valid and well-defined question in the field of plasma physics, specifically concerning magnetohydrodynamic (MHD) instabilities in a Z-pinch. The setup is a standard, idealized model used to explain the fundamental mechanism of the sausage instability. All premises are scientifically sound and consistent for such a model.\n\nThe task is to identify the statement that correctly defines the sausage instability and its driving mechanism. We will analyze the physics based on the provided principles.\n\nA Z-pinch is characterized by an axial current, given as $I$ in the $+\\hat{\\mathbf{z}}$ direction, which generates a purely azimuthal magnetic field $\\mathbf{B} = B_\\theta(r)\\,\\hat{\\boldsymbol{\\theta}}$. This field confines the plasma. In equilibrium, there is a force balance described by $\\nabla p = \\mathbf{J} \\times \\mathbf{B}$, where $p$ is the plasma pressure and $\\mathbf{J}$ is the current density.\n\nThe problem considers a perturbation to the plasma column's radius of the form $R(z,t) = R_0 + \\delta R \\cos(k z) e^{\\gamma t}$. This perturbation is independent of the azimuthal angle $\\theta$, meaning it is axisymmetric. In the Fourier decomposition of perturbations in cylindrical geometry, $e^{i(m\\theta + kz)}$, axisymmetry corresponds to the azimuthal mode number $m=0$. This type of perturbation deforms the cylinder into a series of bulges (where $R > R_0$) and necks (where $R < R_0$), which is characteristic of the \"sausage\" mode.\n\nThe physical mechanism for the growth of this instability (i.e., $\\gamma > 0$) can be understood by analyzing the forces at a perturbed boundary. According to Ampere's Law, for a long, straight cylindrical current distribution, the external magnetic field at a radius $r$ depends on the total enclosed current $I_{enc}$. The problem states that the total current $I$ remains uniform along $z$. Thus, for any $z$, the magnetic field at the surface of the plasma column, at radius $R(z,t)$, is given by:\n$$ B_\\theta(R) = \\frac{\\mu_0 I}{2\\pi R(z,t)} $$\nThis azimuthal magnetic field exerts an inward pressure on the plasma, the magnetic pressure, given by:\n$$ P_B(R) = \\frac{B_\\theta^2(R)}{2\\mu_0} = \\frac{1}{2\\mu_0} \\left( \\frac{\\mu_0 I}{2\\pi R} \\right)^2 = \\frac{\\mu_0 I^2}{8\\pi^2 R^2} $$\nThis fundamental result shows that the confining magnetic pressure is inversely proportional to the square of the plasma radius, $P_B \\propto R^{-2}$.\n\nNow, let us examine the consequences of the perturbation:\n1.  At a **bulge**, the radius $R$ increases ($R > R_0$). As a result, the magnetic field $B_\\theta(R)$ at the surface decreases, and consequently, the confining magnetic pressure $P_B(R)$ decreases significantly.\n2.  At a **neck**, the radius $R$ decreases ($R < R_0$). As a result, the magnetic field $B_\\theta(R)$ at the surface increases, and the confining magnetic pressure $P_B(R)$ increases significantly.\n\nThe problem states that \"the internal gas pressure does not instantaneously adjust to cancel boundary total-pressure differences\". In equilibrium, the internal pressure $p_{int}(R_0)$ at the boundary is balanced by the external magnetic pressure $P_B(R_0)$. When the perturbation appears, the internal pressure is momentarily out of balance with the new, spatially varying external magnetic pressure.\n\n-   At a bulge, the internal pressure is now higher than the reduced external magnetic pressure ($p_{int} > P_B(R_{bulge})$). This pressure imbalance results in a net outward force, causing the bulge to expand further.\n-   At a neck, the internal pressure is now lower than the increased external magnetic pressure ($p_{int} < P_B(R_{neck})$). This pressure imbalance results in a net inward force, causing the neck to be squeezed further.\n\nThis process describes a positive feedback loop: the initial perturbation creates a pressure imbalance that amplifies the perturbation. The bulges grow, and the necks constrict, leading to the rapid growth of the instability, which can ultimately sever the plasma column. This is the physical mechanism of the sausage instability.\n\nWith this derivation, we evaluate the given options:\n\n**A. The sausage instability is the axisymmetric mode with azimuthal mode number $m=0$ in which the column radius modulates along $z$ into bulges and necks. In the ideal limit with predominantly azimuthal field, a local increase of radius at a bulge reduces the surface azimuthal field as $B_\\theta(R)\\simeq \\mu_0 I/(2\\pi R)$, so the magnetic pressure $B^2/(2\\mu_0)$ at the bulge decreases, weakening inward confinement there while it strengthens at the neck. This magnetic pressure imbalance provides positive feedback that amplifies the varicosity.**\nThis statement is perfectly aligned with our derivation. It correctly identifies the mode as $m=0$ and axisymmetric. It correctly relates the change in radius to the change in magnetic field ($B_\\theta \\propto 1/R$) and magnetic pressure ($P_B \\propto 1/R^2$). It correctly identifies the resulting pressure imbalance as the source of a positive feedback loop that drives the instability.\n**Verdict: Correct**\n\n**B. The sausage instability is a helical bending of the column with $m=1$ in which magnetic pressure increases at bulges and therefore pushes plasma out of bulges into necks, smoothing perturbations and providing a restoring force that stabilizes the varicosity.**\nThis statement is incorrect on multiple counts. The sausage instability is the $m=0$ mode, not the $m=1$ mode (which is the kink instability). Magnetic pressure decreases at bulges, it does not increase. An instability is driven by an amplifying force, not a stabilizing restoring force.\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n**C. The sausage instability requires a strong axial field $B_z$ and is driven by magnetic tension along $B_z$ that pulls plasma from bulges into necks even when $B_\\theta=0$; the azimuthal field is not essential to the mechanism.**\nThis statement is incorrect. The sausage instability is a canonical instability of the Z-pinch, which is defined by its azimuthal field $B_\\theta$ generated by an axial current $J_z$. A strong axial field $B_z$ is known to provide magnetic tension that stabilizes the $m=0$ sausage mode. If $B_\\theta=0$, there is no pinching force, and the concept of a pinch instability is moot.\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n**D. In the sausage mode, rapid Ohmic diffusion redistributes the current so that $I$ closely follows the local radius $R(z)$ and keeps $B_\\theta$ nearly constant along $z$, preventing any magnetic pressure imbalance; the growth is therefore driven solely by a decrease of gas pressure at the necks.**\nThis statement is incorrect. The problem specifies \"ideal magnetohydrodynamics,\" which implies zero electrical resistivity and thus no Ohmic diffusion. The proposed mechanism of keeping $B_\\theta$ constant would be a stabilizing effect, not a driver for instability. Furthermore, compressing a plasma at a neck would naively lead to an increase, not a decrease, in gas pressure.\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\nTherefore, only option A provides a correct and physically sound description of the sausage instability based on the principles of MHD."}]


title: To derive the critical magnetic pitch angle for the onset of the relativistic, non-axisymmetric ($m=1$) kink instability in a force-free magnetic tower launched from a supermassive star

Question: A highly relativistic, cylindrical, force-free magnetic tower is launched from the vicinity of a supermassive star. The plasma within the tower is treated as an ideal magnetohydrodynamic (MHD) fluid moving with a constant bulk velocity $\vec{v} = v\hat{z}$ along the axis of the tower. The corresponding Lorentz factor is $\gamma = (1-v^2/c^2)^{-1/2}$, where $c$ is the speed of light.

In the laboratory frame $S$, the stationary, unperturbed magnetic field in cylindrical coordinates $(r, \phi, z)$ is given by $\vec{B} = B_\phi(r)\hat{\phi} + B_z(r)\hat{z}$. Due to the assumption of ideal MHD, the unperturbed electric field in this frame is given by the condition $\vec{E} = -\vec{v} \times \vec{B}$. The magnetic pitch angle in the lab frame, $\Psi$, is defined by $\tan\Psi = B_\phi/B_z$.

The stability of this structure against the non-axisymmetric ($m=1$) kink instability is determined by analyzing its behavior in the plasma's comoving frame, $S'$. In this frame, the non-relativistic Kruskal-Shafranov criterion for the onset of the fastest-growing kink instability mode is applicable. This criterion states that the instability begins when the azimuthal and axial components of the magnetic field, as measured in the comoving frame ($B'_\phi$ and $B'_z$), become equal.

Given the Lorentz transformations for the components of the magnetic field parallel ($\vec{B}_\parallel$) and perpendicular ($\vec{B}_\perp$) to the relative velocity $\vec{v}$:
$$
\vec{B}'_\parallel = \vec{B}_\parallel
$$
$$
\vec{B}'_\perp = \gamma (\vec{B}_\perp + \frac{\vec{v}}{v^2} \times \vec{E})
$$
Note: The transformation for $\vec{B}'_\perp$ is often written as $\gamma(\vec{B}_\perp - \frac{\vec{v}}{c^2} \times \vec{E})$; the provided form is equivalent for $\vec{E}=-\vec{v}\times\vec{B}$.

Derive an expression for the tangent of the critical magnetic pitch angle in the lab frame, $\tan\Psi_{crit}$, at which the tower becomes unstable to the $m=1$ kink mode. Express your answer in terms of the Lorentz factor $\gamma$.



Answer: [{"answer": "$$ \\boxed{\\gamma} $$", "solution": "The problem requires us to find the critical magnetic pitch angle in the lab frame, $\\Psi_{crit}$, by transforming the physical conditions from the lab frame $S$ to the comoving frame $S'$ and applying the given stability criterion in $S'$.\n\n**1. Determine the Electric Field in the Lab Frame ($S$)**\n\nThe unperturbed electric field $\\vec{E}$ is given by the ideal MHD condition $\\vec{E} = -\\vec{v} \\times \\vec{B}$.\nGiven $\\vec{v} = v\\hat{z}$ and $\\vec{B} = B_\\phi(r)\\hat{\\phi} + B_z(r)\\hat{z}$, we calculate the cross product:\n$$\n\\vec{E} = -(v\\hat{z}) \\times (B_\\phi(r)\\hat{\\phi} + B_z(r)\\hat{z})\n$$\n$$\n\\vec{E} = -v B_\\phi(r) (\\hat{z} \\times \\hat{\\phi}) - v B_z(r) (\\hat{z} \\times \\hat{z})\n$$\nUsing the cylindrical coordinate relation $\\hat{z} \\times \\hat{\\phi} = \\hat{r}$ and $\\hat{z} \\times \\hat{z} = 0$, we get:\n$$\n\\vec{E} = -v B_\\phi(r) \\hat{r}\n$$\nSo, the only non-zero component of the electric field is $E_r = -v B_\\phi$.\n\n**2. Decompose the Magnetic Field into Parallel and Perpendicular Components**\n\nThe relative velocity is $\\vec{v} = v\\hat{z}$. The components of the magnetic field parallel and perpendicular to $\\vec{v}$ are:\n$$\n\\vec{B}_\\parallel = B_z(r)\\hat{z}\n$$\n$$\n\\vec{B}_\\perp = B_\\phi(r)\\hat{\\phi}\n$$\n\n**3. Apply the Lorentz Transformations to find $\\vec{B}'$ in the Comoving Frame ($S'$)**\n\nWe use the provided Lorentz transformation equations for the magnetic field.\n\n*   **Parallel Component:** The component of $\\vec{B}$ parallel to $\\vec{v}$ is unchanged.\n    $$\n    B'_z = B_z\n    $$\n\n*   **Perpendicular Component:** The component of $\\vec{B}$ perpendicular to $\\vec{v}$ transforms as:\n    $$\n    \\vec{B}'_\\perp = \\gamma \\left(\\vec{B}_\\perp + \\frac{\\vec{v}}{v^2} \\times \\vec{E}\\right)\n    $$\n    First, we calculate the cross product term $\\vec{v} \\times \\vec{E}$:\n    $$\n    \\vec{v} \\times \\vec{E} = (v\\hat{z}) \\times (-v B_\\phi \\hat{r}) = -v^2 B_\\phi (\\hat{z} \\times \\hat{r})\n    $$\n    Using the relation $\\hat{z} \\times \\hat{r} = \\hat{\\phi}$, we have:\n    $$\n    \\vec{v} \\times \\vec{E} = -v^2 B_\\phi \\hat{\\phi}\n    $$\n    Now, substitute this into the transformation equation for $\\vec{B}'_\\perp$:\n    $$\n    \\vec{B}'_\\perp = \\gamma \\left( B_\\phi \\hat{\\phi} + \\frac{1}{v^2} (-v^2 B_\\phi \\hat{\\phi}) \\right)\n    $$\n    $$\n    \\vec{B}'_\\perp = \\gamma ( B_\\phi \\hat{\\phi} - B_\\phi \\hat{\\phi} )\n    $$\n    There is a mistake in my calculation. Let's re-check the provided transformation formula. A more standard form is $\\vec{B}'_\\perp = \\gamma(\\vec{B}_\\perp - \\frac{\\vec{v}}{c^2} \\times \\vec{E})$. Let's use that one, as it's more common and robust. The prompt says the given one is equivalent, let's verify.\n    $\\frac{\\vec{v}}{v^2} \\times \\vec{E} = \\frac{\\vec{v}}{v^2} \\times (-\\vec{v} \\times \\vec{B}) = -\\frac{1}{v^2}\\vec{v} \\times (\\vec{v} \\times \\vec{B})$.\n    Using the vector triple product identity $\\vec{A} \\times (\\vec{B} \\times \\vec{C}) = \\vec{B}(\\vec{A} \\cdot \\vec{C}) - \\vec{C}(\\vec{A} \\cdot \\vec{B})$:\n    $-\\frac{1}{v^2}[\\vec{v}(\\vec{v} \\cdot \\vec{B}) - \\vec{B}(\\vec{v} \\cdot \\vec{v})] = -\\frac{1}{v^2}[\\vec{v}(v B_z) - \\vec{B}(v^2)] = -\\frac{B_z}{v}\\vec{v} + \\vec{B}$.\n    Then $\\vec{B}'_\\perp = \\gamma(\\vec{B}_\\perp - \\frac{B_z}{v}\\vec{v} + \\vec{B}) = \\gamma(\\vec{B}_\\perp - B_z\\hat{z} + B_\\phi\\hat{\\phi} + B_z\\hat{z}) = \\gamma(2\\vec{B}_\\perp)$. This doesn't look right.\n    There must be a typo in the provided transformation in the prompt. Let's assume the standard form: $\\vec{B}'_\\perp = \\gamma(\\vec{B}_\\perp - \\frac{\\vec{v}}{c^2} \\times \\vec{E})$.\n\n    Let's proceed with the standard transformation:\n    $$\n    \\frac{\\vec{v}}{c^2} \\times \\vec{E} = \\frac{v\\hat{z}}{c^2} \\times (-v B_\\phi \\hat{r}) = -\\frac{v^2 B_\\phi}{c^2} (\\hat{z} \\times \\hat{r}) = -\\frac{v^2 B_\\phi}{c^2} \\hat{\\phi}\n    $$\n    Now substituting into the perpendicular transformation:\n    $$\n    \\vec{B}'_\\perp = \\gamma \\left( B_\\phi \\hat{\\phi} - \\left(-\\frac{v^2 B_\\phi}{c^2} \\hat{\\phi}\\right) \\right) = \\gamma \\left( B_\\phi \\hat{\\phi} + \\frac{v^2}{c^2} B_\\phi \\hat{\\phi} \\right)\n    $$\n    $$\n    \\vec{B}'_\\perp = \\gamma B_\\phi \\left(1 + \\frac{v^2}{c^2}\\right) \\hat{\\phi}\n    $$\n    This is also incorrect. The calculation of $\\vec{E}$ was $\\vec{E} = -(-v B_\\phi \\hat{r}) = +v B_\\phi \\hat{r}$ because $\\hat{z} \\times \\hat{\\phi} = -\\hat{r}$. Let's re-calculate from Step 1.\n\n    **Step 1 (Corrected): Determine $\\vec{E}$ in Lab Frame**\n    $$\n    \\vec{E} = -(v\\hat{z}) \\times (B_\\phi\\hat{\\phi} + B_z\\hat{z}) = -vB_\\phi(\\hat{z}\\times\\hat{\\phi}) = -vB_\\phi(-\\hat{r}) = vB_\\phi\\hat{r}\n    $$\n    So, $E_r = vB_\\phi$.\n\n    **Step 3 (Corrected): Apply Standard Lorentz Transformation**\n    The transformation for $\\vec{B}'_\\perp$ is $\\vec{B}'_\\perp = \\gamma(\\vec{B}_\\perp - \\frac{\\vec{v}}{c^2} \\times \\vec{E})$.\n    $$\n    \\frac{\\vec{v}}{c^2} \\times \\vec{E} = \\frac{v\\hat{z}}{c^2} \\times (v B_\\phi \\hat{r}) = \\frac{v^2 B_\\phi}{c^2} (\\hat{z} \\times \\hat{r}) = \\frac{v^2 B_\\phi}{c^2} \\hat{\\phi}\n    $$\n    Substitute this into the transformation for $\\vec{B}'_\\perp$:\n    $$\n    \\vec{B}'_\\perp = \\gamma \\left( B_\\phi \\hat{\\phi} - \\frac{v^2 B_\\phi}{c^2} \\hat{\\phi} \\right) = \\gamma B_\\phi \\left(1 - \\frac{v^2}{c^2}\\right) \\hat{\\phi}\n    $$\n    Since $\\gamma = (1 - v^2/c^2)^{-1/2}$, we have $1 - v^2/c^2 = 1/\\gamma^2$.\n    $$\n    \\vec{B}'_\\perp = \\gamma B_\\phi \\left(\\frac{1}{\\gamma^2}\\right) \\hat{\\phi} = \\frac{B_\\phi}{\\gamma} \\hat{\\phi}\n    $$\n    So, the azimuthal component of the magnetic field in the comoving frame is $B'_\\phi = B_\\phi / \\gamma$.\n\n    To summarize the fields in the comoving frame $S'$:\n    $$\n    B'_z = B_z\n    $$\n    $$\n    B'_\\phi = \\frac{B_\\phi}{\\gamma}\n    $$\n\n**4. Apply the Comoving Frame Stability Criterion**\n\nThe problem states that the onset of the kink instability occurs when the axial and azimuthal components of the magnetic field are equal in the comoving frame.\n$$\nB'_\\phi = B'_z\n$$\nSubstituting the expressions we derived:\n$$\n\\frac{B_\\phi}{\\gamma} = B_z\n$$\n\n**5. Solve for the Critical Pitch Angle in the Lab Frame**\n\nWe need to find an expression for $\\tan\\Psi_{crit}$. The lab frame pitch angle $\\Psi$ is defined by $\\tan\\Psi = B_\\phi/B_z$.\nFrom our result in the previous step, we can rearrange the equation to find this ratio at the instability threshold:\n$$\n\\frac{B_\\phi}{B_z} = \\gamma\n$$\nTherefore, the tangent of the critical pitch angle is:\n$$\n\\tan\\Psi_{crit} = \\gamma\n$$\nThis is the final expression for the tangent of the critical lab-frame pitch angle for the onset of the relativistic kink instability."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{\\gamma}$$", "solution": "We start with the Lorentz transformations for the magnetic field components (e.g. Jackson):\n$$\nB'_z = B_z,\n\\qquad\n\\vec B'_\\perp = \\gamma\\Bigl(\\vec B_\\perp - \\frac{1}{c^2}\\,\\vec v\\times\\vec E\\Bigr).\n$$\nHere $\\vec v=v\\hat z$, so $\\vec B_\\perp=B_\\phi\\hat\\phi$ and, by ideal MHD, $\\vec E=-\\vec v\\times\\vec B$.  Hence\n$$\n\\vec v\\times\\vec E\n=-\\vec v\\times(\\vec v\\times\\vec B)\n=-\\bigl[\\vec v(\\vec v\\!\\cdot\\!\\vec B)-\\vec B(\\vec v\\!\\cdot\\!\\vec v)\\bigr]\n=-\\bigl[v\\,B_z\\,\\hat z\\,v - v^2\\,B_\\phi\\,\\hat\\phi - v^2\\,B_z\\,\\hat z\\bigr]\n=v^2\\,B_\\phi\\,\\hat\\phi.\n$$\nThus\n$$\nB'_\\phi\n=\\gamma\\Bigl(B_\\phi-\\frac{v^2}{c^2}B_\\phi\\Bigr)\n=\\gamma B_\\phi(1-\\beta^2)\n=\\frac{B_\\phi}{\\gamma}.\n$$\nThe Kruskal–Shafranov criterion $B'_\\phi=B'_z$ then gives\n$$\n\\frac{B_\\phi}{\\gamma}=B_z\n\\;\\Longrightarrow\\;\n\\frac{B_\\phi}{B_z}=\\gamma\n\\;\\Longrightarrow\\;\n\\tan\\Psi_{\\rm crit}=\\gamma.\n$$"}]


title: Derive the modified Kruskal-Shafranov criterion for the kink instability in a flux rope containing a dense, partially ionized prominence, including the stabilizing effect of ion-neutral drag...

Question: A coronal flux rope, which can trigger a Coronal Mass Ejection (CME), is modeled as a straight cylindrical plasma column of radius $a$ and length $L \gg a$. The flux rope contains a dense, cool prominence plasma that is partially ionized. The plasma consists of ions with mass density $\rho_i$ and neutrals with mass density $\rho_n$. The magnetic field is helical, with a strong axial component $B_z$ and a poloidal component $B_\phi(r)$ generated by a uniform axial current density. The system is susceptible to the ideal $m=1$ external kink mode.

The dynamics of the ions (subscript $i$) and neutrals (subscript $n$) are described by the linearized momentum equations for perturbations $\boldsymbol{\xi}_i$ and $\boldsymbol{\xi}_n$ evolving with a growth rate $\gamma$ (i.e., proportional to $e^{\gamma t}$):
1.  Ion momentum equation: $\gamma^2 \rho_i \boldsymbol{\xi}_i = \boldsymbol{F}_{MHD}(\boldsymbol{\xi}_i) - \gamma \nu_{in} \rho_i (\boldsymbol{\xi}_i - \boldsymbol{\xi}_n)$
2.  Neutral momentum equation: $\gamma^2 \rho_n \boldsymbol{\xi}_n = \gamma \nu_{ni} \rho_n (\boldsymbol{\xi}_i - \boldsymbol{\xi}_n)$

Here, $\boldsymbol{F}_{MHD}(\boldsymbol{\xi}_i)$ is the ideal MHD force operator for the ion fluid, $\nu_{in}$ is the ion-neutral collision frequency, and $\nu_{ni}$ is the neutral-ion collision frequency. Momentum conservation in collisions implies $\rho_i \nu_{in} = \rho_n \nu_{ni}$.

In the absence of neutrals ($\rho_n=0$), the instability is governed by the ideal MHD force alone, and its growth rate $\gamma_0$ is given by:
$$ \gamma_0^2 = \frac{V_{A\phi}^2}{a^2} f(q_a) $$
where $V_{A\phi} = B_\phi(a)/\sqrt{\mu_0 \rho_i}$ is the poloidal Alfven speed at the edge of the rope, and $q_a = \frac{2\pi a B_z}{L B_\phi(a)}$ is the safety factor at the edge. The function $f(q_a) = C(1-q_a/q_c)$ represents the free magnetic energy available to drive the instability, where $C$ is a positive, order-unity constant related to the mode's geometry, and $q_c$ is the critical safety factor from ideal MHD theory (the Kruskal-Shafranov limit).

A successful CME eruption is hypothesized to require the instability growth rate $\gamma$ to exceed a certain threshold $\gamma_{crit}$, which parameterizes other energy loss mechanisms or dynamic thresholds.

Derive the modified Kruskal-Shafranov criterion for the flux rope. That is, find the maximum value of the safety factor at the edge, $q_{mod}$, for which the flux rope is unstable with a growth rate $\gamma \ge \gamma_{crit}$. Express your answer in terms of $q_c$, $C$, $\gamma_{crit}$, the poloidal Alfven time $\tau_{A\phi} = a/V_{A\phi}$, the densities $\rho_i$, $\rho_n$, and the collision frequency $\nu_{in}$.



Answer: [{"answer": "$$ \\boxed{q_c \\left[ 1 - \\frac{\\tau_{A\\phi}^2 \\gamma_{crit}^2}{C} \\left( \\frac{\\gamma_{crit} \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} (\\rho_i + \\rho_n)}{\\gamma_{crit} \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} \\rho_i} \\right) \\right]} $$", "solution": "The goal is to find the critical safety factor $q_{mod}$ below which the instability growth rate $\\gamma$ is greater than or equal to a threshold $\\gamma_{crit}$. We begin by finding the dispersion relation for the system including ion-neutral drag.\n\nFirst, we solve the neutral momentum equation for the neutral displacement $\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_n$ in terms of the ion displacement $\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i$:\n$$ \\gamma^2 \\rho_n \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_n = \\gamma \\nu_{ni} \\rho_n (\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i - \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_n) $$\nDividing by $\\gamma \\rho_n$ (assuming $\\gamma \\neq 0$ and $\\rho_n \\neq 0$):\n$$ \\gamma \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_n = \\nu_{ni} (\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i - \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_n) $$\n$$ (\\gamma + \\nu_{ni}) \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_n = \\nu_{ni} \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i $$\n$$ \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_n = \\frac{\\nu_{ni}}{\\gamma + \\nu_{ni}} \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i $$\n\nNow, substitute this expression for $\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_n$ into the ion momentum equation:\n$$ \\gamma^2 \\rho_i \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i = \\boldsymbol{F}_{MHD}(\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i) - \\gamma \\nu_{in} \\rho_i \\left( \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i - \\frac{\\nu_{ni}}{\\gamma + \\nu_{ni}} \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i \\right) $$\n$$ \\gamma^2 \\rho_i \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i = \\boldsymbol{F}_{MHD}(\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i) - \\gamma \\nu_{in} \\rho_i \\left( 1 - \\frac{\\nu_{ni}}{\\gamma + \\nu_{ni}} \\right) \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i $$\nSimplifying the term in the parentheses:\n$$ 1 - \\frac{\\nu_{ni}}{\\gamma + \\nu_{ni}} = \\frac{\\gamma + \\nu_{ni} - \\nu_{ni}}{\\gamma + \\nu_{ni}} = \\frac{\\gamma}{\\gamma + \\nu_{ni}} $$\nThe ion momentum equation becomes:\n$$ \\gamma^2 \\rho_i \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i = \\boldsymbol{F}_{MHD}(\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i) - \\frac{\\gamma^2 \\nu_{in} \\rho_i}{\\gamma + \\nu_{ni}} \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i $$\nWe can now solve for the ideal MHD force operator $\\boldsymbol{F}_{MHD}(\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i)$:\n$$ \\boldsymbol{F}_{MHD}(\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i) = \\left( \\gamma^2 \\rho_i + \\frac{\\gamma^2 \\nu_{in} \\rho_i}{\\gamma + \\nu_{ni}} \\right) \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i = \\gamma^2 \\rho_i \\left( 1 + \\frac{\\nu_{in}}{\\gamma + \\nu_{ni}} \\right) \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i $$\nUsing the momentum conservation relation $\\rho_n \\nu_{ni} = \\rho_i \\nu_{in}$, we replace $\\nu_{ni} = \\nu_{in} \\rho_i / \\rho_n$:\n$$ \\boldsymbol{F}_{MHD}(\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i) = \\gamma^2 \\rho_i \\left( 1 + \\frac{\\nu_{in}}{\\gamma + \\nu_{in} \\rho_i / \\rho_n} \\right) \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i = \\gamma^2 \\rho_i \\left( 1 + \\frac{\\nu_{in} \\rho_n}{\\gamma \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} \\rho_i} \\right) \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i $$\n$$ \\boldsymbol{F}_{MHD}(\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i) = \\gamma^2 \\rho_i \\left( \\frac{\\gamma \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} \\rho_i + \\nu_{in} \\rho_n}{\\gamma \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} \\rho_i} \\right) \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i $$\n\nFrom the problem statement, in the absence of neutrals, the system dynamics are $\\gamma_0^2 \\rho_i \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i = \\boldsymbol{F}_{MHD}(\\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i)$. Thus we can equate the two expressions for $\\boldsymbol{F}_{MHD}$:\n$$ \\gamma_0^2 \\rho_i \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i = \\gamma^2 \\rho_i \\left( \\frac{\\gamma \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} (\\rho_i + \\rho_n)}{\\gamma \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} \\rho_i} \\right) \\boldsymbol{\\xi}_i $$\nThis gives the dispersion relation connecting the ideal growth rate $\\gamma_0$ with the actual growth rate $\\gamma$:\n$$ \\gamma_0^2 = \\gamma^2 \\left( \\frac{\\gamma \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} (\\rho_i + \\rho_n)}{\\gamma \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} \\rho_i} \\right) $$\n\nThe problem requires finding the condition on the safety factor $q_a$ for the growth rate to be at least $\\gamma_{crit}$. We set $\\gamma = \\gamma_{crit}$ in the dispersion relation. This defines a threshold value for $\\gamma_0^2$:\n$$ \\gamma_0^2 = \\gamma_{crit}^2 \\left( \\frac{\\gamma_{crit} \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} (\\rho_i + \\rho_n)}{\\gamma_{crit} \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} \\rho_i} \\right) $$\nNow, we substitute the given expression for $\\gamma_0^2$ in terms of the safety factor $q_a$:\n$$ \\gamma_0^2 = \\frac{V_{A\\phi}^2}{a^2} C\\left(1 - \\frac{q_a}{q_c}\\right) = \\frac{C}{\\tau_{A\\phi}^2}\\left(1 - \\frac{q_a}{q_c}\\right) $$\nEquating the two expressions for $\\gamma_0^2$ gives the condition on $q_a$ at the threshold $\\gamma=\\gamma_{crit}$. Let's call this value $q_{mod}$:\n$$ \\frac{C}{\\tau_{A\\phi}^2}\\left(1 - \\frac{q_{mod}}{q_c}\\right) = \\gamma_{crit}^2 \\left( \\frac{\\gamma_{crit} \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} (\\rho_i + \\rho_n)}{\\gamma_{crit} \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} \\rho_i} \\right) $$\nFinally, we solve for $q_{mod}$:\n$$ 1 - \\frac{q_{mod}}{q_c} = \\frac{\\tau_{A\\phi}^2 \\gamma_{crit}^2}{C} \\left( \\frac{\\gamma_{crit} \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} (\\rho_i + \\rho_n)}{\\gamma_{crit} \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} \\rho_i} \\right) $$\n$$ \\frac{q_{mod}}{q_c} = 1 - \\frac{\\tau_{A\\phi}^2 \\gamma_{crit}^2}{C} \\left( \\frac{\\gamma_{crit} \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} (\\rho_i + \\rho_n)}{\\gamma_{crit} \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} \\rho_i} \\right) $$\n$$ q_{mod} = q_c \\left[ 1 - \\frac{\\tau_{A\\phi}^2 \\gamma_{crit}^2}{C} \\left( \\frac{\\gamma_{crit} \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} (\\rho_i + \\rho_n)}{\\gamma_{crit} \\rho_n + \\nu_{in} \\rho_i} \\right) \\right] $$\nThe instability grows with $\\gamma \\ge \\gamma_{crit}$ when the safety factor $q_a \\le q_{mod}$. Thus, $q_{mod}$ is the maximum value of the safety factor for the specified instability level."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{q_c\\!\\Bigl[1-\\frac{\\tau_{A\\phi}^2\\gamma_{crit}^2\\bigl(\\gamma_{crit}\\rho_n+\\nu_{in}(\\rho_n+\\rho_i)\\bigr)}{C\\bigl(\\gamma_{crit}\\rho_n+\\nu_{in}\\rho_i\\bigr)}\\Bigr]}$$", "solution": "1. Ion and neutral momentum equations (linearized, with $F_{MHD}$ driving the instability):\n$$\\gamma^2\\rho_i\\,\\xi_i = +\\,\\rho_i\\gamma_0^2\\,\\xi_i - \\gamma\\nu_{in}\\rho_i(\\xi_i-\\xi_n),$$\n$$\\gamma^2\\rho_n\\,\\xi_n = +\\,\\gamma\\nu_{ni}\\rho_n(\\xi_i-\\xi_n),\\qquad\n\\rho_i\\nu_{in}=\\rho_n\\nu_{ni}.$$\n2. Divide each by its density and collect terms:\n$$(\\gamma^2 - \\gamma_0^2 + \\gamma\\nu_{in})\\,\\xi_i - \\gamma\\nu_{in}\\,\\xi_n=0,$$\n$$-\\,\\gamma\\nu_{ni}\\,\\xi_i +(\\gamma^2+\\gamma\\nu_{ni})\\,\\xi_n=0.$$\n3. Require non‐trivial solution $\\det=0$:\n$$(\\gamma^2-\\gamma_0^2+\\gamma\\nu_{in})(\\gamma^2+\\gamma\\nu_{ni})-\\gamma^2\\nu_{in}\\nu_{ni}=0.$$\n4. Expand and divide by $\\gamma$ (discard $\\gamma=0$):\n$$\\gamma^3+(\\nu_{in}+\\nu_{ni})\\gamma^2-\\gamma_0^2\\,\\gamma-\\gamma_0^2\\,\\nu_{ni}=0.$$\n5. At the marginal threshold $\\gamma=\\gamma_{crit}$, set this to zero and solve for $\\gamma_0^2$:\n$$\\gamma_0^2(\\gamma_{crit}+\\nu_{ni})=\\gamma_{crit}^3+(\\nu_{in}+\\nu_{ni})\\gamma_{crit}^2,$$\n$$\\gamma_0^2=\\gamma_{crit}^2\\,\\frac{\\gamma_{crit}+\\nu_{in}+\\nu_{ni}}{\\gamma_{crit}+\\nu_{ni}}.$$\n6. Use $\\gamma_0^2=\\frac{C}{\\tau_{A\\phi}^2}\\bigl(1-\\tfrac{q_{mod}}{q_c}\\bigr)$ and $\\nu_{ni}=\\tfrac{\\rho_i}{\\rho_n}\\nu_{in}$:\n$$\\frac{C}{\\tau_{A\\phi}^2}\\Bigl(1-\\frac{q_{mod}}{q_c}\\Bigr)\n=\\gamma_{crit}^2\\,\\frac{\\gamma_{crit}+\\nu_{in}+\\frac{\\rho_i}{\\rho_n}\\nu_{in}}\n{\\gamma_{crit}+\\frac{\\rho_i}{\\rho_n}\\nu_{in}}.$$\n7. Solve for $q_{mod}$:\n$$1-\\frac{q_{mod}}{q_c}\n=\\frac{\\tau_{A\\phi}^2\\gamma_{crit}^2\n\\bigl(\\gamma_{crit}\\rho_n+\\nu_{in}(\\rho_n+\\rho_i)\\bigr)}\n{C\\bigl(\\gamma_{crit}\\rho_n+\\nu_{in}\\rho_i\\bigr)},$$\n$$q_{mod}\n=q_c\\!\\Biggl[1\n-\\frac{\\tau_{A\\phi}^2\\gamma_{crit}^2\n\\bigl(\\gamma_{crit}\\rho_n+\\nu_{in}(\\rho_n+\\rho_i)\\bigr)}\n{C\\bigl(\\gamma_{crit}\\rho_n+\\nu_{in}\\rho_i\\bigr)}\\Biggr].$$"}]


title: [multiple_choice] Define a vertical displacement event and explain how proximity to vertical stability limits generates precursors observable in vertical position and coil currents.

Question: In an axisymmetric tokamak with an elongated plasma, consider the vertical motion of the plasma column modeled as a rigid current filament with plasma current $I_p$ and vertical coordinate $z(t)$ measured about the magnetic midplane. The plasma is surrounded by a conducting structure (the resistive wall) characterized by a wall time constant $\tau_w$ and is controlled by a set of poloidal field (PF) feedback coils carrying current $i_c(t)$ to maintain $z(t) \approx 0$. The interaction between the plasma and magnetic fields is governed by Maxwell–Faraday’s law $\nabla \times \mathbf{E} = - \partial \mathbf{B}/\partial t$, Ampère’s law $\nabla \times \mathbf{B} = \mu_0 \mathbf{J}$, and the Lorentz force $\mathbf{F} = I_p \, \mathbf{l} \times \mathbf{B}$ on the current filament. The resistive wall supports eddy currents induced by changes in the poloidal flux linked to the wall as the plasma moves, and these currents generate a magnetic field that can transiently restore vertical stability. The PF coil circuit is causal and obeys $L_c \, d i_c/dt + R_c \, i_c = v_c(t)$, with $L_c$ and $R_c$ the coil inductance and resistance and $v_c(t)$ the applied voltage.

Linearizing the magnetic potential energy $U(z)$ near $z=0$ yields an effective vertical magnetic stiffness $k_{\mathrm{eff}}(t)$ such that the vertical magnetic force satisfies $F_z \approx - k_{\mathrm{eff}}(t) \, z + g_c \, i_c$, where $g_c$ is the coupling coefficient between the PF coil current and the vertical force. The resistive wall contribution decays on the time scale $\tau_w$ due to $\mathbf{E}$ fields opposing changes in $\mathbf{B}$ according to $\nabla \times \mathbf{E} = - \partial \mathbf{B}/\partial t$, and the PF coil current responds with finite bandwidth. Assume inertial effects of the plasma column are negligible compared to electromagnetic timescales so that the vertical dynamics can be represented by a first-order linear model for small perturbations,
$$
c_w \, \frac{d z}{d t} = - k_{\mathrm{eff}}(t) \, z + g_c \, i_c + \xi(t),
$$
where $c_w>0$ encapsulates inductive coupling to the wall and $\xi(t)$ represents exogenous disturbances (e.g., noise) acting on the column. The feedback law is proportional, $v_c(t) = -K \, z(t)$, giving $i_c(t)$ a finite response due to $L_c$ and $R_c$.

From first principles and the linearized model, one can reason about the onset of vertical instability and the observable precursors as the system approaches a vertical stability limit, defined informally by $k_{\mathrm{eff}} \to 0^+$ in open loop or by the smallest closed-loop eigenvalue of the linearized system approaching the imaginary axis from the stable side.

Which option correctly defines a Vertical Displacement Event (VDE) and explains, in terms of the above physical laws and linear dynamics, how proximity to the vertical stability limit generates precursors observable in the measured vertical position $z(t)$ and PF coil currents $i_c(t)$?

A. A Vertical Displacement Event (VDE) is an axisymmetric ($n=0$) loss of vertical control in which the plasma column drifts vertically toward the wall due to insufficient net restoring magnetic stiffness, typically after the resistive wall’s transient stabilization decays. Near the vertical stability limit, the smallest closed-loop eigenvalue $\lambda$ of the linearized $(z,i_c)$ system approaches $0^+$, so the low-frequency gain from disturbances $\xi(t)$ to $z(t)$ scales like $1/|\lambda|$, amplifying slow drifts in $z(t)$. The PF coil current $i_c(t)$ must grow coherently to oppose the motion, exhibits increased low-frequency content and a phase lag set by $L_c/R_c$ and $\tau_w$, and often trends toward saturation. These $n=0$ precursors are observed as a slowly growing, quasi-exponential vertical excursion and rising control coil current before the rapid wall strike.

B. A Vertical Displacement Event (VDE) is a non-axisymmetric ($n=1$) kink instability characterized by high-frequency Alfvénic oscillations. Approaching the vertical stability limit suppresses low-frequency motion; precursors appear primarily as high-frequency oscillations in the radial position, while $i_c(t)$ decreases because the controller reduces power demand at marginal stability. Therefore, $z(t)$ shows reduced drift and $i_c(t)$ shows diminished amplitudes near onset.

C. A Vertical Displacement Event (VDE) is defined by a rapid drop in electron temperature due to impurity influx, independent of magnetic forces. Proximity to any stability limit is irrelevant; precursors are detected only in thermal diagnostics, while $z(t)$ and $i_c(t)$ remain flat. Coil currents are not involved in precursor behavior.

D. A Vertical Displacement Event (VDE) is an axisymmetric event, but as the system approaches the vertical stability limit the increased damping from the resistive wall ensures that $z(t)$ fluctuations shrink. The feedback coil current $i_c(t)$ correspondingly falls to zero, indicating enhanced passive stabilization. Hence, precursors appear as decreasing variance in $z(t)$ and declining $i_c(t)$, with no phase lag changes even in the presence of finite $L_c$ and $R_c$.

Select all correct options.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "The definition and precursor explanation must follow from electromagnetic principles and linear stability reasoning for the axisymmetric vertical degree of freedom.\n\nBegin with Maxwell–Faraday’s law $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{E} = - \\partial \\mathbf{B}/\\partial t$ and Ampère’s law $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\mu_0 \\mathbf{J}$. When the plasma column with current $I_p$ is displaced by $z$, the poloidal magnetic flux linking the resistive wall changes. By Faraday’s law, the change in flux induces eddy currents in the wall that generate a magnetic field opposing the change (Lenz’s law). These eddy currents transiently provide a vertical restoring force on the plasma, which can be captured by a magnetic potential energy $U(z)$ with $F_z = - \\partial U / \\partial z \\approx - k_{\\mathrm{eff}}(t) \\, z$ near $z=0$. The PF coil current $i_c$ couples to the plasma through a coefficient $g_c$, adding $F_c = g_c \\, i_c$. Neglecting inertia (appropriate for the slow, resistive-wall-limited axisymmetric motion), a minimal first-order dynamical equation is\n$$\nc_w \\, \\frac{d z}{d t} = - k_{\\mathrm{eff}}(t) \\, z + g_c \\, i_c + \\xi(t),\n$$\nwith $c_w>0$ representing the inductive/reactive coupling to the wall and $\\xi(t)$ disturbances. The PF coil obeys $L_c \\, d i_c/dt + R_c \\, i_c = v_c(t)$, and a proportional controller $v_c(t) = -K \\, z(t)$ yields a closed-loop coupling whereby $i_c(t)$ tracks $z(t)$ with finite bandwidth and phase lag $\\phi(\\omega) = - \\arctan(\\omega L_c / R_c)$. The resistive wall contribution to $k_{\\mathrm{eff}}(t)$ decays on the time scale $\\tau_w$ due to resistive diffusion, reducing passive stabilization.\n\nLinearizing about an equilibrium and considering small perturbations, the coupled $(z,i_c)$ system can be written in state-space form where the dominant closed-loop eigenvalue $\\lambda$ (real, for the axisymmetric case under proportional feedback and wall coupling) governs the slow vertical motion:\n$$\n\\frac{d z}{d t} = \\lambda \\, z + \\alpha \\, \\tilde{i}_c + \\tilde{\\xi}(t), \\quad \\tilde{i}_c \\text{ is the filtered } i_c \\text{ due to } L_c, R_c.\n$$\nHere $\\lambda$ emerges from the balance between destabilizing terms (loss of stiffness due to wall resistivity) and stabilizing terms (static shaping fields and feedback), and $\\alpha$ accounts for coupling. Approaching the vertical stability limit means $\\lambda \\to 0^+$ from the stable side: the restoring coefficient $k_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ becomes too small to decisively counter disturbances. For the stochastic disturbance input $\\tilde{\\xi}(t)$, the low-frequency transfer function from $\\tilde{\\xi}$ to $z$ is\n$$\nH_z(\\omega) \\sim \\frac{1}{j \\omega - \\lambda}.\n$$\nThus, the power spectral density at low frequency scales like\n$$\n|H_z(\\omega)|^2 \\propto \\frac{1}{\\lambda^2 + \\omega^2}.\n$$\nAs $\\lambda \\to 0^+$, the gain at low $\\omega$ increases, amplifying slow drifts in $z(t)$ and producing quasi-exponential growth if deterministic biases are present. Because $i_c(t)$ is driven by $v_c(t) = -K z(t)$ and filtered by the coil circuit, $i_c(t)$ grows coherently with $z(t)$, exhibits increased low-frequency content, and shows a phase lag with respect to $z(t)$ controlled by the coil impedance and wall time constant. In practice, $i_c(t)$ trends toward saturation as the controller attempts to counteract the weak passive stiffness, which is a classic precursor signature. These features are axisymmetric ($n=0$) and are observed ahead of the rapid contact phase of a Vertical Displacement Event.\n\nOption-by-option analysis:\n\nA. This option correctly defines a Vertical Displacement Event (VDE) as an axisymmetric ($n=0$) event: the plasma column drifts vertically toward hardware due to insufficient net restoring magnetic stiffness after resistive wall stabilization decays. The explanation that the smallest closed-loop eigenvalue $\\lambda$ approaches $0^+$ near marginal stability is consistent with the linear model $d z/d t = \\lambda z + \\dots$, and the statement that the low-frequency gain from disturbances scales like $1/|\\lambda|$ is consistent with $H_z(\\omega) \\sim 1/(j \\omega - \\lambda)$. It correctly predicts amplified low-frequency content in $z(t)$, coherent growth in $i_c(t)$ with phase lag stemming from finite $L_c/R_c$ and $\\tau_w$, and trends toward saturation as the controller runs out of authority. The identification of these as $n=0$ precursors is consistent with axisymmetric vertical motion. Verdict: Correct.\n\nB. This option incorrectly defines a VDE as a non-axisymmetric ($n=1$) kink instability and misattributes precursors to high-frequency Alfvénic oscillations. VDEs are axisymmetric ($n=0$) vertical control loss events, not $n=1$ kink modes. The claim that approaching the vertical stability limit suppresses low-frequency motion is opposite to the $1/|\\lambda|$ amplification of low frequencies. The assertion that $i_c(t)$ decreases near marginality contradicts the need for increased control effort as passive stiffness wanes. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nC. This option incorrectly defines a VDE by thermal behavior only, claiming independence from magnetic forces and irrelevance of stability limits. While impurity-driven thermal quenches can occur, a VDE is specifically a magnetic, axisymmetric vertical motion instability, and magnetic control and coil currents are central to its precursors. The statement that $z(t)$ and $i_c(t)$ remain flat before a VDE contradicts observed and modeled $n=0$ precursors. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nD. This option incorrectly asserts that damping from the resistive wall increases near the stability limit, causing $z(t)$ fluctuations and $i_c(t)$ to decrease. In reality, resistive wall eddy currents decay on $\\tau_w$, reducing passive stabilization rather than increasing it. Near marginality, the effective eigenvalue $\\lambda \\to 0^+$ reduces the restoring effect, increasing the amplitude of low-frequency $z(t)$ fluctuations and requiring larger $i_c(t)$, with observable phase lag due to finite $L_c$ and $R_c$. The claim of no phase lag changes is inconsistent with coil and wall dynamics. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nTherefore, only option A is correct."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "The user requires a critical validation of the problem statement, followed by a derivation of the solution and evaluation of the provided options.\n\n### Problem Validation\n\n**Step 1: Extract Givens**\n- **System:** Axisymmetric tokamak, elongated plasma.\n- **Plasma Model:** Rigid current filament with current $I_p$ and vertical coordinate $z(t)$.\n- **Passive Stabilization:** Conducting (resistive) wall with time constant $\\tau_w$.\n- **Active Stabilization:** Poloidal field (PF) feedback coils with current $i_c(t)$.\n- **Governing Principles:** Maxwell–Faraday’s law ($\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{E} = - \\partial \\mathbf{B}/\\partial t$), Ampère’s law ($\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\mu_0 \\mathbf{J}$), Lorentz force ($\\mathbf{F} = I_p \\, \\mathbf{l} \\times \\mathbf{B}$).\n- **Linearized Vertical Force:** $F_z \\approx - k_{\\mathrm{eff}}(t) \\, z + g_c \\, i_c$, with $k_{\\mathrm{eff}}(t)$ as effective vertical magnetic stiffness.\n- **Linearized Vertical Dynamics:** $c_w \\, \\frac{d z}{d t} = - k_{\\mathrm{eff}}(t) \\, z + g_c \\, i_c + \\xi(t)$, with $c_w>0$ (wall coupling) and $\\xi(t)$ (disturbance). Inertial effects are neglected.\n- **PF Coil Circuit:** $L_c \\, d i_c/dt + R_c \\, i_c = v_c(t)$.\n- **Feedback Law:** Proportional control, $v_c(t) = -K \\, z(t)$.\n- **Stability Limit Definition:** Approached as $k_{\\mathrm{eff}} \\to 0^+$ in open loop or as the smallest closed-loop eigenvalue approaches the imaginary axis.\n- **Question:** Define a Vertical Displacement Event (VDE) and explain its precursors in $z(t)$ and $i_c(t)$ based on the provided physics and model.\n\n**Step 2: Validate Using Extracted Givens**\nThe problem statement describes the vertical stability control of an elongated plasma in a tokamak, which is a standard and critical topic in fusion energy science.\n\n- **Scientific Groundedness:** The problem is firmly grounded in magnetohydrodynamics (MHD) and control theory as applied to fusion plasmas.
