A unique conceptual answer can be derived.\n*   **Objective:** The language is technical and free of subjective claims.\n\nThe problem does not exhibit any of the listed flaws (e.g., scientific unsoundness, incompleteness, etc.), provided the minor geometric ambiguity is resolved as discussed.\n\n**Step 3: Verdict and Action**\n\nThe problem is deemed **valid**. We may proceed to the solution.\n\n### Derivation and Analysis\n\nThe problem requires distinguishing between two types of MHD instabilities in a tokamak: the Resistive Wall Mode (RWM) and the Vertical Displacement Event (VDE), and identifying the conditions under which one would dominate.\n\n**1. Fundamental Concepts**\n\n*   **MHD Equilibrium:** The starting point is the static equilibrium force balance $\\mathbf{J}\\times\\mathbf{B}=\\nabla p$. Any perturbation that disrupts this balance can lead to an instability.\n*   **Energy Principle:** An instability can grow if a plasma displacement $\\xi$ leads to a negative change in the system's potential energy, $\\delta W < 0$. $\\delta W$ includes contributions from the plasma volume, the plasma surface, and the surrounding vacuum region.\n*   **Role of a Conducting Wall:**\n    *   An **ideal wall** (infinite conductivity, $\\tau_w \\rightarrow \\infty$) acts as a perfect flux conserver. The boundary condition is that the normal component of the perturbed magnetic field, $\\delta B_n$, is zero at the wall. This provides strong stabilization for external modes.\n    *   A **resistive wall** (finite $\\tau_w$) allows magnetic flux to penetrate on the timescale $\\tau_w$. It acts like an ideal wall for fast perturbations (growth rate $\\gamma \\gg 1/\\tau_w$) and provides no stabilization for very slow perturbations ($\\gamma \\ll 1/\\tau_w$). For modes with growth rates on the order of the wall time ($\\gamma \\sim 1/\\tau_w$), the wall slows but does not prevent instability.\n\n**2. Analysis of the $n=1$ Resistive Wall Mode (RWM)**\n\n*   **Symmetry and Driver:** The RWM is a non-axisymmetric instability, typically with toroidal mode number $n=1$. It is fundamentally an external kink mode, driven by plasma current and pressure gradients, especially at high $\\beta_N$.\n*   **Operating Regime and Growth:** The problem specifies the operational space as $\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{nw}} < \\beta_N < \\beta_{N,\\mathrm{ww}}$.\n    *   $\\beta_N > \\beta_{N,\\mathrm{nw}}$ means there is sufficient free energy from the pressure gradient and current density to drive an external kink mode if there were no wall. This corresponds to $\\delta W < 0$ in the absence of a wall.\n    *   $\\beta_N < \\beta_{N,\\mathrm{ww}}$ means an ideal wall would stabilize this mode.\n    *   With a *resistive* wall, the mode is no longer fully stabilized. The wall's resistance allows the stabilizing eddy currents to decay, permitting the external field perturbation to grow. The growth is limited by the rate at which magnetic flux can penetrate the wall. Consequently, the ideal kink mode, which would grow on the fast Alfvén timescale ($\\tau_A$), is converted into a slowly growing RWM with a growth rate $\\gamma_{RWM} \\sim 1/\\tau_w$.\n*   **Stabilization by Rotation:** A non-axisymmetric mode structure ($n=1$) rotates with the plasma at frequency $\\omega_\\phi$. From the perspective of the stationary wall, the mode's magnetic field oscillates. If the oscillation is fast enough, specifically $\\omega_\\phi \\gg 1/\\tau_w$, the resistive wall does not have time to respond and acts like an ideal wall, thus stabilizing the mode. Instability occurs when the rotation is insufficient, i.e., when $\\omega_\\phi \\tau_w \\lesssim O(1)$, allowing the mode to \"lock\" to the wall and grow. The problem states $\\omega_\\phi \\tau_w \\sim O(1)$, placing the plasma near the threshold for rotational stabilization.\n\n**3. Analysis of the $n=0$ Vertical Displacement Event (VDE)**\n\n*   **Symmetry and Driver:** The VDE is an axisymmetric instability ($n=0$). It is not driven by $\\beta_N$ in the same way as a kink mode. Instead, it is a positional instability inherent to plasmas with non-circular, vertically elongated cross-sections ($\\kappa>1$). The external magnetic fields required to create the elongation also create a configuration where a small vertical displacement moves the plasma into a region with a weaker restoring vertical field (or a stronger destabilizing field), leading to an unstable positive feedback loop. This is an instability of the plasma's rigid vertical position.\n*   **Growth Rate and Stabilization:**\n    *   The raw instability grows on the very fast poloidal Alfvén timescale. However, any passive conducting structures, such as the resistive wall at radius $b$, provide passive stabilization. As the plasma moves, it induces eddy currents in the wall that create a restoring force. This slows the growth rate down to the wall's resistive time constant, i.e., $\\gamma_{VDE} \\sim 1/\\tau_w$.\n    *   Since the instability is now slow, it can be controlled by an active feedback system. This system measures the plasma's vertical position and uses a set of axisymmetric ($n=0$) control coils to apply a corrective magnetic field. For stabilization to be successful, the feedback system's characteristic response time $\\tau_c$ must be much shorter than the instability growth time: $\\tau_c \\ll \\tau_w$. If the feedback system is too slow, has insufficient gain, or limited bandwidth ($\\tau_c \\gtrsim \\tau_w$), it will fail to control the plasma's position, resulting in a VDE where the plasma drifts vertically until it collides with the wall.\n*   **Role of Rotation:** Since the VDE is an axisymmetric ($n=0$) motion, it is symmetric with respect to toroidal angle $\\phi$. Therefore, toroidal rotation $\\omega_\\phi$ has no direct stabilizing or destabilizing effect on it.\n\n**4. Conclusion on Dominant Instabilities**\n\n*   An **$n=1$ RWM will dominate** if the plasma operates in the window $\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{nw}} < \\beta_N < \\beta_{N,\\mathrm{ww}}$ AND plasma rotation is insufficient for stabilization ($\\omega_\\phi \\tau_w \\lesssim 1$). This is independent of the vertical position control system.\n*   An **$n=0$ VDE will dominate** if the plasma is sufficiently elongated ($\\kappa > 1$) AND the vertical position feedback is inadequate ($\\tau_c \\gtrsim \\tau_w$). This is largely independent of $\\beta_N$ and $\\omega_\\phi$.\n\n### Option-by-Option Analysis\n\n**A. A resistive wall mode is a non-axisymmetric ($n=1$) external kink destabilized when $\\beta_N$ exceeds the no-wall limit and plasma rotation is insufficient relative to the wall diffusion ($\\omega_\\phi \\lesssim 1/\\tau_w$), so its growth is limited by $\\tau_w$. A vertical displacement event is an axisymmetric ($n=0$) motion driven by weak vertical restoring force at high $\\kappa$, and it dominates when the vertical control bandwidth is inadequate ($\\tau_c \\gtrsim$ the inverse vertical growth rate), even if $\\beta_N$ is below the with-wall kink limit. Thus $n=0$ dominates with high elongation and poor position control, while $n=1$ dominates near the kink threshold with low rotation, regardless of vertical feedback quality.**\nThis statement accurately defines both the RWM and VDE. It correctly identifies the toroidal mode numbers ($n=1$ for RWM, $n=0$ for VDE), the physical drivers ($\\beta_N > \\beta_{N,\\mathrm{nw}}$ for RWM, high $\\kappa$ for VDE), the instability timescales ($\\sim \\tau_w$ for both), and the distinct stabilization mechanisms (rotation for RWM, feedback for VDE). The summary of dominance conditions is also correct.\n**Verdict: Correct.**\n\n**B. A vertical displacement event is a non-axisymmetric ($n=1$) pressure-driven kink that occurs only when $\\beta_N>\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{ww}}$, whereas a resistive wall mode is axisymmetric ($n=0$) and is triggered by high elongation but can be completely prevented by a perfectly conducting wall; therefore, $n=1$ dominates at low elongation and strong feedback, and $n=0$ dominates at high rotation.**\nThis statement incorrectly swaps the mode numbers of VDEs and RWMs. VDEs are $n=0$, RWMs are $n=1$. It also incorrectly states VDEs occur for $\\beta_N>\\beta_{N,\\mathrm{ww}}$, which describes an ideal instability, not a VDE. It falsely claims RWMs are triggered by elongation. The conclusion is based on these incorrect premises.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\n**C. Both RWMs and VDEs are primarily stabilized by increasing plasma rotation; in particular, raising $\\omega_\\phi$ above $1/\\tau_w$ destabilizes the RWM by decoupling the plasma from the wall, whereas the VDE is independent of feedback bandwidth and is controlled only by $\\beta_N$. Therefore, $n=1$ dominates at high rotation and $n=0$ dominates at low $\\beta_N$.**\nThis statement is fundamentally flawed. Only the non-axisymmetric RWM is stabilized by rotation; the axisymmetric VDE is not. It incorrectly claims rotation *destabilizes* the RWM. It also falsely states the VDE is independent of feedback, which is its primary active control mechanism.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\n**D. RWMs grow on the Alfvén time $\\tau_A$ and are unaffected by wall resistivity, while VDEs grow only on resistive wall times $\\tau_w$; accordingly, $n=1$ dominates when $\\tau_w\\gg \\tau_A$ and $n=0$ dominates when $\\beta_N$ exceeds the no-wall limit but rotation is fast.**\nThis statement incorrectly describes the RWM growth time. Its defining characteristic is that its growth is slow, on the resistive wall time $\\tau_w$, not the Alfvén time $\\tau_A$. An instability that is unaffected by wall resistivity and grows on $\\tau_A$ is an ideal mode, not an RWM. The conclusion is based on this false premise; fast rotation *stabilizes* the $n=1$ RWM.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\n**E. $n=1$ RWMs and $n=0$ VDEs are identical in symmetry and both can be eliminated by fast vertical position feedback ($\\tau_c\\ll \\tau_w$); the wall only affects $n=0$ modes. Consequently, whichever instability has the higher linear growth rate is always suppressed first by the same control coils, independent of $\\beta_N$ or $\\omega_\\phi$.**\nThis statement is incorrect on multiple counts. The modes have different symmetries ($n=1 \\ne n=0$). Vertical position feedback is an $n=0$ system and cannot control an $n=1$ mode. The resistive wall is critical to the physics of both modes. Different control coils are required for instabilities of different symmetries.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**"}]


title: Deriving the stabilizing torque on a resistive wall mode from the ponderomotive force of a rotating, externally applied RF magnetic field...

Question: A simplified model for the stabilizing torque on a resistive wall mode (RWM) in a cylindrical plasma considers the ponderomotive force from an externally applied rotating Radio-Frequency (RF) magnetic field. Consider a large aspect-ratio toroidal plasma modeled as a straight cylinder of radius $a$. A magnetohydrodynamic (MHD) instability, the RWM, is represented as a static, helical, electrically resistive pathway for current on the plasma surface at $r=a$. This pathway has a poloidal mode number $m$ and a uniform effective surface resistivity $\eta_s$.

An external antenna system generates a rotating RF magnetic field. At the plasma surface $r=a$, the radial component of this field is given by:
$$B_r(a, \theta, t) = B_0 \cos(m\theta - \omega t)$$
where $B_0$ is the field amplitude, $\theta$ is the poloidal angle, and $\omega$ is the RF antenna's angular frequency.

Due to the time-varying nature of the RF field, an axial electric field $E_z$ is induced at the plasma surface. For a wave with poloidal mode number $m$, this induced field is related to the radial magnetic field by the long-wavelength approximation of Faraday's law in cylindrical coordinates:
$$E_z = \frac{a\omega}{m} B_r$$
This axial electric field drives an axial surface current density, $K_z$, within the resistive pathway representing the RWM. The plasma effectively provides a return path, so this current flows primarily along the z-axis.

The surface current $K_z$ interacts with the radial RF magnetic field $B_r$ via the Lorentz force, producing a poloidal force density $f_\theta$ on the surface. The time-average of this force density results in a net torque that can synchronize the RWM with the RF field, thereby providing a stabilizing effect.

Your task is to derive an expression for the time-averaged torque per unit axial length, $\mathcal{T}$, exerted on the plasma cylinder.



Answer: [{"answer": "$$ \\boxed{\\frac{\\pi a^3 \\omega B_0^2}{m \\eta_s}} $$", "solution": "The problem asks for the time-averaged torque per unit axial length, $\\mathcal{T}$, on a cylindrical plasma due to an external rotating RF magnetic field interacting with a static resistive wall mode (RWM).\n\n**Step 1: Determine the induced axial electric field**\n\nThe problem provides the radial component of the RF magnetic field at the plasma surface $r=a$:\n$$B_r(a, \\theta, t) = B_0 \\cos(m\\theta - \\omega t)$$\nIt also provides the relationship between the induced axial electric field $E_z$ and $B_r$:\n$$E_z = \\frac{a\\omega}{m} B_r$$\nSubstituting the expression for $B_r$, we get:\n$$E_z(a, \\theta, t) = \\frac{a\\omega B_0}{m} \\cos(m\\theta - \\omega t)$$\n\n**Step 2: Calculate the induced surface current density**\n\nThe RWM is modeled as a resistive path on the plasma surface with an effective surface resistivity $\\eta_s$. The induced electric field $E_z$ drives an axial surface current density $K_z$ according to the surface version of Ohm's law:\n$$K_z = \\frac{E_z}{\\eta_s}$$\nSubstituting the expression for $E_z$:\n$$K_z(a, \\theta, t) = \\frac{a\\omega B_0}{m \\eta_s} \\cos(m\\theta - \\omega t)$$\n\n**Step 3: Calculate the poloidal Lorentz force density**\n\nThe surface current $K_z$ flows in the axial direction ($\\hat{z}$) in the presence of the RF magnetic field, which has a radial component $B_r$. The interaction produces a Lorentz force. The force per unit area (force density) on the surface is given by $\\vec{f} = \\vec{K} \\times \\vec{B}$. Here, $\\vec{K} = K_z \\hat{z}$ and we are interested in the force from the radial component of the magnetic field $\\vec{B} = B_r \\hat{r}$. The poloidal component of the force density, $f_\\theta$, is:\n$$f_\\theta = (\\vec{K} \\times \\vec{B})_\\theta = (K_z \\hat{z} \\times B_r \\hat{r})_\\theta$$\nUsing the cross product in cylindrical coordinates, $\\hat{z} \\times \\hat{r} = \\hat{\\theta}$, we get:\n$$f_\\theta = K_z B_r$$\nSubstituting the expressions for $K_z$ and $B_r$:\n$$f_\\theta(\\theta, t) = \\left( \\frac{a\\omega B_0}{m \\eta_s} \\cos(m\\theta - \\omega t) \\right) \\left( B_0 \\cos(m\\theta - \\omega t) \\right)$$\n$$f_\\theta(\\theta, t) = \\frac{a\\omega B_0^2}{m \\eta_s} \\cos^2(m\\theta - \\omega t)$$\n\n**Step 4: Time-average the poloidal force density**\n\nTo find the steady torque, we need to time-average the force density $\\langle f_\\theta \\rangle$. The average is over one or more RF periods $T = 2\\pi/\\omega$.\n$$\\langle f_\\theta \\rangle = \\frac{1}{T} \\int_0^T f_\\theta(\\theta, t) dt = \\frac{a\\omega B_0^2}{m \\eta_s} \\frac{1}{T} \\int_0^T \\cos^2(m\\theta - \\omega t) dt$$\nThe time average of $\\cos^2(x)$ is $\\frac{1}{2}$.\n$$\\langle \\cos^2(m\\theta - \\omega t) \\rangle = \\frac{1}{2}$$\nTherefore, the time-averaged poloidal force density is:\n$$\\langle f_\\theta \\rangle = \\frac{a\\omega B_0^2}{2 m \\eta_s}$$\nNote that this average force density is uniform in $\\theta$.\n\n**Step 5: Calculate the total torque per unit length**\n\nThe torque is the moment of the force. For a force density $f_\\theta$ acting on a surface element $dA = a \\, d\\theta \\, dz$, the differential torque is $d\\mathcal{T} = r f_\\theta dA = a f_\\theta (a \\, d\\theta \\, dz)$. The torque per unit axial length is obtained by integrating over the poloidal angle $\\theta$:\n$$\\mathcal{T} = \\int_0^{2\\pi} a \\langle f_\\theta \\rangle (a \\, d\\theta) = a^2 \\int_0^{2\\pi} \\langle f_\\theta \\rangle d\\theta$$\nSubstituting the expression for $\\langle f_\\theta \\rangle$:\n$$\\mathcal{T} = a^2 \\int_0^{2\\pi} \\left( \\frac{a\\omega B_0^2}{2 m \\eta_s} \\right) d\\theta$$\nSince the integrand is constant with respect to $\\theta$, the integral is straightforward:\n$$\\mathcal{T} = a^2 \\left( \\frac{a\\omega B_0^2}{2 m \\eta_s} \\right) \\int_0^{2\\pi} d\\theta = a^2 \\left( \\frac{a\\omega B_0^2}{2 m \\eta_s} \\right) (2\\pi)$$\nSimplifying the expression gives the final result:\n$$\\mathcal{T} = \\frac{\\pi a^3 \\omega B_0^2}{m \\eta_s}$$"}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{\\frac{\\pi a^3\\omega B_0^2}{m\\eta_s}}$$", "solution": "State relevant equations:\n- Faraday’s law (long‐wavelength):\n$$E_z=\\frac{a\\omega}{m}B_r$$\n- Ohm’s law on the resistive surface:\n$$K_z=\\frac{E_z}{\\eta_s}$$\n- Lorentz force density:\n$$f_\\theta=K_z\\,B_r$$\n- Torque per unit length:\n$$\\mathcal T=\\int_0^{2\\pi}(r\\,f_\\theta)\\,r\\,d\\theta\\quad(r=a)$$\n\nDerivation:\n1. RF field:\n$$B_r(a,\\theta,t)=B_0\\cos(m\\theta-\\omega t)$$\n2. Induced axial electric field:\n$$E_z=\\frac{a\\omega}{m}B_0\\cos(m\\theta-\\omega t)$$\n3. Surface current density:\n$$K_z=\\frac{E_z}{\\eta_s}=\\frac{a\\omega}{m\\eta_s}B_0\\cos(m\\theta-\\omega t)$$\n4. Instantaneous force density:\n$$f_\\theta=K_z\\,B_r=\\frac{a\\omega}{m\\eta_s}B_0^2\\cos^2(m\\theta-\\omega t)$$\n5. Time‐average over one RF period:\n$$\\langle f_\\theta\\rangle=\\frac{a\\omega}{m\\eta_s}B_0^2\\frac12$$\n6. Integrate around the cylinder:\n$$\\mathcal T\n=a^2\\int_0^{2\\pi}\\langle f_\\theta\\rangle\\,d\\theta\n=2\\pi a^2\\frac{a\\omega}{m\\eta_s}B_0^2\\frac12\n=\\frac{\\pi a^3\\omega B_0^2}{m\\eta_s}$$"}]


title: If the moment coefficient about the aerodynamic center is constant and negative, what does this imply about the airfoil's inherent pitching moment and static stability?

Question: An aerospace engineering team is conducting a series of low-speed wind tunnel tests on a novel airfoil section intended for a small Unmanned Aerial Vehicle (UAV). During the analysis of the collected data, the team makes a crucial observation: for the entire range of non-stalled angles of attack, the moment coefficient measured about the airfoil's Aerodynamic Center (AC), denoted as $C_{m, ac}$, is a constant negative value. For an aircraft to be flyable, its overall longitudinal static stability is determined by the moment characteristics about its Center of Gravity (CG). Based *solely* on the experimental finding that $C_{m, ac}$ is a constant negative value, what is the most accurate conclusion that can be drawn regarding the airfoil's geometry and its role in the aircraft's static stability?

A. The airfoil has negative camber, and it is inherently statically stable regardless of the CG location.
B. The airfoil must be symmetric, and as a result, it generates no pitching moment.
C. The airfoil has positive camber, and its contribution to the aircraft's static stability depends on the location of the CG relative to the AC.
D. The airfoil has positive camber, and it is inherently statically unstable regardless of the CG location.
E. The Center of Pressure (CP) and the Aerodynamic Center (AC) are located at the same point for all angles of attack.



Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{C}$$", "solution": "The problem asks for the correct conclusion based on the experimental observation that the moment coefficient about the aerodynamic center, $C_{m, ac}$, is a constant and negative value. We need to analyze what this implies for the airfoil's geometry (camber) and its effect on longitudinal static stability.\n\nStep 1: Analyze the implication of a non-zero $C_{m, ac}$ on airfoil geometry.\nThe moment coefficient about the aerodynamic center, $C_{m, ac}$, represents the inherent pitching moment of the airfoil section. This moment arises from the pressure distribution over the airfoil's surface.\nFor a symmetric airfoil, the geometry is identical above and below the chord line. At any angle of attack, the pressure distribution creates a lift force, but due to the symmetry, there is no net pitching moment about the quarter-chord point (which is the approximate location of the AC in subsonic flow). Therefore, for a symmetric airfoil, $C_{m, ac} = 0$.\nFor a cambered airfoil, the curvature of the mean camber line results in an asymmetric pressure distribution even at zero lift conditions.\nA positively cambered airfoil (where the mean camber line is above the chord line) naturally produces a pressure distribution that results in a nose-down pitching moment. By convention in aerodynamics, a nose-down moment is negative. Thus, for a positively cambered airfoil, $C_{m, ac} < 0$.\nA negatively cambered airfoil (where the mean camber line is below the chord line, or has a reflexed trailing edge) produces a nose-up pitching moment, so $C_{m, ac} > 0$.\nThe problem states that $C_{m, ac}$ is a constant *negative* value. This directly implies that the airfoil is not symmetric and specifically that it has positive camber. This eliminates option B and contradicts the first part of option A.\n\nStep 2: Analyze the implication of the given information on longitudinal static stability.\nLongitudinal static stability for an aircraft requires that if it is disturbed from its trim angle of attack (e.g., nose pitches up, increasing the angle of attack $\\alpha$), a restoring moment (nose-down) must be generated. This restoring moment acts about the aircraft's Center of Gravity (CG).\nMathematically, the condition for static stability is that the rate of change of the moment coefficient about the CG with respect to the angle of attack must be negative:\n$$ \\frac{dC_{m, CG}}{d\\alpha} < 0 $$\nThe moment coefficient about the CG, $C_{m, CG}$, can be related to the moment coefficient about the AC, $C_{m, AC}$, using the following relationship, which is derived by transferring the moment from the AC to the CG:\n$$ C_{m, CG} = C_{m, AC} + C_L \\frac{(x_{CG} - x_{AC})}{c} $$\nHere, $C_L$ is the lift coefficient, $c$ is the airfoil chord length, and $x_{CG}$ and $x_{AC}$ are the positions of the CG and AC along the chord line, respectively.\n\nTo find the stability derivative, we differentiate this equation with respect to the angle of attack, $\\alpha$:\n$$ \\frac{dC_{m, CG}}{d\\alpha} = \\frac{dC_{m, AC}}{d\\alpha} + \\frac{dC_L}{d\\alpha} \\frac{(x_{CG} - x_{AC})}{c} $$\nThe problem states that $C_{m, ac}$ is constant. By the very definition of the aerodynamic center, the moment coefficient about it does not change with the angle of attack. Therefore, $\\frac{dC_{m, AC}}{d\\alpha} = 0$.\nThe equation simplifies to:\n$$ \\frac{dC_{m, CG}}{d\\alpha} = \\frac{dC_L}{d\\alpha} \\frac{(x_{CG} - x_{AC})}{c} $$\nFor a conventional airfoil operating below its stall angle, the lift coefficient increases with the angle of attack, meaning the lift curve slope, $\\frac{dC_L}{d\\alpha}$, is positive.\nTherefore, for the stability condition $\\frac{dC_{m, CG}}{d\\alpha} < 0$ to be satisfied, the term $(x_{CG} - x_{AC})$ must be negative.\n$$ x_{CG} - x_{AC} < 0 \\implies x_{CG} < x_{AC} $$\nThis means that for the aircraft to be statically stable, its Center of Gravity must be located *ahead* of the Aerodynamic Center. The value of $C_{m, ac}$ (being negative, positive, or zero) does not determine stability on its own. It only sets the baseline pitching moment. Stability is determined by the relative positions of the CG and the AC.\nTherefore, the airfoil is not \"inherently\" stable or unstable; its contribution to the aircraft's stability is conditional on the CG placement. This refutes the claims in options A and D that stability is inherent.\n\nStep 3: Evaluate the options based on the analysis.\n- A. The airfoil has negative camber, and it is inherently statically stable regardless of the CG location. (Incorrect. It has positive camber, and stability depends on CG location.)\n- B. The airfoil must be symmetric, and as a result, it generates no pitching moment. (Incorrect. $C_{m, ac}$ is non-zero.)\n- C. The airfoil has positive camber, and its contribution to the aircraft's static stability depends on the location of the CG relative to the AC. (Correct. This aligns perfectly with our analysis in both Step 1 and Step 2.)\n- D. The airfoil has positive camber, and it is inherently statically unstable regardless of the CG location. (Incorrect. Stability is conditional on CG location.)\n- E. The Center of Pressure (CP) and the Aerodynamic Center (AC) are located at the same point for all angles of attack. (Incorrect. The CP is the point where the resultant aerodynamic force acts, producing zero moment. Its position on the chord changes with the angle of attack. The AC is the point where the moment is constant with angle of attack. These two points are generally not the same, except perhaps at one specific angle of attack.)\n\nThe most accurate conclusion is that the airfoil has positive camber, and a stable aircraft configuration can be achieved if the CG is placed forward of the AC. Thus, the airfoil's contribution to stability is conditional."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{C}$$", "solution": "Given a two-dimensional airfoil in low-speed flow over a non-stalled range of angles of attack, the aerodynamic center (AC) is defined as the point about which the aerodynamic moment is independent of angle of attack. Hence, by definition,\n$$\n\\frac{d C_{m,ac}}{d \\alpha} = 0.\n$$\nThe experimental observation states that $C_{m,ac}$ is a constant negative value, i.e.,\n$$\nC_{m,ac} = \\text{constant} < 0.\n$$\nFrom thin airfoil theory and standard airfoil aerodynamics, the sign of $C_{m,ac}$ is governed by camber:\n- For a symmetric airfoil (zero camber), $C_{m,ac} = 0$.\n- For a positively cambered airfoil, $C_{m,ac} < 0$ (nose-down moment).\n- For a negatively cambered (reflexed) airfoil, $C_{m,ac} > 0$ (nose-up moment).\nTherefore, the finding $C_{m,ac} < 0$ implies the airfoil is cambered with positive camber and is not symmetric.\n\nTo assess longitudinal static stability about the center of gravity (CG), use the standard relation for the moment coefficient about the CG:\n$$\nC_{m,cg} = C_{m,ac} + C_{l}\\left(\\frac{x_{ac} - x_{cg}}{c}\\right),\n$$\nwhere $x_{ac}$ and $x_{cg}$ are measured from the same reference (e.g., the leading edge), $c$ is the chord, and $C_{l}$ is the lift coefficient. Over the non-stalled range,\n$$\nC_{l} = a\\left(\\alpha - \\alpha_{0L}\\right),\n$$\nwith $a > 0$ and $\\alpha_{0L}$ the zero-lift angle. Differentiating $C_{m,cg}$ with respect to $\\alpha$ yields the static stability slope:\n$$\n\\frac{d C_{m,cg}}{d \\alpha} = \\frac{d C_{l}}{d \\alpha}\\left(\\frac{x_{ac} - x_{cg}}{c}\\right) = a\\left(\\frac{x_{ac} - x_{cg}}{c}\\right).\n$$\nStatic stability requires\n$$\n\\frac{d C_{m,cg}}{d \\alpha} < 0 \\quad \\Longrightarrow \\quad x_{cg} < x_{ac}.\n$$\nThus, the aircraft’s static stability depends on the relative location of the CG to the AC and not on the sign or magnitude of $C_{m,ac}$. The constant negative $C_{m,ac}$ affects trim (a constant nose-down bias) but does not determine the stability slope; the latter is governed by $\\left(x_{ac} - x_{cg}\\right)$.\n\nEvaluating the options:\n- A is incorrect: the observation implies positive camber, and stability is not independent of CG location.\n- B is incorrect: symmetric airfoils have $C_{m,ac} = 0$, not a negative constant.\n- C is correct: the airfoil has positive camber (consistent with $C_{m,ac} < 0$), and its contribution to stability depends on $x_{cg}$ relative to $x_{ac}$ via the slope $a\\left(\\frac{x_{ac} - x_{cg}}{c}\\right)$.\n- D is incorrect: stability is not inherently unstable; it depends on CG location.\n- E is incorrect: the center of pressure generally moves with $\\alpha$ and does not coincide with the AC except in special cases; $C_{m,ac} \\neq 0$ specifically implies CP is not at AC.\n\nTherefore, the most accurate conclusion is option C."}]</wiki_content5.2>

<wiki_content5.3>title: [multiple_choice] Define resistive tearing modes in RFPs and describe their role in driving enhanced transport and relaxation oscillations in the plasma.

Question: A cylindrical Reversed Field Pinch (RFP) is modeled by single-fluid resistive magnetohydrodynamics (MHD), with uniform resistivity and low plasma beta. The governing electromagnetic laws are the induction equation and Ohm’s law, together with Ampère’s law and Faraday’s law. Consider small helical perturbations of the form $\exp(i m \theta + i n z / R - i \omega t)$ in a cylindrical equilibrium, with safety factor $q(r)$ and multiple rational surfaces. From this first-principles base, determine which statements correctly define resistive tearing modes in RFPs and describe their role in driving enhanced transport and relaxation oscillations.

Select all that apply:

A. In resistive MHD, a tearing mode in an RFP reconnects magnetic field lines at rational surfaces where the tearing stability index satisfies $\Delta' &lt; 0$, and its linear growth rate increases with the Lundquist number $S$ as $\gamma \propto S^{3/5}$. The resulting magnetic topology reduces radial transport by eliminating stochasticity.

B. In resistive MHD with uniform resistivity $\eta$, the inner resistive layer of a classical tearing mode satisfies a scaling $\delta/a \sim S^{-2/5} (k a)^{-1/5}$ and a growth rate scaling $\gamma \tau_{A} \sim \Delta'^{4/5} S^{-3/5} (k a)^{2/5}$ (the Furth–Killeen–Rosenbluth regime), where $k$ is the parallel wavenumber, $\tau_{A}$ is the Alfvén time, and $\Delta'$ is the outer-region matching parameter. In RFPs, many resonant $m=1$ modes can overlap, producing stochastic magnetic fields that enhance radial transport, and a fluctuation-induced mean electromotive force $\langle \tilde{\mathbf{v}} \times \tilde{\mathbf{B}} \rangle_{\parallel}$ that participates in sustaining field reversal and relaxation oscillations.

C. The quasi-steady relaxed state achieved after repeated tearing-driven relaxation in an RFP can be modeled by minimizing magnetic energy at fixed magnetic helicity, yielding $\nabla \times \mathbf{B} = \lambda \mathbf{B}$ with constant $\lambda$ (a Taylor state). During each relaxation cycle, the parallel electric field $E_{\parallel}$ is identically zero everywhere, implying that reconnection proceeds without any parallel electric field.

D. Tearing modes in RFPs require finite plasma pressure and bad curvature to be driven; they are interchange-like and are stabilized as $\beta \to 0$.

E. In a cylindrical RFP with safety factor $q(r)$, the resonant condition for an $m/n$ mode is $q(r_{s}) = m/n$, which in typical RFP profiles with $q(r) &lt; 1$ admits many $m=1$ resonances. When multiple islands nonlinearly overlap, the field lines become stochastic, and the effective radial thermal diffusivity can scale like $\chi_{\mathrm{eff}} \sim v_{\parallel} D_{\mathrm{FL}}$ with a field-line diffusion coefficient $D_{\mathrm{FL}} \sim (\delta B / B)^{2} L_{c}$ (Rechester–Rosenbluth scaling), contributing to profile flattening during sawtooth-like relaxation oscillations.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{BE}$$", "solution": "The problem statement is a valid exercise in magnetohydrodynamic (MHD) stability theory as applied to Reversed Field Pinch (RFP) plasmas. It is scientifically grounded, well-posed, objective, and provides sufficient information to evaluate the provided options. The problem asks for a correct description of resistive tearing modes in RFPs, their properties, and their consequences for transport and plasma relaxation, based on the principles of single-fluid resistive MHD.\n\nThe fundamental governing equations for this system are the resistive MHD equations. The induction equation, derived from Faraday's law and a simplified Ohm's law ($\\mathbf{E} + \\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\eta \\mathbf{j}$), is:\n$$\n\\frac{\\partial \\mathbf{B}}{\\partial t} = \\nabla \\times (\\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{B}) - \\nabla \\times (\\eta \\mathbf{j})\n$$\nIn ideal MHD ($\\eta = 0$), magnetic field lines are frozen into the plasma fluid. Resistivity ($\\eta \\neq 0$) breaks this frozen-in constraint, allowing for changes in magnetic topology, i.e., magnetic reconnection.\n\nA tearing mode is a resistive instability that is localized near a rational surface $r_s$ where the safety factor $q(r_s) = m/n$, which is equivalent to the condition $\\mathbf{k} \\cdot \\mathbf{B} = 0$ for a helical perturbation with wavevector $\\mathbf{k}$. Instability occurs if there is free magnetic energy available from the equilibrium current profile. This is quantified by the tearing stability index, $\\Delta'$, which is the jump in the logarithmic derivative of the perturbed radial magnetic field across the resistive layer. A mode is unstable if $\\Delta' > 0$.\n\nIn RFPs, the safety factor profile is typically $q(r) \\ll 1$, shearing from a small positive value on-axis to a small negative value at the edge. This allows for a dense spectrum of rational surfaces for modes with poloidal mode number $m=1$ and various toroidal mode numbers $n$. These modes are often found to be linearly unstable (i.e., $\\Delta' > 0$), driven by the strong parallel current gradients in the RFP equilibrium.\n\nThe nonlinear evolution of these multiple unstable tearing modes is central to RFP physics. The magnetic islands associated with each mode can grow and overlap. According to the Chirikov criterion, island overlap leads to the destruction of well-defined magnetic flux surfaces and the formation of a volume-filling region of stochastic magnetic field lines. This stochasticity dramatically enhances particle and heat transport along the now-chaotic field lines. The process occurs in cycles of profile peaking followed by a rapid crash and flattening, known as relaxation oscillations or sawteeth. This same nonlinear mode activity also generates a mean electromotive force, $\\langle \\tilde{\\mathbf{v}} \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{B}} \\rangle$, which acts as a dynamo to sustain the RFP's characteristic reversed toroidal field against resistive dissipation.\n\nNow, we evaluate each option based on these principles.\n\n**A. In resistive MHD, a tearing mode in an RFP reconnects magnetic field lines at rational surfaces where the tearing stability index satisfies $\\Delta' < 0$, and its linear growth rate increases with the Lundquist number $S$ as $\\gamma \\propto S^{3/5}$. The resulting magnetic topology reduces radial transport by eliminating stochasticity.**\n\nThis statement contains three fundamental errors:\n1.  The condition for a tearing instability to be driven by current gradients is $\\Delta' > 0$. A negative $\\Delta'$ implies stability.\n2.  The linear growth rate $\\gamma$ of a classical tearing mode *decreases* with the Lundquist number $S = \\tau_R / \\tau_A$ (where $\\tau_R$ is the resistive time and $\\tau_A$ is the Alfvén time). The correct scaling is $\\gamma \\propto S^{-3/5}$. A larger $S$ corresponds to a more conductive plasma, where reconnection is slower.\n3.  The growth and interaction of multiple tearing modes in an RFP *creates* magnetic stochasticity, which dramatically *increases* radial transport, rather than reducing it.\n\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n**B. In resistive MHD with uniform resistivity $\\eta$, the inner resistive layer of a classical tearing mode satisfies a scaling $\\delta/a \\sim S^{-2/5} (k a)^{-1/5}$ and a growth rate scaling $\\gamma \\tau_{A} \\sim \\Delta'^{4/5} S^{-3/5} (k a)^{2/5}$ (the Furth–Killeen–Rosenbluth regime), where $k$ is the parallel wavenumber, $\\tau_{A}$ is the Alfvén time, and $\\Delta'$ is the outer-region matching parameter. In RFPs, many resonant $m=1$ modes can overlap, producing stochastic magnetic fields that enhance radial transport, and a fluctuation-induced mean electromotive force $\\langle \\tilde{\\mathbf{v}} \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{B}} \\rangle_{\\parallel}$ that participates in sustaining field reversal and relaxation oscillations.**\n\nThis statement is a comprehensive and accurate description.\n1.  The scaling laws for the resistive layer width ($\\delta$) and the growth rate ($\\gamma$) are consistent with the classic Furth–Killeen–Rosenbluth (FKR) theory for tearing modes. Specifically, the dependencies $\\delta \\propto S^{-2/5}$ and $\\gamma \\propto S^{-3/5}$ are correct. The dependencies on $\\Delta'$ and the wavenumber $k$ are also consistent with established theory.\n2.  The description of the RFP context is correct: the presence of many resonant $m=1$ modes, their overlap producing stochastic magnetic fields, and the consequent enhancement of radial transport are all defining features of RFP dynamics.\n3.  The identification of the fluctuation-induced mean electromotive force, $\\langle \\tilde{\\mathbf{v}} \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{B}} \\rangle$, as the MHD dynamo mechanism responsible for sustaining the field reversal and driving relaxation oscillations is also a cornerstone of RFP physics.\n\n**Verdict: Correct**\n\n**C. The quasi-steady relaxed state achieved after repeated tearing-driven relaxation in an RFP can be modeled by minimizing magnetic energy at fixed magnetic helicity, yielding $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\lambda \\mathbf{B}$ with constant $\\lambda$ (a Taylor state). During each relaxation cycle, the parallel electric field $E_{\\parallel}$ is identically zero everywhere, implying that reconnection proceeds without any parallel electric field.**\n\nThis statement contains a critical flaw.\n1.  The first part is correct: Taylor's relaxation theory, which posits that a plasma relaxes to a minimum energy state subject to conserved magnetic helicity, leading to the force-free equilibrium $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\lambda \\mathbf{B}$ with constant $\\lambda$, is a highly successful conceptual model for the global structure of RFP relaxed states.\n2.  The second part is fundamentally incorrect. Magnetic reconnection, the process that allows for the topological change of magnetic field lines during relaxation, is an inherently resistive process. From the generalized Ohm's law, $E_{\\parallel} = \\eta j_{\\parallel} - (\\text{other terms})$. In resistive MHD, a non-zero current density $j_{\\parallel}$ must flow within the reconnection layer to support the change in magnetic field. This requires a non-zero parallel electric field, $E_{\\parallel} = \\eta j_{\\parallel}$, in this layer. The statement that $E_{\\parallel}$ is \"identically zero everywhere\" during the relaxation cycle is a direct contradiction of the physics of resistive reconnection.\n\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n**D. Tearing modes in RFPs require finite plasma pressure and bad curvature to be driven; they are interchange-like and are stabilized as $\\beta \\to 0$.**\n\nThis statement mischaracterizes the nature of tearing modes.\n1.  Classical tearing modes are driven by gradients in the plasma current density, not plasma pressure. The source of free energy is the non-uniform magnetic field configuration. They are a fundamental instability even in the zero-pressure limit ($\\beta \\to 0$).\n2.  Interchange modes are distinct instabilities driven by plasma pressure in a region of \"bad\" magnetic curvature (where $\\nabla p \\cdot \\nabla B > 0$). While finite-$\\beta$ effects can modify tearing modes (e.g., the \"resistive interchange\" or \"g-mode\"), the primary tearing modes in an RFP are current-driven.\n3.  Consequently, tearing modes are not stabilized as $\\beta \\to 0$; in fact, the $\\beta = 0$ limit provides the purest example of a current-driven tearing instability.\n\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\n**E. In a cylindrical RFP with safety factor $q(r)$, the resonant condition for an $m/n$ mode is $q(r_{s}) = m/n$, which in typical RFP profiles with $q(r) < 1$ admits many $m=1$ resonances. When multiple islands nonlinearly overlap, the field lines become stochastic, and the effective radial thermal diffusivity can scale like $\\chi_{\\mathrm{eff}} \\sim v_{\\parallel} D_{\\mathrm{FL}}$ with a field-line diffusion coefficient $D_{\\mathrm{FL}} \\sim (\\delta B / B)^{2} L_{c}$ (Rechester–Rosenbluth scaling), contributing to profile flattening during sawtooth-like relaxation oscillations.**\n\nThis statement is an accurate description of a key consequence of tearing modes in RFPs.\n1.  The resonance condition $q(r_s) = m/n$ is stated correctly. The observation that typical RFP profiles admit a dense spectrum of $m=1$ resonances is also correct.\n2.  The description of island overlap leading to stochastic magnetic fields is correct.\n3.  The model for transport in a stochastic field is correctly identified as the Rechester-Rosenbluth scaling, where the effective diffusivity $\\chi_{\\mathrm{eff}}$ is proportional to the parallel velocity of the transporting species ($v_{\\parallel}$, typically the electron thermal velocity $v_{th,e}$) and a field-line diffusion coefficient $D_{\\mathrm{FL}}$.\n4.  The scaling provided for the field-line diffusion coefficient, $D_{\\mathrm{FL}} \\sim (\\delta B/B)^2 L_c$, where $\\delta B/B$ is the normalized magnetic fluctuation amplitude and $L_c$ is a parallel correlation length, is a standard quasi-linear estimate for this quantity.\n5.  Finally, this enhanced transport is correctly identified as the mechanism responsible for the rapid profile flattening observed during the crash phase of RFP relaxation oscillations.\n\n**Verdict: Correct**"}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{BE}$$", "solution": "We begin from single-fluid resistive magnetohydrodynamics (MHD), where the magnetic field $\\mathbf{B}$, velocity $\\mathbf{v}$, and current density $\\mathbf{J}$ obey\n- Faraday’s law: $\\partial \\mathbf{B} / \\partial t = - \\nabla \\times \\mathbf{E}$,\n- Ohm’s law: $\\mathbf{E} + \\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\eta \\mathbf{J}$ (uniform resistivity $\\eta$),\n- Ampère’s law (neglecting displacement current): $\\mu_{0} \\mathbf{J} = \\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B}$,\n- Momentum and continuity equations (for completeness), with low plasma beta $\\beta$ simplifying the force balance to primarily magnetic terms near reconnection layers.\n\nCombining Faraday’s law with Ohm’s law gives the induction equation\n$\\partial \\mathbf{B} / \\partial t = \\nabla \\times (\\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{B}) - \\nabla \\times (\\eta \\mathbf{J})$.\nLinearizing about a cylindrical equilibrium and considering helical perturbations, a resistive tearing mode arises at a rational (resonant) surface $r_{s}$ where the ideal-MHD parallel wavevector vanishes, equivalently $\\mathbf{k} \\cdot \\mathbf{B}_{0} = 0$. For a mode with poloidal and toroidal integers $(m,n)$, this resonance condition is commonly written as $q(r_{s}) = m/n$ in a cylindrical approximation.\n\nIn the classical Furth–Killeen–Rosenbluth (FKR) regime of resistive tearing, the outer (ideal) region solution determines a matching parameter $\\Delta'$ that quantifies the jump in the logarithmic derivative of the perturbed magnetic flux across the inner resistive layer. Instability requires $\\Delta' &gt; 0$. The inner layer is governed by a balance between resistive diffusion along the perturbed reconnecting field and advection, leading to a characteristic inner layer width $\\delta$ and a growth rate $\\gamma$ that scale with the Lundquist number $S \\equiv \\mu_{0} a V_{A}/\\eta$ (with $V_{A}$ the Alfvén speed and $a$ a minor-radius scale). The FKR asymptotics yield $\\delta/a \\sim S^{-2/5} (k a)^{-1/5}$ and $\\gamma \\tau_{A} \\sim \\Delta'^{4/5} S^{-3/5} (k a)^{2/5}$, where $\\tau_{A} \\equiv a/V_{A}$ and $k$ is the effective parallel wavenumber entering the inner-layer equations. Thus, in the resistive regime, the growth rate decreases as resistivity decreases (that is, as $S$ increases).\n\nIn an RFP, the safety factor satisfies $q(r) \\lesssim \\mathcal{O}(1)$ and typically $q(r) &lt; 1$ across most of the radius, so many $m=1$ rational surfaces exist for a range of $n$. A spectrum of tearing modes can thus be resonant across the plasma. Nonlinearly, these modes form magnetic islands; when the islands overlap (e.g., with a Chirikov parameter exceeding unity), the field lines become stochastic across extended regions. Parallel free-streaming along stochastic field lines strongly enhances effective radial transport. In the collisionless Rechester–Rosenbluth picture, the field-line diffusion coefficient scales as $D_{\\mathrm{FL}} \\sim (\\delta B / B)^{2} L_{c}$, with $L_{c}$ a correlation length along the field lines; the effective thermal diffusivity scales as $\\chi_{\\mathrm{eff}} \\sim v_{\\parallel} D_{\\mathrm{FL}}$, with $v_{\\parallel}$ a characteristic thermal speed. This enhanced transport promotes flattening of temperature and current profiles.\n\nRFPs also exhibit global relaxation oscillations, akin to sawteeth, in which tearing activity transiently increases, causing rapid profile flattening and magnetic energy redistribution while approximately conserving the global magnetic helicity $K \\equiv \\int \\mathbf{A} \\cdot \\mathbf{B} \\, dV$ on timescales short compared to resistive diffusion. Minimization of magnetic energy at fixed $K$ yields a Beltrami (Taylor) state $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\lambda \\mathbf{B}$ with constant $\\lambda$. The fluctuation-induced mean electromotive force (EMF) $\\boldsymbol{\\mathcal{E}} \\equiv \\langle \\tilde{\\mathbf{v}} \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{B}} \\rangle$ enters the mean induction equation as an effective dynamo term along $\\mathbf{B}$, $\\boldsymbol{\\mathcal{E}}_{\\parallel}$, aiding the sustainment of reversed toroidal field against resistive decay. Reconnection in tearing layers fundamentally requires a finite parallel electric field $E_{\\parallel}$ in the inner region, supplied by resistivity (or other non-ideal effects), so $E_{\\parallel}$ cannot be identically zero during reconnection events.\n\nWe now analyze each option:\n\nA. This option asserts instability for $\\Delta' &lt; 0$ and a growth rate increasing with $S$ as $\\gamma \\propto S^{3/5}$, and claims transport is reduced by eliminating stochasticity. Classical tearing requires $\\Delta' &gt; 0$, and in the resistive regime $\\gamma$ decreases with increasing $S$, scaling as $\\gamma \\propto S^{-3/5}$ under FKR ordering. Moreover, nonlinear tearing in RFPs tends to enhance, not reduce, radial transport due to island overlap and stochastic field lines. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nB. The stated FKR scalings $\\delta/a \\sim S^{-2/5} (k a)^{-1/5}$ and $\\gamma \\tau_{A} \\sim \\Delta'^{4/5} S^{-3/5} (k a)^{2/5}$ follow from inner-outer matching in resistive MHD for $\\Delta' a \\ll 1$ ordering and are standard results. The description that many $m=1$ resonances overlap in RFPs, producing stochastic magnetic fields and enhanced transport, and that the fluctuation-induced mean electromotive force $\\langle \\tilde{\\mathbf{v}} \\times \\tilde{\\mathbf{B}} \\rangle_{\\parallel}$ contributes to sustaining field reversal and relaxation oscillations, is consistent with mean-field dynamo theory and observed RFP phenomenology. Verdict: Correct.\n\nC. The first sentence correctly states the Taylor relaxation principle: minimize magnetic energy at fixed helicity to obtain $\\nabla \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\lambda \\mathbf{B}$. The second sentence, however, incorrectly claims $E_{\\parallel}$ is identically zero during relaxation; tearing reconnection requires nonzero $E_{\\parallel}$ in the resistive layer (or other non-ideal terms) to break field-line freezing. Thus the option as a whole is false. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nD. Tearing modes are primarily current-gradient driven resistive instabilities that exist even as $\\beta \\to 0$. They do not require bad curvature drive; that is characteristic of interchange or ballooning modes. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nE. The resonance condition $q(r_{s}) = m/n$ is the standard cylindrical criterion. In typical RFP profiles with $q(r) &lt; 1$, many $m=1$ resonances exist. When islands overlap, field lines become stochastic and heat transport can follow Rechester–Rosenbluth scaling, $\\chi_{\\mathrm{eff}} \\sim v_{\\parallel} D_{\\mathrm{FL}}$ with $D_{\\mathrm{FL}} \\sim (\\delta B / B)^{2} L_{c}$, contributing to profile flattening during sawtooth-like relaxation oscillations. This is consistent with established theory and simulations. Verdict: Correct.\n\nTherefore, the correct statements are B and E."}]


title: Find the growth rate of the resistive tearing mode instability in a plasma sheet by asymptotically matching solutions to the ideal and resistive MHD equations.

Question: In the theory of magnetic reconnection in high-temperature plasmas, the resistive tearing mode instability plays a crucial role. Its analysis often involves matching solutions of magnetohydrodynamic (MHD) equations in an "outer" ideal plasma region with those in a thin "inner" resistive layer.

The stability of the outer region for a mode with wave vector $\mathbf{k}$ is determined by a parameter $\Delta'$, representing the jump in the logarithmic derivative of the perturbed magnetic flux function across the resistive layer. A positive $\Delta'$ indicates a source of free energy for the instability. For a particular plasma configuration confined in a slab of characteristic thickness $L$, the stability parameter $\Delta'$ is found to be a function of the dimensionless wavenumber $x = kL$:
$$ \Delta'(x) L = A x e^{-x} - B $$
Here, $A$ and $B$ are positive, dimensionless constants related to the geometry of the magnetic field and the background plasma pressure. The term proportional to $A$ represents the destabilizing influence of the plasma current gradient, while the constant $B$ represents the stabilizing effect of magnetic field line bending. A tearing mode can only grow if $\Delta' > 0$.

The physics of the inner resistive layer provides a dispersion relation that connects $\Delta'$ to the instability's growth rate, $\gamma$. For the regime of interest, this relation is:
$$ \gamma \tau_A = C \left(\Delta'(x) L\right)^{4/5} S^{-3/5} $$
Here, $\tau_A$ is the characteristic Alfvén time, $S$ is the dimensionless Lundquist number (which is very large for typical fusion plasmas, $S \gg 1$), and $C$ is a dimensionless coefficient of order unity.

Assuming the condition $A > Be$ is satisfied, find the maximum possible growth rate, $\gamma_{max}$, for this system. Express your answer as a symbolic expression in terms of the given constants $A$, $B$, $C$, $S$, and $\tau_A$.



Answer: [{"answer": "\n$$ \\boxed{\\frac{C}{\\tau_A} \\left(\\frac{A}{e} - B\\right)^{4/5} S^{-3/5}} $$\n", "solution": "The problem asks for the maximum growth rate $\\gamma_{max}$ of the resistive tearing mode. The growth rate $\\gamma$ is given as a function of the dimensionless wavenumber $x=kL$ through the stability parameter $\\Delta'(x)L$.\n\nThe provided dispersion relation is:\n$$ \\gamma(x) \\tau_A = C \\left(\\Delta'(x) L\\right)^{4/5} S^{-3/5} $$\n\nThe stability parameter is given by:\n$$ \\Delta'(x) L = A x e^{-x} - B $$\n\nThe growth rate $\\gamma$ is real and positive only when $\\Delta'(x) L > 0$. Since $C$, $S$, and $\\tau_A$ are positive constants, maximizing $\\gamma(x)$ is equivalent to maximizing the function $f(x) = \\Delta'(x) L$. The exponent $4/5$ is positive, so the maximum of $\\gamma$ will occur at the same value of $x$ that maximizes $\\Delta'(x)L$.\n\nLet's define the function we want to maximize:\n$$ f(x) = A x e^{-x} - B $$\nThe physical domain for the dimensionless wavenumber $x=kL$ is $x > 0$.\n\nTo find the maximum value of $f(x)$, we first compute its derivative with respect to $x$ and set it to zero.\n$$ \\frac{df}{dx} = \\frac{d}{dx} \\left( A x e^{-x} - B \\right) $$\nUsing the product rule for the first term:\n$$ \\frac{df}{dx} = A \\left( (1)e^{-x} + x(-e^{-x}) \\right) - 0 $$\n$$ \\frac{df}{dx} = A e^{-x} (1 - x) $$\n\nSet the derivative to zero to find critical points:\n$$ A e^{-x} (1 - x) = 0 $$\nSince $A > 0$ and $e^{-x} > 0$ for all real $x$, the only way for the derivative to be zero is if:\n$$ 1 - x = 0 \\implies x = 1 $$\n\nSo, a critical point exists at $x=1$. To determine if this is a maximum, we check the second derivative of $f(x)$:\n$$ \\frac{d^2f}{dx^2} = \\frac{d}{dx} \\left( A e^{-x} - A x e^{-x} \\right) $$\n$$ \\frac{d^2f}{dx^2} = -A e^{-x} - A(e^{-x} - x e^{-x}) $$\n$$ \\frac{d^2f}{dx^2} = -A e^{-x} - A e^{-x} + A x e^{-x} = A e^{-x} (x - 2) $$\nNow, evaluate the second derivative at the critical point $x=1$:\n$$ \\left. \\frac{d^2f}{dx^2} \\right|_{x=1} = A e^{-1} (1 - 2) = -A e^{-1} = -\\frac{A}{e} $$\nSince $A > 0$, the second derivative is negative. This confirms that $x=1$ corresponds to a local maximum of $f(x)$.\n\nThe maximum value of the function $f(x)$ is obtained by substituting $x=1$ back into its expression:\n$$ f_{max} = f(1) = A (1) e^{-1} - B = \\frac{A}{e} - B $$\n\nThe problem states that an unstable mode exists, which requires $\\Delta' > 0$. The maximum value of $\\Delta'L$ is $f_{max} = A/e - B$. The condition given in the problem, $A > Be$, ensures that $A/e > B$, so $f_{max}$ is positive. Therefore, there is a range of unstable $x$ values, and the growth rate reaches its maximum at $x=1$.\n\nNow we can find the maximum growth rate, $\\gamma_{max}$, by substituting $f_{max}$ for $\\Delta'L$ in the dispersion relation:\n$$ \\gamma_{max} \\tau_A = C (f_{max})^{4/5} S^{-3/5} $$\n$$ \\gamma_{max} \\tau_A = C \\left(\\frac{A}{e} - B\\right)^{4/5} S^{-3/5} $$\n\nFinally, we solve for $\\gamma_{max}$:\n$$ \\gamma_{max} = \\frac{C}{\\tau_A} \\left(\\frac{A}{e} - B\\right)^{4/5} S^{-3/5} $$\nThis expression represents the highest possible growth rate for the tearing mode instability in this specific plasma configuration."}, {"answer": "\n$$\\boxed{\\frac{C\\,S^{-3/5}\\Bigl(\\frac{A}{e}-B\\Bigr)^{4/5}}{\\tau_A}}$$\n", "solution": "1. The outer‐region stability parameter is \n$$\\Delta'(x)L = A\\,x\\,e^{-x} - B.$$\n2. The inner‐layer dispersion relation is \n$$\\gamma\\,\\tau_A = C\\bigl(\\Delta'(x)L\\bigr)^{4/5}S^{-3/5}.$$\n3. To maximize $\\gamma$, we maximize \n$$D(x)=\\Delta'(x)L = A\\,x\\,e^{-x} - B.$$\n4. Compute \n$$\\frac{dD}{dx} = A\\,e^{-x}(1 - x)\\quad\\Longrightarrow\\quad x=1$$ \nis the only critical point.\n5. Check concavity: \n$$\\frac{d^2D}{dx^2}\\bigg|_{x=1} = -A\\,e^{-1}<0,$$ \nso $x=1$ is a maximum.\n6. The maximum jump is \n$$D_{\\max} = A\\cdot1\\cdot e^{-1}-B = \\frac{A}{e}-B.$$\n7. Thus \n$$\\gamma_{\\max}\\,\\tau_A = C\\Bigl(\\frac{A}{e}-B\\Bigr)^{4/5}S^{-3/5},$$ \nand hence \n$$\\gamma_{\\max} = \\frac{C\\,S^{-3/5}\\Bigl(\\frac{A}{e}-B\\Bigr)^{4/5}}{\\tau_A}.$$"}]


title: Calculating the linear growth rate of a long-wavelength tearing mode instability for a given Harris current sheet equilibrium in resistive magnetohydrodynamics.

Question: In the theory of resistive magnetohydrodynamics, the tearing mode is a crucial instability responsible for magnetic reconnection in current sheets. For a standard Harris current sheet equilibrium, characterized by a magnetic field profile $\mathbf{B}_0(z) = B_0 \tanh(z/L) \hat{\mathbf{x}}$, the linear growth rate $\gamma$ of a tearing mode with wavenumber $k$ is often studied in the context of Furth-Killeen-Rosenbluth (FKR) theory.

A refined model, incorporating certain kinetic effects that become significant at long wavelengths, proposes the following phenomenological expression for the growth rate $\gamma(k)$:
$$ \gamma(k) = C (kL)^{\beta} \left( \frac{1}{kL} - kL \right)^{\alpha} $$
This expression is valid for wavenumbers in the range $0 < kL < 1$. Here, $L$ is the characteristic half-width of the current sheet, $C$ is a positive constant that depends on the equilibrium parameters (such as $B_0$, an equilibrium density $\rho_c$, and the plasma resistivity $\eta$), and $\alpha$ and $\beta$ are dimensionless positive constants that characterize the physical regime of the instability.

Consider a specific physical scenario where the tearing mode instability is described by the parameters $\alpha = 4/5$ and $\beta = 8/5$. Your task is to determine the maximum possible growth rate, $\gamma_{max}$, for this mode. Express your answer as a function of the constant $C$.



Answer: [{"answer": "\n$$\\boxed{C\\frac{2^{4/5}}{3^{6/5}}}$$\n", "solution": "We set $x = kL$ so that $0<x<1$ and write\n$$\\gamma(x)\\;=\\;C\\,(kL)^{8/5}\\Bigl(\\tfrac1{kL}-kL\\Bigr)^{4/5}\n\\;=\\;C\\,x^{8/5}\\Bigl(\\tfrac{1-x^2}{x}\\Bigr)^{4/5}\n\\;=\\;C\\,x^{4/5}(1-x^2)^{4/5}\\,. $$\n\nDefine the auxiliary function \n$$f(x)=x(1-x^2)\\,,\\quad 0<x<1,$$ \nso that $\\gamma(x)=C\\,[f(x)]^{4/5}$.  To find the maximum, compute\n$$f'(x)=1-3x^2\\,,\\quad f'(x)=0\\ \\Rightarrow\\ x^2=\\tfrac13\\ \\Rightarrow\\ x=\\tfrac1{\\sqrt3}\\,. $$\nAt this critical point,\n$$f_{\\max}=f\\bigl(\\tfrac1{\\sqrt3}\\bigr)\n=\\tfrac1{\\sqrt3}-\\tfrac1{(\\sqrt3)^3}\n=\\tfrac1{\\sqrt3}-\\tfrac1{3\\sqrt3}\n=\\tfrac{2}{3\\sqrt3}\\,. $$\nHence the maximum growth rate is\n$$\n\\gamma_{\\max}\n=C\\Bigl(\\tfrac{2}{3\\sqrt3}\\Bigr)^{4/5}\n=C\\,\\frac{2^{4/5}}{(3\\sqrt3)^{4/5}}\n=C\\frac{2^{4/5}}{3^{6/5}}\\,.\n$$"}, {"answer": "\n$$ \\boxed{C \\cdot 2^{4/5} \\cdot 3^{-6/5}} $$\n", "solution": "The problem asks for the maximum value of the growth rate $\\gamma(k)$ given by the expression:\n$$ \\gamma(k) = C (kL)^{\\beta} \\left( \\frac{1}{kL} - kL \\right)^{\\alpha} $$\nwith the specific parameter values $\\alpha = 4/5$ and $\\beta = 8/5$. The expression is valid for $0 < kL < 1$.\n\nTo simplify the notation, let's introduce the dimensionless wavenumber $x = kL$. The expression for the growth rate as a function of $x$ is:\n$$ \\gamma(x) = C x^{\\beta} \\left( \\frac{1}{x} - x \\right)^{\\alpha} $$\nwhere $x \\in (0, 1)$.\n\nWe can rewrite the expression to consolidate the powers of $x$:\n$$ \\gamma(x) = C x^{\\beta} \\left( \\frac{1-x^2}{x} \\right)^{\\alpha} = C x^{\\beta} \\frac{(1-x^2)^{\\alpha}}{x^\\alpha} = C x^{\\beta-\\alpha} (1-x^2)^{\\alpha} $$\n\nTo find the maximum value of $\\gamma(x)$, we can find the maximum of its logarithm, which is a monotonic transformation. Let $g(x) = \\ln(\\gamma(x)/C)$.\n$$ g(x) = \\ln\\left(x^{\\beta-\\alpha} (1-x^2)^{\\alpha}\\right) = (\\beta-\\alpha) \\ln(x) + \\alpha \\ln(1-x^2) $$\n\nNow, we differentiate $g(x)$ with respect to $x$ and set the derivative to zero to find the critical points.\n$$ \\frac{dg}{dx} = (\\beta-\\alpha) \\frac{1}{x} + \\alpha \\frac{-2x}{1-x^2} $$\nSetting $\\frac{dg}{dx} = 0$:\n$$ \\frac{\\beta-\\alpha}{x} = \\frac{2\\alpha x}{1-x^2} $$\n\nNow, we solve for $x^2$.\n$$ (\\beta-\\alpha)(1-x^2) = 2\\alpha x^2 $$\n$$ \\beta-\\alpha - (\\beta-\\alpha)x^2 = 2\\alpha x^2 $$\n$$ \\beta-\\alpha = (2\\alpha + \\beta - \\alpha)x^2 $$\n$$ \\beta-\\alpha = (\\alpha + \\beta)x^2 $$\n$$ x^2 = \\frac{\\beta-\\alpha}{\\beta+\\alpha} $$\n\nNow, we substitute the given values $\\alpha = 4/5$ and $\\beta = 8/5$.\n$$ x^2 = \\frac{8/5 - 4/5}{8/5 + 4/5} = \\frac{4/5}{12/5} = \\frac{4}{12} = \\frac{1}{3} $$\nThis gives the value of $x=kL$ that maximizes the growth rate:\n$$ x_{max} = \\sqrt{\\frac{1}{3}} = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}} $$\nSince $0 < 1/\\sqrt{3} < 1$, this critical point is within the domain of validity.\n\nTo confirm it's a maximum, we could check the second derivative, but it's clear from the function's behavior ($\\gamma(x) \\to 0$ as $x \\to 0$ because $\\beta=8/5 > 0$ and as $x \\to 1$ because of the $(1-x^2)$ term) that this stationary point must be a maximum.\n\nFinally, we substitute this value of $x$ back into the expression for $\\gamma(x)$ to find the maximum growth rate, $\\gamma_{max}$.\n$$ \\gamma_{max} = \\gamma(x_{max}) = C x_{max}^{\\beta-\\alpha} (1-x_{max}^2)^{\\alpha} $$\nUsing $x_{max}^2 = 1/3$:\n$$ \\gamma_{max} = C \\left( \\frac{1}{3} \\right)^{(\\beta-\\alpha)/2} \\left( 1 - \\frac{1}{3} \\right)^{\\alpha} $$\nSubstitute $\\alpha=4/5$ and $\\beta=8/5$:\n$$ \\beta-\\alpha = 8/5 - 4/5 = 4/5 $$\nSo, $(\\beta-\\alpha)/2 = (4/5)/2 = 2/5$.\n$$ \\gamma_{max} = C \\left( \\frac{1}{3} \\right)^{2/5} \\left( \\frac{2}{3} \\right)^{4/5} $$\nNow, let's simplify this expression.\n$$ \\gamma_{max} = C \\cdot 3^{-2/5} \\cdot (2^{4/5} \\cdot 3^{-4/5}) $$\n$$ \\gamma_{max} = C \\cdot 2^{4/5} \\cdot 3^{-2/5 - 4/5} $$\n$$ \\gamma_{max} = C \\cdot 2^{4/5} \\cdot 3^{-6/5} $$\nThis is the final expression for the maximum growth rate in terms of the constant $C$."}]


title: [multiple_choice] Define NTMs as tearing modes driven by bootstrap current deficit inside islands and write the modified Rutherford equation including the bootstrap term that depends on pressure flattening.

Question: A neoclassical tearing mode (NTM) is observed in a large–aspect-ratio tokamak in which a three-dimensional magnetic island forms at a rational surface where the safety factor $q$ satisfies $q = m/n$ for integers $m$ and $n$. The island destroys axisymmetry locally and produces a region of flattened thermodynamic pressure along the perturbed helical field lines. Consider single-fluid resistive magnetohydrodynamics (MHD), with Ohm’s law $\mathbf{E} + \mathbf{v} \times \mathbf{B} = \eta \mathbf{J}$, Ampère’s law $\nabla \times \mathbf{B} = \mu_0 \mathbf{J}$, and quasineutrality. Let $r_s$ be the rational surface radius, $\eta$ the Spitzer resistivity, and $\tau_R \equiv \mu_0 r_s^2 / \eta$ the local resistive diffusion time. The classical Rutherford picture relates the island half-width $w$ to the helical flux evolution through the parallel electric field and yields a growth term proportional to the classical tearing stability parameter $\Delta'$.

In a three-dimensional MHD equilibrium containing an island, the pressure $p$ tends to flatten along island flux surfaces when parallel conduction dominates over cross-field diffusion. This flattening reduces the local bootstrap current $J_{\mathrm{bs}}$, which depends on the pressure gradient, thereby introducing a helical current perturbation that can drive the island even when the classical tearing drive is weak. Let $L_p \equiv - p/(dp/dr)$ be the pressure scale length, $L_q \equiv q/(dq/dr)$ the magnetic shear length, and $w_c$ a transport-set critical island width separating the regimes of negligible versus strong pressure flattening. A dimensionless function $G(w/w_c)$ is used to capture the transition, with $G \to 0$ when $w \ll w_c$ (no flattening) and $G \to 1$ when $w \gg w_c$ (full flattening). The modified Rutherford equation for an NTM is obtained by supplementing the classical term with a bootstrap drive term proportional to $G(w/w_c)$ and the ratio $L_q/L_p$. The sign of the bootstrap term reflects that a deficit of bootstrap current inside the island tends to amplify the tearing perturbation.

Which option correctly defines an NTM and provides a dimensionally consistent modified Rutherford equation that includes the bootstrap current deficit term with physically correct dependence on pressure flattening and magnetic/pressure scale lengths?

A. An NTM is a tearing mode whose drive is provided by the deficit of bootstrap current inside a pressure-flattened island. The modified Rutherford equation is
$$
\frac{\tau_R}{r_s}\,\frac{dw}{dt} \;=\; r_s\,\Delta' \;+\; C_{\mathrm{bs}}\,\frac{L_q}{L_p}\,\frac{w^2}{w^2 + w_c^2},
$$
where $C_{\mathrm{bs}}$ is a dimensionless constant set by geometry and neoclassical coefficients.

B. An NTM is an ideal MHD mode driven by parallel current enhancement, independent of pressure flattening. The modified Rutherford equation is
$$
\frac{\tau_R}{r_s}\,\frac{dw}{dt} \;=\; r_s\,\Delta' \;-\; C_{\mathrm{bs}}\,\frac{L_q}{L_p}\,\frac{w^2}{w^2 + w_c^2}.
$$

C. An NTM is a tearing mode driven by the bootstrap current deficit, but the bootstrap drive is strongest at small island width since pressure flattening is maximal when $w \ll w_c$. The modified Rutherford equation is
$$
\frac{\tau_R}{r_s}\,\frac{dw}{dt} \;=\; r_s\,\Delta' \;+\; C_{\mathrm{bs}}\,\frac{L_q}{L_p}\,\frac{w_c^2}{w^2 + w_c^2}.
$$

D. An NTM is a tearing mode driven by the bootstrap current deficit inside an island, but the bootstrap term depends on the inverse ratio $\frac{L_p}{L_q}$ and the flattening function is $1 - \exp\!\big(-w^2/w_c^2\big)$. The modified Rutherford equation is
$$
\frac{\tau_R}{r_s}\,\frac{dw}{dt} \;=\; \Delta' \;+\; C_{\mathrm{bs}}\,\frac{L_p}{L_q}\,\Big(1 - e^{-w^2/w_c^2}\Big).
$$

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "We derive the structure of the modified Rutherford equation for a neoclassical tearing mode (NTM) from first principles and then evaluate each option.\n\nPrinciple-based derivation:\n\n1. Resistive magnetohydrodynamics (MHD) and classical tearing growth. In single-fluid resistive MHD, Ohm’s law is $\\mathbf{E} + \\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{B} = \\eta \\mathbf{J}$. For a slowly evolving tearing mode, the parallel electric field balances resistive dissipation, $E_\\parallel \\simeq \\eta J_\\parallel$, and relates to the time rate of change of the helical flux function $\\psi$ through $E_\\parallel = - \\partial_t \\psi / L_\\parallel$, where $L_\\parallel$ is a parallel connection length. The magnetic island half-width $w$ is proportional to the square root of the helical flux perturbation, $w \\propto \\sqrt{\\psi}$, so $\\partial_t w$ scales with $\\eta J_\\parallel$ and the jump condition across the rational surface characterized by the classical tearing stability parameter $\\Delta'$. Rutherford’s coarse-grained balance yields, in normalized form,\n$$\n\\frac{\\tau_R}{r_s}\\,\\frac{dw}{dt} \\;=\\; r_s\\,\\Delta' \\;+\\; \\text{(additional drives)},\n$$\nwith $\\tau_R = \\mu_0 r_s^2 / \\eta$. Here, $r_s \\Delta'$ is dimensionless, ensuring the left-hand side is dimensionless.\n\n2. Bootstrap current and its deficit due to pressure flattening. In a tokamak, the bootstrap current density $J_{\\mathrm{bs}}$ arises from trapped particle dynamics and depends on the pressure gradient, $J_{\\mathrm{bs}} \\propto -\\left(dp/dr\\right)$ times neoclassical geometric factors and field strength. When a three-dimensional magnetic island forms, parallel transport along the distorted field lines is fast, flattening pressure inside the island provided the island exceeds a transport-set critical width $w_c$. The local reduction of $\\left|dp/dr\\right|$ inside the island reduces $J_{\\mathrm{bs}}$, producing a helical current perturbation that tends to enhance the tearing perturbation. This is the defining feature of a neoclassical tearing mode: a tearing instability driven by a bootstrap current deficit created by pressure flattening inside the island.\n\n3. Bootstrap drive term structure. The bootstrap term must:\n- Vanish when the island is too small to flatten pressure, $w \\ll w_c$.\n- Approach a finite drive when $w \\gg w_c$, where pressure is fully flattened in the island.\n- Scale with the ratio of magnetic shear length to pressure scale length, $\\frac{L_q}{L_p}$, since the neoclassical drive depends on how a given pressure gradient translates into current relative to magnetic shear.\n\nA widely used closure for the flattening is the monotone transition function\n$$\nG\\!\\left(\\frac{w}{w_c}\\right) \\;=\\; \\frac{w^2}{w^2 + w_c^2},\n$$\nwhich has $G \\to 0$ for $w \\ll w_c$ and $G \\to 1$ for $w \\gg w_c$, consistent with transport physics. The bootstrap drive then adds a term of the form\n$$\nC_{\\mathrm{bs}}\\,\\frac{L_q}{L_p}\\,G\\!\\left(\\frac{w}{w_c}\\right),\n$$\nwhere $C_{\\mathrm{bs}}$ is a dimensionless coefficient encapsulating geometry and neoclassical factors. The sign is positive because a deficit of bootstrap current inside the island introduces a helical current that drives the island growth (it is a source term, not a damping term). Combining with the classical drive yields\n$$\n\\frac{\\tau_R}{r_s}\\,\\frac{dw}{dt} \\;=\\; r_s\\,\\Delta' \\;+\\; C_{\\mathrm{bs}}\\,\\frac{L_q}{L_p}\\,\\frac{w^2}{w^2 + w_c^2}.\n$$\nThis expression is dimensionally consistent: the left-hand side is dimensionless, $r_s \\Delta'$ is dimensionless, and $C_{\\mathrm{bs}} (L_q/L_p) G$ is dimensionless.\n\nOption-by-option analysis:\n\nA. Definition and equation:\n- Definition: “An NTM is a tearing mode whose drive is provided by the deficit of bootstrap current inside a pressure-flattened island.” This is correct: an NTM is a neoclassical tearing mode driven by bootstrap current deficit caused by pressure flattening in the island.\n- Equation: $\\frac{\\tau_R}{r_s}\\frac{dw}{dt} = r_s\\Delta' + C_{\\mathrm{bs}}\\frac{L_q}{L_p}\\frac{w^2}{w^2 + w_c^2}$. This has the correct classical term normalization, the correct dependence on $L_q/L_p$, and a physically appropriate flattening function $G = \\frac{w^2}{w^2 + w_c^2}$ with the correct sign. Verdict: Correct.\n\nB. Definition and equation:\n- Definition: It claims an NTM is an ideal MHD mode driven by parallel current enhancement and independent of pressure flattening. This is incorrect; NTMs are resistive tearing modes driven by bootstrap current deficit that emerges due to pressure flattening inside the island.\n- Equation: The bootstrap term appears with a negative sign, $-\\; C_{\\mathrm{bs}}\\frac{L_q}{L_p}\\frac{w^2}{w^2 + w_c^2}$, implying damping rather than driving. This sign contradicts the physical mechanism. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nC. Definition and equation:\n- Definition: It acknowledges the bootstrap deficit but states that the drive is strongest at small $w$ due to “maximal flattening when $w \\ll w_c$.” That is physically backwards. This reduces the local bootstrap current $J_{\\mathrm{bs}}$, which depends on the pressure gradient. The general form of this equation describes the evolution of the island half-width, $w$.\n\n$1$. **Structure of the Equation:** The equation relates the rate of change of the island width, $dw/dt$, to various drive and damping terms. A common dimensionless form of the equation, as suggested by the options, is:\n$$\n\\frac{\\tau_R}{r_s}\\,\\frac{dw}{dt} = \\text{Sum of dimensionless terms}\n$$\nThe left-hand side is dimensionless: $\\tau_R$ has units of time ($T$), $r_s$ has units of length ($L$), and $dw/dt$ has units of velocity ($L/T$). Thus, $(\\tau_R/r_s) \\cdot (dw/dt)$ has dimensions of $(T/L) \\cdot (L/T)$, which is dimensionless.\n\n$2$. **Classical Tearing Term:** The classical drive for a tearing mode is quantified by the parameter $\\Delta'$. $\\Delta'$ has units of inverse length ($L^{-1}$). To make it a dimensionless drive term in the equation, it must be multiplied by a characteristic length, which is naturally the rational surface radius, $r_s$. The classical term is therefore $r_s \\Delta'$. This term is positive for an unstable classical tearing mode and negative for a stable one.\n\n$3$. **Neoclassical Bootstrap Term:** The problem states this term arises from a deficit of bootstrap current, $J_{\\mathrm{bs}}$, due to pressure flattening inside the island.\n    -   **Sign:** The problem states this current deficit \"amplify the tearing perturbation\".
