The statement's central premise is the exact opposite of the correct physical relationship. The mechanism described is factually wrong.\n\n**Verdict: Incorrect**\n\nThus, only statement A is consistent with the provided model and first principles."}]


title: [multiple_choice] Define massive gas injection and shattered pellet injection and explain their intended effects on thermal quench uniformity and runaway suppression.

Question: A toroidal plasma of major radius $R$ and minor radius $a$ experiences a disruption characterized by a rapid thermal quench and subsequent current quench. Consider two mitigation techniques: Massive Gas Injection (MGI), defined as the rapid release of neutral impurity gas through a fast valve at a single toroidal location, and Shattered Pellet Injection (SPI), defined as the injection of a cryogenic solid pellet that is deliberately shattered upstream of the plasma to form many fragments that penetrate and ablate inside the plasma on a short timescale.

Construct a minimal disruption scenario model that captures two intended mitigation goals: uniform thermal quench radiation and runaway electron suppression. Use the following first-principles bases and well-tested facts to formulate your reasoning:

- Energy balance: the thermal energy is $W_{\mathrm{th}} = \frac{3}{2} n_e V k_B T$, where $n_e$ is the electron density, $V$ is the plasma volume, $k_B$ is Boltzmann’s constant, and $T$ is the electron temperature. Radiative cooling due to injected impurities is modeled by $P_{\mathrm{rad}}(\mathbf{r},t) = n_e(\mathbf{r},t) n_Z(\mathbf{r},t) L_Z\!\left(T(\mathbf{r},t)\right)$, where $n_Z$ is the impurity density and $L_Z(T)$ is the radiative cooling function for charge state $Z$; the poloidally and toroidally uniform thermal quench corresponds to $P_{\mathrm{rad}}$ being approximately uniform on magnetic surfaces during the quench.
- Transport and source distribution: for MGI, a neutral gas front enters at a single toroidal location and spreads toroidally with characteristic speed $v_g$, giving a toroidal spreading time $\tau_\phi^{\mathrm{MGI}} \sim \frac{2\pi R}{v_g}$. For SPI, many fragments ablate along magnetic field lines, producing a distributed impurity source with effective mixing time $\tau_{\mathrm{mix}}^{\mathrm{SPI}}$ dominated by fast magnetohydrodynamic (MHD) redistribution once cooling starts.
- Timescale comparison: the thermal quench time $\tau_{\mathrm{TQ}}$ is the characteristic time over which $T$ collapses; define an asymmetry parameter $\alpha \equiv \frac{\text{source-distribution timescale}}{\tau_{\mathrm{TQ}}}$, where $\alpha \gg 1$ implies strongly nonuniform radiation and $\alpha \ll 1$ implies near-uniform radiation.
- Runaway electron suppression: the parallel inductive electric field $E_\parallel$ during current quench accelerates electrons; collisional drag provides a threshold characterized by the Connor–Hastie critical field $E_c$ obtained by equating electric acceleration $e E_\parallel$ and collisional slowing-down force, yielding the scaling $E_c \propto n_e$ (with logarithmic corrections via the Coulomb logarithm). Avalanche multiplication requires $E_\parallel / E_c > 1$ sustained over the current quench; rapid density rise increases $E_c$ and reduces $E_\parallel / E_c$.
- Assimilation fraction: the fraction of injected impurity atoms that enter and ionize within the plasma, denoted $A_{\mathrm{MGI}}$ for MGI and $A_{\mathrm{SPI}}$ for SPI; deeper penetration and prompt ablation generally increase $A_{\mathrm{SPI}}$ relative to $A_{\mathrm{MGI}}$.

Assume physically plausible parameters: $R \sim 3\,\mathrm{m}$, $a \sim 1\,\mathrm{m}$, $\tau_{\mathrm{TQ}} \sim 1\,\mathrm{ms}$, $v_g \sim 500\,\mathrm{m/s}$, and $\tau_{\mathrm{mix}}^{\mathrm{SPI}} \lesssim 0.5\,\mathrm{ms}$ once cooling initiates. Under these assumptions and the model above, select all statements that are consistent with first-principles reasoning about the intended effects of Massive Gas Injection and Shattered Pellet Injection on thermal quench uniformity and runaway electron suppression.

A. Massive Gas Injection introduces impurities at a single toroidal location, so if $\tau_\phi^{\mathrm{MGI}} \gg \tau_{\mathrm{TQ}}$ the radiated power is toroidally nonuniform during the quench. Shattered Pellet Injection produces many ablation sites and fast redistribution, so if $\tau_{\mathrm{mix}}^{\mathrm{SPI}} \ll \tau_{\mathrm{TQ}}$ the radiated power is more uniform. Because $E_c \propto n_e$, the faster density rise typically achieved by SPI (via higher $A_{\mathrm{SPI}}$ and deeper penetration) reduces $E_\parallel / E_c$ more effectively than MGI, suppressing runaway avalanche under otherwise similar conditions.

B. Massive Gas Injection produces more uniform radiation than Shattered Pellet Injection because neutral gas equilibrates toroidally effectively instantaneously (i.e., $\tau_\phi^{\mathrm{MGI}} \ll \tau_{\mathrm{TQ}}$), whereas fragmented pellets ablate slowly, so SPI yields a delayed density rise and worsens runaway suppression.

C. Uniform thermal quench radiation requires low-$Z$ impurities; high-$Z$ impurities necessarily produce nonuniform radiation during the quench regardless of source distribution, so neither Massive Gas Injection nor Shattered Pellet Injection can achieve uniformity if $Z$ is large.

D. Runaway suppression is primarily accomplished by reducing the inductive field $E_\parallel$ through lowering resistivity; since both Massive Gas Injection and Shattered Pellet Injection cool the plasma, they lower resistivity and thereby reduce $E_\parallel$, ensuring runaway suppression in both cases.

Choose all that apply. There may be more than one correct option.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "We analyze uniformity of thermal quench radiation and runaway electron suppression using the provided model and first principles.\n\nThermal quench uniformity: The radiative power is $P_{\\mathrm{rad}}(\\mathbf{r},t) = n_e n_Z L_Z(T)$. For $P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$ to be approximately uniform on magnetic surfaces during the quench, the impurity source $n_Z(\\mathbf{r},t)$ must be distributed toroidally and poloidally on a timescale shorter than the thermal quench time. The asymmetry parameter is defined as $\\alpha \\equiv \\frac{\\text{source-distribution timescale}}{\\tau_{\\mathrm{TQ}}}$, with $\\alpha \\ll 1$ yielding near uniformity and $\\alpha \\gg 1$ yielding strong nonuniformity.\n\nFor Massive Gas Injection, the neutral front enters at a single toroidal location. The toroidal spreading time is $\\tau_\\phi^{\\mathrm{MGI}} \\sim \\frac{2\\pi R}{v_g}$. With $R \\sim 3\\,\\mathrm{m}$ and $v_g \\sim 500\\,\\mathrm{m/s}$, we find $\\tau_\\phi^{\\mathrm{MGI}} \\sim \\frac{2\\pi \\times 3\\,\\mathrm{m}}{500\\,\\mathrm{m/s}} \\approx \\frac{18.85\\,\\mathrm{m}}{500\\,\\mathrm{m/s}} \\approx 0.0377\\,\\mathrm{s} \\approx 37.7\\,\\mathrm{ms}$. Given $\\tau_{\\mathrm{TQ}} \\sim 1\\,\\mathrm{ms}$, the asymmetry parameter is $\\alpha_{\\mathrm{MGI}} \\approx \\frac{37.7\\,\\mathrm{ms}}{1\\,\\mathrm{ms}} \\approx 37.7 \\gg 1$, which implies that $P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$ is strongly toroidally nonuniform during the thermal quench (radiation peaks near the injection port).\n\nFor Shattered Pellet Injection, many fragments penetrate and ablate inside the plasma along field lines. Once cooling initiates, fast magnetohydrodynamic (MHD) mixing can redistribute impurities on a timescale $\\tau_{\\mathrm{mix}}^{\\mathrm{SPI}} \\lesssim 0.5\\,\\mathrm{ms}$. With $\\tau_{\\mathrm{TQ}} \\sim 1\\,\\mathrm{ms}$, we have $\\alpha_{\\mathrm{SPI}} \\approx \\frac{\\tau_{\\mathrm{mix}}^{\\mathrm{SPI}}}{\\tau_{\\mathrm{TQ}}} \\lesssim \\frac{0.5\\,\\mathrm{ms}}{1\\,\\mathrm{ms}} \\approx 0.5 \\ll 1$; this supports more uniform $P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$ during the quench.\n\nRunaway suppression: Electrons are accelerated by the parallel inductive electric field $E_\\parallel$; collisional drag produces a threshold known as the Connor–Hastie critical field $E_c$, obtained by equating the electric acceleration $e E_\\parallel$ with the collisional slowing-down force. In a Coulomb collision framework, the friction force scales with $n_e$ (with logarithmic corrections via the Coulomb logarithm), yielding $E_c \\propto n_e$. Avalanche multiplication requires $E_\\parallel / E_c > 1$. Therefore, rapidly increasing $n_e$ reduces $E_\\parallel / E_c$ and suppresses avalanche growth. The assimilation fraction $A$ controls how many impurity atoms ionize in the plasma and contribute electrons through ionization and entrainment. Due to deeper penetration and prompt ablation, Shattered Pellet Injection generally achieves $A_{\\mathrm{SPI}} > A_{\\mathrm{MGI}}$, translating to a faster and larger $n_e$ rise and a larger $E_c$, thereby more effectively reducing $E_\\parallel / E_c$ during the current quench. Massive Gas Injection, being edge-localized and subject to slower toroidal spreading, tends to yield a slower density rise and stronger toroidal asymmetry; this can leave $E_\\parallel / E_c$ comparatively larger, making runaway suppression less robust under otherwise similar conditions.\n\nWe now evaluate each option.\n\nOption A: It states that Massive Gas Injection introduces impurities at a single toroidal location and, with $\\tau_\\phi^{\\mathrm{MGI}} \\gg \\tau_{\\mathrm{TQ}}$, produces toroidally nonuniform radiation during the quench. Our calculation gave $\\tau_\\phi^{\\mathrm{MGI}} \\approx 37.7\\,\\mathrm{ms}$ and $\\tau_{\\mathrm{TQ}} \\sim 1\\,\\mathrm{ms}$, so $\\alpha_{\\mathrm{MGI}} \\gg 1$ and nonuniformity is expected. It further states that Shattered Pellet Injection produces many ablation sites and fast redistribution, with $\\tau_{\\mathrm{mix}}^{\\mathrm{SPI}} \\ll \\tau_{\\mathrm{TQ}}$, yielding more uniform radiation; our estimate gave $\\alpha_{\\mathrm{SPI}} \\lesssim 0.5$, consistent with improved uniformity. Finally, it invokes $E_c \\propto n_e$ and argues that the faster density rise from SPI (via larger $A_{\\mathrm{SPI}}$ and deeper penetration) reduces $E_\\parallel / E_c$ more effectively than MGI, suppressing runaway avalanche. This aligns with the first-principles scaling of the Connor–Hastie critical field and the role of assimilation fraction. Verdict: Correct.\n\nOption B: It claims that Massive Gas Injection produces more uniform radiation than Shattered Pellet Injection because neutral gas equilibrates toroidally effectively instantaneously (i.e., $\\tau_\\phi^{\\mathrm{MGI}} \\ll \\tau_{\\mathrm{TQ}}$). Our timescale estimate gives $\\tau_\\phi^{\\mathrm{MGI}} \\approx 37.7\\,\\mathrm{ms}$, much larger than $\\tau_{\\mathrm{TQ}} \\sim 1\\,\\mathrm{ms}$, so the premise is incorrect; gas does not equilibrate toroidally instantaneously in the disruption context. It also asserts that SPI yields a delayed density rise and worsens runaway suppression, which contradicts the deeper penetration and prompt ablation characteristic of SPI and the $E_c \\propto n_e$ scaling. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nOption C: It claims that uniform thermal quench radiation requires low-$Z$ impurities and that high-$Z$ impurities necessarily produce nonuniform radiation regardless of source distribution. The uniformity criterion in this model depends on the source distribution timescale relative to $\\tau_{\\mathrm{TQ}}$, quantified by $\\alpha$, rather than strictly on $Z$. While $L_Z(T)$ depends on $Z$ and can influence the magnitude of $P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$, uniformity is fundamentally governed by how uniformly $n_Z$ is deposited and redistributed across magnetic surfaces during the quench. High-$Z$ impurities can produce uniform radiation if distributed uniformly (i.e., if $\\alpha \\ll 1$). Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nOption D: It asserts that runaway suppression is primarily accomplished by reducing $E_\\parallel$ through lowering resistivity and that cooling lowers resistivity. In tokamak plasmas, the Spitzer resistivity $\\eta$ scales as $\\eta \\propto T^{-3/2}$, so cooling increases resistivity. Increased resistivity at fixed inductive drive tends to increase $E_\\parallel$ during current quench. Runaway suppression is achieved by increasing $E_c$ via raising $n_e$ (and pitch-angle scattering), not by cooling alone; cooling without sufficient density rise can actually worsen inductive fields. Therefore, the statement is false in both its premise (cooling lowers resistivity) and its conclusion. Verdict: Incorrect.\n\nTherefore, only Option A is consistent with the model and first-principles reasoning provided."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{A}$$", "solution": "The problem statement provides a simplified but physically self-consistent model for evaluating two tokamak disruption mitigation techniques: Massive Gas Injection (MGI) and Shattered Pellet Injection (SPI). The model is to be assessed against two goals: uniform thermal quench radiation and runaway electron suppression. Before evaluating the options, the problem statement itself must be validated.\n\n### Step 1: Extract Givens\n-   **Plasma Geometry**: Toroidal plasma with major radius $R$ and minor radius $a$.\n-   **Thermal Energy**: $W_{\\mathrm{th}} = \\frac{3}{2} n_e V k_B T$, with electron density $n_e$, volume $V$, Boltzmann's constant $k_B$, and electron temperature $T$.\n-   **Radiated Power**: $P_{\\mathrm{rad}}(\\mathbf{r},t) = n_e(\\mathbf{r},t) n_Z(\\mathbf{r},t) L_Z\\!\\left(T(\\mathbf{r},t)\\right)$, where $n_Z$ is the impurity density and $L_Z(T)$ is the radiative cooling function.\n-   **Uniformity Condition**: $P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$ is approximately uniform on magnetic surfaces.\n-   **MGI Source**: Neutral gas from a single toroidal location, spreads with speed $v_g$. title: [multiple_choice] Define key runaway diagnostics including hard X-ray, gamma-ray, and synchrotron emission, and explain which aspects of avalanche dynamics each technique probes.

Question: A large-radius tokamak undergoes a deuterium thermal quench that leaves a strong post-disruption toroidal electric field, driving a rapid increase of a relativistic runaway electron population via collisional avalanche. Three line-of-sight instruments are available: a scintillator detecting hard X-ray (HXR) photons in the range $0.05\text{–}1\ \mathrm{MeV}$, a high-dynamic-range detector for gamma-ray photons with energies $>\!1\ \mathrm{MeV}$, and a tangentially viewing, band-limited camera in the near-infrared (IR) range $0.8\text{–}1.6\ \mu \mathrm{m}$ imaging toroidally extended light from the plasma midplane. During the current quench, the HXR count rate increases approximately exponentially with an e-folding time of $2\ \mathrm{ms}$; the gamma-ray count rate shows a similar time dependence with a harder photon spectrum; the IR camera records a bright, outboard-localized crescent whose spectrum peaks near $1\ \mu \mathrm{m}$. 

Select all statements that correctly define these diagnostics and identify which aspects of avalanche runaway electron dynamics each technique principally constrains.

A. The HXR diagnostic (hard X-ray) primarily detects bremsstrahlung produced when relativistic runaways scatter off background ions and electrons; its line-integrated intensity growth reflects the multiplication of runaway electron density, so the exponential rise rate of the HXR signal can be used to estimate the avalanche growth rate, and its photon spectrum constrains the high-energy slope of the electron momentum distribution.

B. The gamma-ray detector at energies $>\!10\ \mathrm{MeV}$ predominantly measures synchrotron photons from the most energetic runaways in a tokamak-scale magnetic field, so it directly constrains the pitch-angle distribution of the runaway population.

C. The near-IR camera images synchrotron radiation from relativistic electrons gyrating in the magnetic field; the strong dependence of synchrotron power and spectral peak on perpendicular momentum and energy makes this diagnostic sensitive to the pitch-angle distribution and maximum energy, and its spatially resolved brightness pattern can constrain the radial profile of the runaway beam.

D. Because both the seed and avalanche-generated runaways emit photons, HXR and gamma-ray diagnostics cannot provide any information on avalanche dynamics and are therefore useless for estimating avalanche multiplication rates.

E. A collimated gamma-ray camera provides chord-integrated bremsstrahlung emissivity; with multiple lines of sight, it can localize the emission region and thus constrain the radial distribution of runaways even without synchrotron imaging, thereby informing how the avalanche is distributed across flux surfaces.

F. The synchrotron brightness measured by the near-IR camera is proportional only to the runaway electron density and is essentially independent of the electron energy and pitch angle, so it directly measures density but not anisotropy or maximum energy.

Choose all that apply.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{ACE}$$", "solution": "We begin from fundamental radiation mechanisms and their dependence on particle dynamics.\n\nBremsstrahlung from relativistic electrons arises when a charged particle is accelerated in the Coulomb field of a nucleus or electron. The differential emissivity is proportional to the target density and depends on the electron energy through the bremsstrahlung cross section. For a runaway electron population with distribution $f(\\mathbf{p},t)$ and background ion density $n_{Z}$, the line-integrated HXR or gamma-ray intensity along a chord is schematically\n\n$$\nI_{\\gamma,X}(t)\\ \\propto\\ \\int_{\\mathrm{LOS}} \\mathrm{d}\\ell\\ \\int \\mathrm{d}^{3}p\\ f(\\mathbf{p},t)\\ n_{Z}\\ \\sigma_{\\mathrm{br}}(\\mathbf{p}\\to \\hbar\\omega)\\ v ,\n$$\n\nwhere $\\sigma_{\\mathrm{br}}$ is the bremsstrahlung cross section and $v$ is electron speed. If the runaway density $n_{\\mathrm{RE}}(t)$ grows approximately exponentially due to avalanche, $n_{\\mathrm{RE}}(t)\\sim n_{0}\\,\\exp(\\Gamma t)$, and the background density $n_{Z}$ and spectral shape evolve slowly, then the chord-integrated bremsstrahlung intensity inherits the same exponential factor, $I_{\\gamma,X}(t)\\propto \\exp(\\Gamma t)$. Thus, the measured e-folding time provides an estimate of the avalanche growth rate $\\Gamma$. Moreover, the photon spectrum at HXR/gamma energies reflects the shape of the high-energy tail of $f(\\mathbf{p})$, allowing inference of the effective slope of the runaway momentum distribution.\n\nSynchrotron radiation from a relativistic electron results from acceleration perpendicular to its velocity due to the Lorentz force in a magnetic field $\\mathbf{B}$. The radiated power for a relativistic electron with Lorentz factor $\\gamma$ and pitch angle $\\alpha$ scales approximately as\n\n$$\nP_{\\mathrm{syn}} \\propto \\gamma^{2}\\, \\beta_{\\perp}^{2}\\, B^{2},\\quad \\beta_{\\perp} \\equiv \\frac{v_{\\perp}}{c}=\\beta \\sin \\alpha,\n$$\n\nand the characteristic (critical) photon frequency is\n\n$$\n\\omega_{c}\\ \\approx\\ \\frac{3}{2}\\ \\gamma^{3}\\ \\frac{e B}{m_{e}}\\ \\sin\\alpha.\n$$\n\nFor tokamak fields $B\\sim \\mathcal{O}(1\\text{–}5)\\ \\mathrm{T}$ and relativistic factors $\\gamma \\sim \\mathcal{O}(10\\text{–}100)$ with small pitch $\\alpha$, one finds $\\hbar\\omega_{c}$ in the visible/infrared range. As an example, take $B=5\\ \\mathrm{T}$, $\\gamma=50$, and $\\sin\\alpha = 0.05$. Using $eB/m_{e} \\approx 1.76\\times 10^{11}\\ B[\\mathrm{T}]\\ \\mathrm{s}^{-1}$, we have\n\n$$\n\\omega_{c} \\approx \\frac{3}{2}\\times 50^{3}\\times (1.76\\times 10^{11}\\times 5)\\times 0.05\\ \\mathrm{s}^{-1}\\ \\approx\\ 5.5\\times 10^{15}\\ \\mathrm{s}^{-1},\n$$\n\nso $\\hbar\\omega_{c}\\approx 3.6\\ \\mathrm{eV}$, squarely in the visible, not in the mega-electron-volt range. The strong dependence of $P_{\\mathrm{syn}}$ on $\\beta_{\\perp}$ and $\\gamma$ makes synchrotron imaging highly sensitive to pitch-angle distribution and high-energy content; because emission is forward-beamed, the spatial pattern encodes the guiding-center orbit geometry, enabling inference of the runaway beam’s radial profile.\n\nWe now analyze each statement.\n\nA. This statement identifies HXR as bremsstrahlung from relativistic runaways scattering off background charges. The line-integrated intensity is proportional to the product of runaway density and target density, with a spectral response determined by $\\sigma_{\\mathrm{br}}(\\mathbf{p}\\to \\hbar\\omega)$ and $f(\\mathbf{p})$. Under approximately stationary background conditions, an avalanche $n_{\\mathrm{RE}}(t)\\propto \\exp(\\Gamma t)$ yields $I_{X}(t)\\propto \\exp(\\Gamma t)$, so the exponential rise rate indicates $\\Gamma$. The photon spectrum constrains the high-energy slope of the electron momentum distribution. This is correct, acknowledging that absolute $\\Gamma$ extraction requires calibration and accounting for losses.\n\nVerdict: Correct.\n\nB. This claims that $>\\!10\\ \\mathrm{MeV}$ gamma-ray detectors primarily measure synchrotron photons and therefore directly diagnose pitch angle. As derived above, synchrotron critical photon energies in tokamak-strength fields are in the optical/IR for $\\gamma\\sim 10\\text{–}100$; MeV photons from synchrotron would require unrealistically large $\\gamma^{3} B \\sin\\alpha$ for fusion devices. Gamma-ray signals in this context predominantly arise from high-energy bremsstrahlung, not synchrotron. Thus the premise and conclusion are incorrect.\n\nVerdict: Incorrect.\n\nC. This asserts that the near-IR camera images synchrotron radiation with strong sensitivity to perpendicular momentum and energy, hence probing pitch-angle distribution and maximum energy; further, that the spatial pattern constrains the radial profile. This aligns with $P_{\\mathrm{syn}}\\propto \\gamma^{2}\\beta_{\\perp}^{2}B^{2}$, $\\omega_{c}\\propto \\gamma^{3}B\\sin\\alpha$, and the forward-beaming and orbit geometry that produce crescent-shaped images localized on the outboard side in a tokamak. Therefore, this technique is indeed a probe of anisotropy, high-energy content, and beam radial distribution.\n\nVerdict: Correct.\n\nD. This claims HXR and gamma-ray diagnostics are useless for avalanche dynamics. As discussed, the exponential rise of bremsstrahlung intensity tracks the avalanche multiplication under steady conditions, and spectral evolution provides information about the evolving distribution. While these diagnostics cannot uniquely separate seed and avalanche components without modeling, they are far from useless and do provide quantitative constraints on growth rates and distribution shape.\n\nVerdict: Incorrect.\n\nE. This states that a collimated gamma-ray camera provides chord-integrated emissivity that can be localized with multiple lines of sight to constrain the runaway radial distribution. This is the principle of gamma tomography: with adequate angular coverage and inversion, one can reconstruct emissivity profiles and infer the runaway beam’s spatial extent, which directly informs how avalanche multiplication populates flux surfaces.\n\nVerdict: Correct.\n\nF. This claims synchrotron brightness depends only on density and not on energy or pitch angle. This contradicts the strong dependencies $P_{\\mathrm{syn}}\\propto \\gamma^{2}\\beta_{\\perp}^{2}$ and $\\omega_{c}\\propto \\gamma^{3}\\sin\\alpha$. Synchrotron measurements are weighted toward the highest $\\gamma$ and larger $\\beta_{\\perp}$, making them sensitive to anisotropy and maximum energy rather than being simple density meters.\n\nVerdict: Incorrect.\n\nTherefore, the correct statements are A, C, and E."}, {"answer": "$$\\boxed{ACE}$$", "solution": "The user has provided a problem statement regarding the interpretation of diagnostic signals during a runaway electron avalanche in a tokamak. My task is to first validate the problem statement and then, if valid, provide a detailed solution evaluating each of the given options.\n\n### Problem Validation\n\n**Step 1: Extract Givens**\n\n*   **System:** A large-radius tokamak.\n*   **Plasma Species:** Deuterium ($D$).\n*   **Event:** A thermal quench followed by a strong post-disruption toroidal electric field.\n*   **Phenomenon:** A rapid increase of a relativistic runaway electron (RE) population via collisional avalanche.\n*   **Diagnostic 1:** Scintillator detecting hard X-ray (HXR) photons in the energy range $0.05 \\text{–} 1 \\ \\mathrm{MeV}$.\n*   **Diagnostic 2:** High-dynamic-range detector for gamma-ray photons with energies $E > 1 \\ \\mathrm{MeV}$.\n*   **Diagnostic 3:** Tangentially viewing, band-limited camera in the near-infrared (IR) range $0.8 \\text{–} 1.6 \\ \\mu \\mathrm{m}$, imaging toroidally extended light from the plasma midplane.\n*   **Observation 1:** The HXR count rate increases approximately exponentially with an e-folding time of $2 \\ \\mathrm{ms}$.\n*   **Observation 2:** The gamma-ray count rate shows a similar time dependence (to the HXR rate) with a harder photon spectrum.\n*   **Observation 3:** The IR camera records a bright, outboard-localized crescent whose spectrum peaks near $1 \\ \\mu \\mathrm{m}$.\n*   **Question:** Select all statements that correctly define these diagnostics and identify which aspects of avalanche runaway electron dynamics each technique principally constrains.\n\n**Step 2: Validate Using Extracted Givens**\n\nThe problem statement is subjected to validation against the specified criteria.\n\n*   **Scientifically Grounded:** The scenario is a well-established and critical issue in the operation of large tokamaks, particularly for future devices like ITER. A disruption-induced thermal quench rapidly cools the plasma, increasing its resistivity and allowing a large toroidal electric field to develop, which in turn can generate a beam of relativistic runaway electrons through primary (Dreicer) and secondary (avalanche) mechanisms. The diagnostics described—HXR detectors, gamma-ray detectors, and near-IR cameras for synchrotron radiation—are standard and essential tools for studying these phenomena. The observations, including the exponential growth of high-energy radiation signals and the characteristic crescent shape of synchrotron emission, are consistent with decades of experimental results and theoretical modeling. The problem is firmly based on established principles of plasma physics, electromagnetism, and special relativity.\n*   **Well-Posed:** The question is an interpretative problem asking to evaluate the correctness of statements about physical diagnostics. Each statement can be assessed against fundamental principles of radiation physics and runaway electron dynamics. A unique set of correct/incorrect verdicts for the options can be determined.\n*   **Objective:** The problem is described using precise, technical language common in the field of nuclear fusion and plasma physics. It is free of ambiguity, subjectivity, and non-scientific claims.\n*   **Completeness and Consistency:** The problem provides all necessary information to evaluate the options. The descriptions of the machine, the event, the diagnostics, and the observations are self-contained and mutually consistent. For instance, the exponential rise in HXR/gamma signals is the expected signature of an avalanche, and the IR crescent is the expected signature of synchrotron radiation from a relativistic electron beam in a tokamak.\n\n**Step 3: Verdict and Action**\n\nThe problem statement is **valid**. It is scientifically sound, well-posed, objective, and consistent. I will now proceed with the solution by analyzing the underlying physics and evaluating each option.\n\n### Solution Derivation\n\nThe analysis rests on the two primary mechanisms by which relativistic electrons radiate in a plasma: bremsstrahlung and synchrotron radiation.\n\n1.  **Bremsstrahlung (\"braking radiation\"):** This occurs when a relativistic electron is deflected by the Coulomb field of background plasma particles (primarily ions, but also electrons). The emitted photon can have an energy up to the kinetic energy of the electron. The total power radiated is proportional to the product of the runaway electron density ($n_{RE}$) and the background ion density ($n_Z$) and scales with the square of the ion charge ($Z^2$). For a given population of runaways, the total bremsstrahlung emission rate is directly proportional to $n_{RE}$. Therefore, the time evolution of the spatially-integrated bremsstrahlung signal (HXR and gamma-rays) tracks the time evolution of the total number of runaway electrons. The shape of the photon energy spectrum provides information about the runaway electron energy distribution function, with higher-energy electrons producing higher-energy photons.\n\n2.  **Synchrotron Radiation:** This occurs when a relativistic electron with energy $E = \\gamma m_e c^2$ and velocity perpendicular to the magnetic field ($v_\\perp$) is accelerated by the Lorentz force. The radiation is emitted in a narrow cone tangent to the electron's trajectory. The total radiated power per electron is strongly dependent on energy and pitch angle (which determines the perpendicular momentum $p_\\perp$), scaling as $P_{syn} \\propto \\gamma^2 B^2 \\sin^2\\alpha$, where $\\alpha$ is the pitch angle. The spectrum of this radiation is broad, peaking at a critical frequency $\\omega_c \\propto \\gamma^3 B_\\perp$, where $B_\\perp$ is the magnetic field component perpendicular to the electron velocity. For typical multi-MeV runaways in a tokamak magnetic field of several Tesla, this radiation falls in the near-infrared and visible parts of the spectrum. The observation of a bright \"crescent\" on the outboard side (low-field side) is a classic signature, resulting from the combination of the tangential camera view, the toroidal curvature of the electron orbits, and the strong dependence of synchrotron emission on energy.\n\nBased on these principles, I will now evaluate each option.\n\n### Option-by-Option Analysis\n\n**A. The HXR diagnostic (hard X-ray) primarily detects bremsstrahlung produced when relativistic runaways scatter off background ions and electrons; its line-integrated intensity growth reflects the multiplication of runaway electron density, so the exponential rise rate of the HXR signal can be used to estimate the avalanche growth rate, and its photon spectrum constrains the high-energy slope of the electron momentum distribution.**\nThis statement correctly identifies bremsstrahlung as the source of HXR radiation. It correctly links the intensity of this radiation to the runaway electron density ($n_{RE}$). Therefore, an exponential increase in the HXR signal with an e-folding time of $2 \\ \\mathrm{ms}$ is a direct measurement of the exponential growth of the runaway population, which defines the avalanche growth rate. The statement also correctly notes that the photon energy spectrum is determined by the electron energy distribution, thus constraining it. This statement is entirely accurate.\n**Verdict: Correct.**\n\n**B. The gamma-ray detector at energies $>\\!10\\ \\mathrm{MeV}$ predominantly measures synchrotron photons from the most energetic runaways in a tokamak-scale magnetic field, so it directly constrains the pitch-angle distribution of the runaway population.**\nThis statement's central premise is flawed. While synchrotron radiation is emitted, its characteristic photon energies are typically in the eV range (visible/IR), not the MeV range. Generating synchrotron photons with energy $E_{ph} > 10 \\ \\mathrm{MeV}$ would require electron energies of many hundreds of MeV, which are not typical for tokamak runaway populations, and even then, the flux would be negligible. The overwhelming source of photons with energy $E > 1 \\ \\mathrm{MeV}$, and certainly $E > 10 \\ \\mathrm{MeV}$, is bremsstrahlung from collisions of high-energy runaways with the background plasma. Therefore, the statement incorrectly identifies the radiation mechanism.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\n**C. The near-IR camera images synchrotron radiation from relativistic electrons gyrating in the magnetic field; the strong dependence of synchrotron power and spectral peak on perpendicular momentum and energy makes this diagnostic sensitive to the pitch-angle distribution and maximum energy, and its spatially resolved brightness pattern can constrain the radial profile of the runaway beam.**\nThis statement correctly identifies the radiation source in the near-IR as synchrotron radiation. It correctly states the strong dependence of synchrotron emission on both energy ($\\gamma$) and pitch angle (via $p_\\perp$). This sensitivity allows these key parameters of the runaway distribution function to be diagnosed. Finally, as it is an imaging diagnostic, it provides spatial information. The observed brightness distribution can be used to infer the spatial location and shape of the runaway beam within the plasma cross-section. The statement is a complete and accurate description of synchrotron diagnostics for runaways.\n**Verdict: Correct.**\n\n**D. Because both the seed and avalanche-generated runaways emit photons, HXR and gamma-ray diagnostics cannot provide any information on avalanche dynamics and are therefore useless for estimating avalanche multiplication rates.**\nThis statement contains a logical fallacy. While it is true that all runaways (seed or avalanche-generated) contribute to the emission, the avalanche is a *dynamic* process characterized by the *rate of change* of the runaway population. The total HXR/gamma-ray signal is proportional to the total number of runaways, $N_{RE}(t)$. In an avalanche-dominated regime, $N_{RE}(t) \\approx N_{seed}e^{t/\\tau_{ava}}$, where $\\tau_{ava}$ is the avalanche e-folding time. The HXR signal will therefore also increase exponentially, $S_{HXR}(t) \\propto e^{t/\\tau_{ava}}$. Measuring this exponential growth rate is precisely how one estimates the avalanche multiplication rate. The argument that the diagnostic is \"useless\" because it doesn't distinguish between individual seed and secondary electrons is incorrect; it measures the net effect of the multiplication process.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**\n\n**E. A collimated gamma-ray camera provides chord-integrated bremsstrahlung emissivity; with multiple lines of sight, it can localize the emission region and thus constrain the radial distribution of runaways even without synchrotron imaging, thereby informing how the avalanche is distributed across flux surfaces.**\nThis statement accurately describes the principle of a gamma-ray camera. Each detector in such a system is collimated to view a narrow chord through the plasma. The signal is an integral of the local bremsstrahlung emissivity along that chord. By using a set of detectors with multiple intersecting chords, one can perform a tomographic inversion to reconstruct the two-dimensional emissivity profile, $\\epsilon(R, Z)$. Since bremsstrahlung emissivity is proportional to $n_{RE}(R, Z)$, this reconstruction directly yields the spatial distribution of the runaway electron population. This is a valid technique for localizing the runaway beam, complementary to synchrotron imaging.\n**Verdict: Correct.**\n\n**F. The synchrotron brightness measured by the near-IR camera is proportional only to the runaway electron density and is essentially independent of the electron energy and pitch angle, so it directly measures density but not anisotropy or maximum energy.**\nThis statement is the direct opposite of the physical reality. As detailed in the analysis for option C, synchrotron emissivity is *extremely* sensitive to the electron energy ($\\gamma$) and pitch angle ($\\alpha$). The power scales as $P_{syn} \\propto n_{RE} \\gamma^2 \\sin^2\\alpha$. Claiming that the brightness is independent of energy and pitch angle is fundamentally incorrect. Due to this complex dependency, deconvolving the separate contributions of density, energy distribution, and pitch-angle distribution from synchrotron measurements is a significant challenge and a major focus of diagnostic interpretation. The brightness is absolutely not a direct measure of only the density.\n**Verdict: Incorrect.**"}]


title: [calculation] Construct a figure of merit for mitigation success incorporating $P_{\mathrm{rad}}/P_{\mathrm{TQ}}$, symmetry factor $S$, runaway electron suppression probability, and actuator reliability, and justify each term from first principles.

Question: A large tokamak undergoing a thermal quench is mitigated by Shattered Pellet Injection (SPI). You will construct a dimensionless, probability-like figure of merit for overall mitigation success that incorporates the radiated power fraction, toroidal symmetry of the radiation, the probability of runaway electron suppression, and the reliability of the actuator chain. Your construction must be justified from first principles using only fundamental conservation laws, elementary transport and loading considerations, and basic probability rules. Then evaluate the figure of merit numerically for a specified scenario.

Consider the following well-characterized disruption event:
- The pre-quench thermal energy is $W_{\mathrm{th}} = 8.0 \times 10^{7} \ \mathrm{J}$, and the thermal quench duration is $\tau_{\mathrm{TQ}} = 4.0 \times 10^{-3} \ \mathrm{s}$, so that the average thermal quench power is $P_{\mathrm{TQ}} = W_{\mathrm{th}}/\tau_{\mathrm{TQ}}$.
- Bolometric inversion yields a time-averaged radiated power during the thermal quench of $P_{\mathrm{rad}} = 1.4 \times 10^{10} \ \mathrm{W}$, so that the radiated fraction is $f_{\mathrm{rad}} = P_{\mathrm{rad}}/P_{\mathrm{TQ}}$.
- The toroidal distribution of radiated power density on the first wall can be modeled as $p(\phi) = \bar{p}\,[1 + a \cos(\phi)]$ with $\bar{p} = P_{\mathrm{rad}}/(2\pi A)$ for some effective wall area $A$ and with $a = 0.5$, and poloidal variation is negligible. Define a dimensionless symmetry factor $S$ as the ratio of the toroidally averaged to the toroidally peaked power density, $S = \langle p \rangle_{\phi}/\max_{\phi} p(\phi)$.
- Runaway electron suppression is governed by the condition that the post-injection electron density $n$ exceeds a critical value $n_{c}$, here $n_{c} = 1.05 \times 10^{20} \ \mathrm{m}^{-3}$. The achieved $n$ due to injection variability can be modeled as a normal random variable with mean $\mu = 1.20 \times 10^{20} \ \mathrm{m}^{-3}$ and standard deviation $\sigma = 0.10 \times 10^{20} \ \mathrm{m}^{-3}$. The runaway suppression probability is $p_{\mathrm{RE}} = \mathbb{P}(n \ge n_{c})$.
- The actuator chain for SPI has four independent stages: disruption detection with probability $r_{d} = 0.995$, command and timing execution with probability $r_{c} = 0.990$, valve opening with probability $r_{v} = 0.975$, and reliable pellet shattering with probability $r_{s} = 0.980$. The overall actuator reliability is $r_{\mathrm{act}}$.

Tasks:
1. Using energy conservation and load distribution arguments, justify from first principles why the contribution of $f_{\mathrm{rad}}$ to a success figure of merit should be linear in $f_{\mathrm{rad}}$ and dimensionless.
2. Using the given toroidal distribution, derive $S$ explicitly from its definition, and justify from first principles why the mitigation quality should scale linearly with $S$ as a measure of peak-load reduction.
3. Using basic probability theory, argue why the overall mitigation success figure of merit can be modeled as the product of independent success factors, and construct a dimensionless figure of merit $M$ that incorporates $f_{\mathrm{rad}}$, $S$, $p_{\mathrm{RE}}$, and $r_{\mathrm{act}}$.
4. Compute $f_{\mathrm{rad}}$, $S$, $p_{\mathrm{RE}}$, $r_{\mathrm{act}}$, and then evaluate $M$ for the scenario above. Express the final figure of merit as a pure number (no units), and round your answer to four significant figures.

Answer: [{"answer": "$$\\boxed{0.4099}$$", "solution": "We construct a mitigation success figure of merit by appealing to energy conservation, load peaking physics, and the rules of probability for independent events.\n\n1. Radiated fraction contribution. By energy conservation, the thermal quench power is $P_{\\mathrm{TQ}} = W_{\\mathrm{th}}/\\tau_{\\mathrm{TQ}}$. A fraction $f_{\\mathrm{rad}} = P_{\\mathrm{rad}}/P_{\\mathrm{TQ}}$ of that power is converted into volumetric radiation, which is then distributed broadly and mitigates localized conductive and convective heat fluxes that can damage plasma-facing components. In the absence of an additional scale, the reduction in hazardous power to localized surfaces must be proportional to the fraction of power diverted into radiation. Hence the contribution of radiation to a dimensionless success measure should be linear in $f_{\\mathrm{rad}}$, with $f_{\\mathrm{rad}} = 0$ yielding zero contribution and $f_{\\mathrm{rad}} = 1$ yielding full contribution.\n\n2. Symmetry factor and peak-load scaling. The toroidal distribution is $p(\\phi) = \\bar{p}\\,[1+a\\cos(\\phi)]$ with $a \\in [0,1)$ for physical realizability. The toroidal average is $\\langle p \\rangle_{\\phi} = \\frac{1}{2\\pi}\\int_{0}^{2\\pi} p(\\phi)\\, d\\phi = \\bar{p}$, because $\\langle \\cos(\\phi) \\rangle_{\\phi} = 0$. The maximum occurs at $\\phi = 0$, with $\\max_{\\phi} p(\\phi) = \\bar{p}(1+a)$. Therefore the symmetry factor is\n$$\nS \\equiv \\frac{\\langle p \\rangle_{\\phi}}{\\max_{\\phi} p(\\phi)} = \\frac{\\bar{p}}{\\bar{p}(1+a)} = \\frac{1}{1+a}.\n$$\nFrom first principles of load distribution, the peak surface temperature rise for a short pulse on a semi-infinite solid scales linearly with the peak heat flux amplitude for fixed duration. Since the peaking factor is $\\max p/\\langle p \\rangle_{\\phi} = 1/S$, the peak load reduction scales linearly with $S$. Thus, to reflect improved isotropy and reduced peaking, a dimensionless success measure should scale linearly with $S$, with $S=0$ corresponding to extreme peaking and $S=1$ to perfect uniformity.\n\n3. Probability composition and figure of merit structure. There are two intrinsically probabilistic components: runaway electron suppression and actuator reliability. Runaway avalanche is a stochastic outcome governed by whether post-injection density exceeds a critical threshold; the mitigation succeeds in avoiding runaways with probability $p_{\\mathrm{RE}} = \\mathbb{P}(n \\ge n_{c})$. The actuator chain contains independent stages with success probabilities $r_{d}$, $r_{c}$, $r_{v}$, and $r_{s}$; by the multiplication rule for independent events, the overall reliability is\n$$\nr_{\\mathrm{act}} = r_{d}\\, r_{c}\\, r_{v}\\, r_{s}.\n$$\nAssuming the radiative fraction $f_{\\mathrm{rad}}$, symmetry factor $S$, runaway suppression outcome, and actuator function are independent contributors to success, and requiring that the figure of merit be dimensionless, bounded between $0$ and $1$, and monotonic in each contributor, the natural construction is a product of the four independent success factors:\n$$\nM = f_{\\mathrm{rad}} \\, S \\, p_{\\mathrm{RE}} \\, r_{\\mathrm{act}}.\n$$\nThis $M$ is dimensionless, equals $0$ if any indispensable element fails completely, and approaches $1$ only when all elements are ideal.\n\n4. Numerical evaluation.\n- Thermal quench power:\n$$\nP_{\\mathrm{TQ}} = \\frac{W_{\\mathrm{th}}}{\\tau_{\\mathrm{TQ}}} = \\frac{8.0 \\times 10^{7} \\ \\mathrm{J}}{4.0 \\times 10^{-3} \\ \\mathrm{s}} = 2.0 \\times 10^{10} \\ \\mathrm{W}.\n$$\n- Radiated fraction:\n$$\nf_{\\mathrm{rad}} = \\frac{P_{\\mathrm{rad}}}{P_{\\mathrm{TQ}}} = \\frac{1.4 \\times 10^{10} \\ \\mathrm{W}}{2.0 \\times 10^{10} \\ \\mathrm{W}} = 0.7.\n$$\n- Symmetry factor for $a = 0.5$:\n$$\nS = \\frac{1}{1+a} = \\frac{1}{1 + 0.5} = \\frac{2}{3}.\n$$\n- Runaway suppression probability. With $n \\sim \\mathcal{N}(\\mu,\\sigma^{2})$, one has\n$$\np_{\\mathrm{RE}} = \\mathbb{P}(n \\ge n_{c}) = \\mathbb{P}\\!\\left(\\frac{n-\\mu}{\\sigma} \\ge \\frac{n_{c}-\\mu}{\\sigma}\\right) = 1 - \\Phi\\!\\left(\\frac{n_{c}-\\mu}{\\sigma}\\right) = \\Phi\\!\\left(\\frac{\\mu - n_{c}}{\\sigma}\\right),\n$$\nwhere $\\Phi$ is the standard normal cumulative distribution function. Here\n$$\n\\frac{\\mu - n_{c}}{\\sigma} = \\frac{1.20 \\times 10^{20} - 1.05 \\times 10^{20}}{0.10 \\times 10^{20}} = 1.5,\n$$\nso\n$$\np_{\\mathrm{RE}} = \\Phi(1.5) \\approx 0.9331927987.\n$$\n- Actuator reliability:\n$$\nr_{\\mathrm{act}} = r_{d}\\, r_{c}\\, r_{v}\\, r_{s} = 0.995 \\times 0.990 \\times 0.975 \\times 0.980 = 0.941215275.\n$$\n- Figure of merit:\n$$\nM = f_{\\mathrm{rad}} \\, S \\, p_{\\mathrm{RE}} \\, r_{\\mathrm{act}} = 0.7 \\times \\frac{2}{3} \\times 0.9331927987 \\times 0.941215275 \\approx 0.4098898145.\n$$\nRounded to four significant figures and expressed as a pure number, the result is $0.4099$."}, {"answer": "$$\n\\boxed{0.4099}\n$$", "solution": "The problem statement is deemed valid as it is scientifically grounded in the principles of fusion plasma physics and disruption mitigation, is well-posed with all necessary information provided, and is stated objectively without subjective claims or ambiguity.\n\nThe solution proceeds by addressing the four specified tasks in sequence.\n\nTask 1: Justification for the contribution of the radiated power fraction, $f_{\\mathrm{rad}}$.\n\nThe total thermal energy of the plasma prior to the quench is $W_{\\mathrm{th}}$. This energy is dissipated over the thermal quench duration $\\tau_{\\mathrm{TQ}}$. The average power flow out of the plasma during this period is therefore $P_{\\mathrm{TQ}} = W_{\\mathrm{th}}/\\tau_{\\mathrm{TQ}}$. From a fundamental energy conservation perspective, this power must be channeled either to the surrounding material surfaces via conduction and convection, $P_{\\mathrm{cond}}$, or be converted into electromagnetic radiation, $P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$, which is emitted isotropically (ideally) and spread over a large surface area. Thus, we have the power balance equation:\n$$P_{\\mathrm{TQ}} = P_{\\mathrm{cond}} + P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$$\nThe primary objective of disruption mitigation is to protect the plasma-facing components from excessive, localized heat loads that can cause melting, vaporization, and erosion. These damaging loads are carried by $P_{\\mathrm{cond}}$. The goal is therefore to minimize $P_{\\mathrm{cond}}$. We can express the conducted power in terms of the total power and the radiated power:\n$$P_{\\mathrm{cond}} = P_{\\mathrm{TQ}} - P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$$\nTo create a dimensionless measure of performance, we can divide by $P_{\\mathrm{TQ}}$:\n$$\\frac{P_{\\mathrm{cond}}}{P_{\\mathrm{TQ}}} = 1 - \\frac{P_{\\mathrm{rad}}}{P_{\\mathrm{TQ}}}$$\nThe problem defines the radiated power fraction as $f_{\\mathrm{rad}} = P_{\\mathrm{rad}}/P_{\\mathrm{TQ}}$. Substituting this definition gives:\n$$\\frac{P_{\\mathrm{cond}}}{P_{\\mathrm{TQ}}} = 1 - f_{\\mathrm{rad}}$$\nThis shows that the fraction of the total power that is conducted to the walls is $1 - f_{\\mathrm{rad}}$. Minimizing this conducted fraction is equivalent to maximizing $f_{\\mathrm{rad}}$. A figure of merit for success should be a monotonically increasing function of mitigation performance. The simplest such relationship is a direct proportionality. Therefore, it is justified from first principles to have the success metric scale linearly with $f_{\\mathrm{rad}}$. Since $f_{\\mathrm{rad}}$ is a ratio of two powers, it is intrinsically dimensionless. An ideal mitigation system would convert all thermal energy to radiation, making $P_{\\mathrm{cond}} = 0$ and $f_{\\mathrm{rad}} = 1$. A complete failure to radiate results in $f_{\\mathrm{rad}} = 0$.\n\nTask 2: Derivation and justification for the symmetry factor, $S$.\n\nThe toroidal distribution of radiated power density on the first wall is given as $p(\\phi) = \\bar{p}\\,[1 + a \\cos(\\phi)]$, where $\\phi$ is the toroidal angle.\nThe toroidally averaged power density, $\\langle p \\rangle_{\\phi}$, is calculated by integrating over the full toroidal angle $2\\pi$ and dividing by the period:\n$$\\langle p \\rangle_{\\phi} = \\frac{1}{2\\pi} \\int_{0}^{2\\pi} p(\\phi) \\, d\\phi = \\frac{1}{2\\pi} \\int_{0}^{2\\pi} \\bar{p}\\,[1 + a \\cos(\\phi)] \\, d\\phi$$\n$$\\langle p \\rangle_{\\phi} = \\frac{\\bar{p}}{2\\pi} \\left[ \\phi + a \\sin(\\phi) \\right]_{0}^{2\\pi} = \\frac{\\bar{p}}{2\\pi} [(2\\pi + a \\sin(2\\pi)) - (0 + a \\sin(0))] = \\bar{p}$$\nThe toroidally peaked power density, $\\max_{\\phi} p(\\phi)$, is the maximum value of the function $p(\\phi)$. As the parameter $a$ is given as $a=0.5 > 0$, the maximum occurs when $\\cos(\\phi)$ is at its maximum value of $1$, which is at $\\phi=0$.\n$$\\max_{\\phi} p(\\phi) = p(0) = \\bar{p}\\,[1 + a \\cos(0)] = \\bar{p}(1+a)$$\nThe dimensionless symmetry factor $S$ is defined as the ratio of the average to the peak power density:\n$$S = \\frac{\\langle p \\rangle_{\\phi}}{\\max_{\\phi} p(\\phi)} = \\frac{\\bar{p}}{\\bar{p}(1+a)} = \\frac{1}{1+a}$$\nThe justification for including $S$ linearly in a figure of merit stems from load distribution principles. Material damage is driven by the *peak* thermal load, not the average. For a fixed total radiated power $P_{\\mathrm{rad}}$, the average power density $\\langle p \\rangle_{\\phi}$ is fixed. The peak load is then $\\max_{\\phi} p(\\phi) = \\langle p \\rangle_{\\phi} / S$. To minimize the risk of damage, the peak load must be minimized. This is equivalent to maximizing the symmetry factor $S$. An ideal scenario corresponds to a perfectly uniform radiation distribution ($a=0$), which yields $S=1$. Any asymmetry ($a>0$) leads to $S<1$ and a higher peak load for the same total radiated power. A linear scaling of the figure of merit with $S$ represents a direct, unweighted preference for reducing peak loads, which is the most straightforward assumption based on fundamental loading considerations.\n\nTask 3: Construction of the figure of merit, $M$.\n\nThe overall success of a complex engineering task like disruption mitigation depends on the successful outcome of several independent stages or criteria. Basic probability theory states that the probability of a set of independent events all occurring is the product of their individual probabilities. We can extend this logic to form a composite figure of merit by treating each criterion as an independent 'factor of success'.\nThe factors are:\n1. Actuator chain reliability ($r_{\\mathrm{act}}$): The physical system must operate correctly. This is a prerequisite for any mitigation to occur. The actuator chain has four independent stages with success probabilities $r_d$, $r_c$, $r_v$, and $r_s$. The total reliability is the product: $r_{\\mathrm{act}} = r_d \\cdot r_c \\cdot r_v \\cdot r_s$.\n2. Runaway electron suppression probability ($p_{\\mathrm{RE}}$): A critical goal is to prevent the formation of a damaging runaway electron beam. This is a probabilistic event dependent on achieving a sufficient density.\n3. Radiated power fraction ($f_{\\mathrm{rad}}$): A measure of how effectively conducted heat loads are converted to radiated power. This is a continuous quality factor between $0$ and $1$.\n4. Radiation symmetry ($S$): A measure of how well the radiated power is spread to avoid localized 'hot spots'. This is also a continuous quality factor between $0$ and $1$.\n\nA multiplicative model combines these factors in a logical way. If any factor is zero (e.g., $r_{\\mathrm{act}} = 0$ if the system fails to fire), the entire mitigation is a failure and the overall merit $M$ becomes zero. If all factors are ideal (i.e., equal to $1$), the overall merit is $1$. This product structure correctly captures the notion that overall success requires simultaneous success across all domains.\nTherefore, the dimensionless figure of merit $M$ is constructed as the product of these four independent success factors:\n$$M = r_{\\mathrm{act}} \\cdot p_{\\mathrm{RE}} \\cdot S \\cdot f_{\\mathrm{rad}}$$\n\nTask 4: Numerical Evaluation.\n\nFirst, we compute the value of each factor.\nThe average thermal quench power is:\n$$P_{\\mathrm{TQ}} = \\frac{W_{\\mathrm{th}}}{\\tau_{\\mathrm{TQ}}} = \\frac{8.0 \\times 10^{7} \\ \\mathrm{J}}{4.0 \\times 10^{-3} \\ \\mathrm{s}} = 2.0 \\times 10^{10} \\ \\mathrm{W}$$\nThe radiated power fraction is:\n$$f_{\\mathrm{rad}} = \\frac{P_{\\mathrm{rad}}}{P_{\\mathrm{TQ}}} = \\frac{1.4 \\times 10^{10} \\ \\mathrm{W}}{2.0 \\times 10^{10} \\ \\mathrm{W}} = 0.7$$\nThe symmetry factor, with $a=0.5$, is:\n$$S = \\frac{1}{1+a} = \\frac{1}{1+0.5} = \\frac{1}{1.5} = \\frac{2}{3}$$\nThe runaway suppression probability $p_{\\mathrm{RE}} = \\mathbb{P}(n \\ge n_{c})$ is found by standardizing the normal random variable $n \\sim N(\\mu, \\sigma^2)$, where $\\mu = 1.20 \\times 10^{20} \\ \\mathrm{m}^{-3}$, $\\sigma = 0.10 \\times 10^{20} \\ \\mathrm{m}^{-3}$, and $n_{c} = 1.05 \\times 10^{20} \\ \\mathrm{m}^{-3}$. Let $Z = (n-\\mu)/\\sigma$ be the standard normal variable.\n$$z_{c} = \\frac{n_c - \\mu}{\\sigma} = \\frac{(1.05 - 1.20) \\times 10^{20}}{0.10 \\times 10^{20}} = \\frac{-0.15}{0.10} = -1.5$$\n$$p_{\\mathrm{RE}} = \\mathbb{P}(Z \\ge -1.5) = 1 - \\mathbb{P}(Z < -1.5) = \\mathbb{P}(Z \\le 1.5)$$\nThis is given by the cumulative distribution function (CDF) of the standard normal distribution, $\\Phi(1.5)$. Using a standard value, $\\Phi(1.5) \\approx 0.9331928$.\nThe overall actuator reliability is the product of the probabilities of its independent stages:\n$$r_{\\mathrm{act}} = r_d \\cdot r_c \\cdot r_v \\cdot r_s = (0.995)(0.990)(0.975)(0.980) = 0.941215275$$\nFinally, we compute the figure of merit $M$:\n$$M = r_{\\mathrm{act}} \\cdot p_{\\mathrm{RE}} \\cdot S \\cdot f_{\\mathrm{rad}}$$\n$$M = (0.941215275) \\times (0.9331928) \\times \\left(\\frac{2}{3}\\right) \\times (0.7)$$\n$$M \\approx (0.878298) \\times \\left(\\frac{2}{3}\\right) \\times (0.7) \\approx (0.585532) \\times (0.7) \\approx 0.4098724$$\nRounding the result to four significant figures gives $M \\approx 0.4099$."}]</wiki_content5.4>
